The Many Forms of the Three Proofs
Observation on diverse formulations of the three proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the three proofs for the finitude of dimensions have been presented in many variant forms, all stemming from modifications of the original arguments.

Core Idea
A single proof can be expressed in many ways, all pointing to the same truth.

The scholar al-Sanusi notes that the three proofs for the finitude of dimensions have been formulated in many different ways. This is not a criticism but an observation of the richness of Islamic theological reasoning. In this lesson, we explore what this statement means and why it matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the gist.
2
Understand the key phrase
Focus on 'كثرت الوجوه بتصرف في الثلاثة'. Literally 'the aspects have multiplied by modification in the three'. This means many variations exist.
The 'three' refers to the three core proofs for finitude.
3
Recognize the source of variations
The commentator explains that these variations come from modifying (tasarruf) the three original proofs. Note the word 'استعانة' (assistance) suggesting these variations are helpful.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why having multiple formulations strengthens the argument. It shows the proofs are robust and can be adapted for different audiences or contexts.
This is not a weakness but a sign of depth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وقد كثرت الوجوه بتصرف في الثلاثة. أي وجوه الاستدلال على تناهي الأبعاد بتصرف في البراهين الثلاثة، واستعانة
He said: 'And the aspects have multiplied by modification in the three.' That is, ways of inferring the finitude of dimensions by modifying the three proofs, and assistance.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This brief statement indicates that the three proofs for the finitude of dimensions have been presented in many variant forms by scholars, who have modified (tasarruf) the original arguments. The author explains that 'the aspects have multiplied' means that there are many ways (wujūh) of reasoning for the finitude of dimensions, all derived from modifications of the three core proofs. The word 'assistance' (istiʿāna) may suggest that these variations are helpful or supplemented by additional considerations.

Key Terms
وجوه
استعانة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This remark suggests the proofs are weak or inconsistent.
Correction: It is an observation of their diverse formulations, not a criticism. Diversity shows robustness.

Checkpoint
What does 'كثرت الوجوه بتصرف في الثلاثة' mean?
A) The three proofs are weak.
B) There are many versions of the three proofs.
C) The proofs are invalid.
D) The proofs are all the same.

Key Takeaway
The three proofs for finitude of dimensions have been expressed in numerous variants, each modification serving to clarify or strengthen the argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a truth like the finitude of dimensions, do you seek to understand it from multiple angles, or do you settle for one formulation? What does your approach say about your love for the truth?
?
The scholars multiplied the forms of these proofs to assist understanding. In what area of your religious knowledge have you benefited from seeing a single reality expressed in different ways? How did that deepen your conviction?
?
This passage shows that even well-established proofs can be expressed in many ways without contradiction. When you see different opinions or formulations among scholars, do you view them as signs of richness or confusion? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in the tradition?

Two Interpretations of Simple and Composite Bodies
Al-Taftazani explains two interpretations of simple and composite with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Taftazani distinguishes between existential and privational interpretations of simple and composite bodies, and how these apply to examples like animal, water, celestial sphere, and gold.

Core Idea
Simple and composite are not fixed categories; they depend on interpretation and consideration.

Al-Taftazani introduces a sophisticated classification of bodies that goes beyond simple labels. He shows that what is considered 'simple' or 'composite' depends on two lenses: existential vs. privational interpretation, and real vs. sensory consideration. This passage lays the groundwork for understanding the nuanced categorization of physical substances in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two interpretations
Identify the existential (wujudi) and privational ('adami) interpretations for simple and composite. Note how they relate to the two criteria: composition from different natures and equality of part to whole.
Remember: existential focuses on presence of composition, privational on absence.
2
Learn the two bases for classification
The two bases are: (a) composition from bodies of different natures, (b) equality of part and whole in name and definition. These can be considered from reality or from sense.
3
See how reality and sense affect the classification
Understand that applying reality (haqiqa) or sense (hiss) yields four possible interpretations for both simple and composite. Note the interplay between existential/privational and real/sensory.
The author states that the celestial sphere is simple by the first interpretation but composite by the second.
4
Apply the framework to the four examples
Examine animal, water, celestial sphere, and gold. See why each falls into its category based on the two interpretations and two considerations.
Gold is composite in reality but simple sensorily under the first interpretation, and the opposite under the second.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (والمأخوذ في كل) من تفسيري كل قد يعتبر حسياً، وقد يعتبر حقيقياً. فبالاعتبارية الحيوية مركب حقيقة والماء بسيط مطلقاً، والفلك بالتفسير الأول خاصة، والذهب بالتفسيرين مركب حقيقة، بسيط حسّاً. قد ذكر لكل من الجسم البسيط والجسم المركب تفسيرين أحدهما وجودي والآخر عدمي. فهو الآن يشير إلى أن ما جعل مأخذ التفسيرين، أعني التألف من الأجسام المختلفة الطبائع، وتساوي الجزء والكل في الاسم والحد، قد يعتبر من حيث الحقيقة وقد يعتبر من حيث الحس، فيحصل لكل من البسيط والمركب أربعة تفسيرات مختلفة، بالعموم والخصوص، متعاكسة في الوجودية والعدمية، فللبسيط ما لا يتألف من المختلفات، حقيقة ما لا يتألف منها حساً ما يساوي جزؤه الكل حقيقة ما يساويه حساً، وللمركب ما يتألف حقيقة ما يتألف حساً ما لا يساوي حقيقة ما لا يساوي حساً. فالمأخوذ من المأخذ الأول للمركب وجودي، والبسيط عدمي، المأخذ الثاني بالعكس. فمثل الحيوان لتألفه حساً وحقيقة من الأجسام المختلفة، وعدم مساواة جزئه الكل في الاسم والحد لا حساً ولا حقيقة كان مركباً بأي تفسير فسر، وبأي اعتبار أخذ والماء لعدم تألفه منها، ولمساواة جزئه الكل فيهما كان بسيطاً كذلك، والفلك لعدم تألفه منها لا حساً ولا حقيقة، وعدم مساواة جزئه الكل كذلك كان بسيطاً على التفسير الأول بالاعتبارين، مركباً على التفسير الثاني بالاعتبارين، والذهب لتألفه من الأجسام المختلفة حقيقة لا حساً ولمساواة جزئه الكل حساً لا حقيقة كان على التفسير الأول مركباً، إذا أخذنا باعتبار الحقيقة بسيطاً، إذا أخذ باعتبار الحس وعلى التفسير الثاني بالعكس.
He said: 'And that which is taken in each' of the two interpretations may be considered sensory and may be considered real. For by the sensory consideration, animal is composite in reality, water is absolutely simple, the celestial sphere is [simple] by the first interpretation especially, and gold is composite in reality and simple sensorily by both interpretations. He has mentioned for each of the simple body and the composite body two interpretations: one existential and the other privational. So now he is indicating that what is made the basis of the two interpretations—namely, composition from bodies of different natures and equality of the part and whole in name and definition—may be considered from the aspect of reality and may be considered from the aspect of sense. Thus four different interpretations arise for each of the simple and composite, by generality and specificity, opposite in existential and privational. For the simple: what is not composed of different natures, really what is not composed of them sensorily, what its part equals the whole really, what equals it sensorily. For the composite: what is composed really, what is composed sensorily, what does not equal really, what does not equal sensorily. So what is taken from the first basis for the composite is existential, and for the simple is privational; the second basis is the opposite. For example, animal, because it is composed sensorily and really of different bodies, and its part is not equal to the whole in name and definition sensorily nor really, is composite by any interpretation and by any consideration taken. Water, because it is not composed of them and its part equals the whole in both, is simple likewise. The celestial sphere, because it is not composed of them sensorily nor really, and its part is not equal to the whole likewise, is simple according to the first interpretation in both considerations, composite according to the second interpretation in both considerations. Gold, because it is composed of different bodies really not sensorily, and its part equals the whole sensorily not really, is according to the first interpretation composite if we take it by consideration of reality, simple if taken by consideration of sense, and according to the second interpretation the opposite.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage explains two interpretations (tafsiran) of simple and composite bodies. The author begins by quoting a statement about 'that which is taken in each interpretation' and then gives examples: animal is composite in reality when considered sensorily, water is absolutely simple, the celestial sphere is simple by the first interpretation only, gold is composite in reality but simple sensorily by both interpretations. He then states that there are two interpretations: existential (wujudi) and privational ('adami). The basis for these interpretations are two criteria: composition from different natures, and equality of part to whole. These can be considered either from the aspect of reality (haqiqa) or from the aspect of sense (hiss). This yields four possible interpretations for both simple and composite, each with a combination of existential and privational status. The author then applies these to examples: animal is composite by all interpretations because it is composed both sensorily and really and its part is not equal; water is simple by all because it lacks composition and its part equals; the celestial sphere is simple by the first interpretation (existential) but composite by the second (privational) because it lacks both composition and equality; gold is composite by the first interpretation in reality but simple sensorily, and opposite under the second interpretation. The passage demonstrates the nuanced classification of bodies based on different conceptual and perceptual criteria.

Key Terms
تفسير وجودي
تفسير عدمي
حقيقة
حس
تألف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simple and composite are fixed categories inherent to the body itself.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows they depend on interpretation (existential vs privational) and perspective (reality vs sense).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which body is simple by the first interpretation but composite by the second interpretation?
A) Animal
B) Water
C) The celestial sphere
D) Gold

Key Takeaway
The classification of bodies as simple or composite depends on two factors: the interpretation type (existential/privational) and the consideration (real/sensory).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you label something as 'simple' or 'composite' in your own life—whether it's a concept, a problem, or a relationship—do you pause to ask whether you are judging by sensory appearance or by reality? How might this distinction change how you approach it?
?
The celestial sphere is both simple and composite depending on the interpretation. Is there an area of your faith where you have hastily classified something as one or the other without considering the deeper reality? Commit to examining one such area today.
?
Gold is composite in reality but simple to the senses. What in your character appears simple on the surface but is composed of many hidden traits? Will you take time to understand the true composition of your own soul?

Foundations of Simple and Composite Bodies
Commentary introducing the two interpretations and two considerations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations (existential and non-existential) and the two considerations (reality and sense) that form the basis for defining simple and composite bodies.

Core Idea
Definitions of simple and composite depend on interpretation and consideration.

In this passage, the commentator begins by quoting the foundational premise that simple and composite bodies are defined by two interpretations: existential (based on composition) and non-existential (based on lack thereof). He then explains that these interpretations can be considered from the perspective of reality or sense, yielding four possible definitions. This lesson establishes the conceptual framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the matn quote
Read the opening quotation carefully. Notice the mention of the two interpretations and the statement about gold.
The matn gives a preview of the classification.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The commentator states there are two interpretations for each body: one existential and one non-existential. Understand what each means.
Existential = based on composition; non-existential = based on lack of composition.
3
Understand the two considerations
The basis of these interpretations (composition and equality) can be considered either in reality (ḥaqīqatan) or in sense (ḥissan). This is the key to the four definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه لا يكون مثل الكل في الاسم والحد لا في البسيط ولا في المركب. قال (والمأخوذ في كل من تفسيري كل قد يعتبر حسياً، وقد يعتبر حقيقياً. فبالاعتبار الحسي مركب حقيقة، والماء بسيط مطلقاً، والفلك بالتفسير الأول خاصة، والذهب بالتفسيرين مركب حقيقة، بسيط حساً). قد ذكر لكل من الجسم البسيط والجسم المركب تفسيرين أحدهما وجودي والآخر عدمي. فهو الآن يشير إلى أن ما جعل مأخذ التفسيرين – أعني التألف من الأجسام المختلفة الطبائع، وتساوي الجزء والكل في الاسم والحد – قد يعتبر من حيث الحقيقة وقد يعتبر من حيث الحس، فيحصل لكل من البسيط والمركب أربعة تفسيرات مختلفة، بالعموم والخصوص، متعاكسة في الوجودية والعدمية. فللبسيط ما لا يتألف من المختلفات حقيقة، ما لا يتألف منها حساً، ما يساوي جزؤه الكل حقيقة، ما يساويه حساً. وللمركب ما يتألف حقيقة، ما يتألف حساً، ما لا يساوي حقيقة، ما لا يساوي حساً. فالمأخوذ من المأخذ الأول للمركب وجودي، والبسيط عدمي، والمأخذ الثاني بالعكس. فمثل الحيوان لتألفه حساً وحقيقة من الأجسام المختلفة، وعدم مساواة جزئه الكل في الاسم والحد لا حساً ولا حقيقة كان مركباً بأي تفسير فسر، وبأي اعتبار أخذ. والماء لعدم تألفه منها، ولمساواة جزئه الكل فيهما كان بسيطاً كذلك. والفلك لعدم تألفه منها لا حساً ولا حقيقة، وعدم مساواة جزئه الكل كذلك كان بسيطاً على التفسير الأول بالاعتبارين، مركباً على التفسير الثاني بالاعتبارين. والذهب لتألفه من الأجسام المختلفة حقيقة لا حساً، ولمساواة جزئه الكل حساً لا حقيقة كان على التفسير الأول مركباً إذا أخذنا باعتبار الحقيقة، بسيطاً إذا أخذ باعتبار الحس، وعلى التفسير الثاني بالعكس.
Then it is not like the whole in name and definition, neither in the simple nor in the composite. He said: 'And what is taken from each of the two interpretations of each (body) may be considered sensibly, and may be considered really. Thus by sense-consideration it is composite in reality, and water is absolutely simple, and the celestial sphere specifically according to the first interpretation, and gold according to both interpretations is composite in reality, simple in sense.' He already mentioned for each of the simple body and the composite body two interpretations, one existential and the other non-existential. Now he indicates that what is made the basis of the two interpretations – namely, composition from bodies of different natures, and equality of the part and whole in name and definition – may be considered in terms of reality and may be considered in terms of sense. Thus for each of the simple and composite there arise four different interpretations, in terms of generality and specificity, opposite in existentiality and non-existentiality. For the simple: what is not composed of different things in reality, what is not composed of them in sense, what its part equals the whole in reality, what equals it in sense. For the composite: what is composed in reality, what is composed in sense, what is not equal in reality, what is not equal in sense. So what is taken from the first basis for the composite is existential, and for the simple non-existential; the second basis is opposite. For example, the animal, because it is composed sensibly and really of different bodies, and its part does not equal the whole in name and definition either sensibly or really, is composite regardless of which interpretation it is interpreted by and which consideration it is taken with. Water, because it is not composed of them, and its part equals the whole in both, is simple likewise. The celestial sphere, because it is not composed of them either sensibly or really, and its part does not equal the whole likewise, is simple according to the first interpretation with both considerations, composite according to the second interpretation with both considerations. Gold, because it is composed of different bodies really but not sensibly, and its part equals the whole sensibly but not really, is according to the first interpretation composite if we take it by reality-consideration, simple if taken by sense-consideration, and according to the second interpretation the opposite.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definitions of simple and composite bodies. It begins by quoting a statement from the matn: 'Then it is not like the whole in name and definition, neither in the simple nor in the composite.' Then the commentator explains that there are two interpretations for each body: one existential (based on actual composition) and one non-existential (based on lack thereof). These interpretations can be considered either from the perspective of reality (ḥaqīqatan) or from the perspective of sense perception (ḥissan). This yields four possible definitions for each simple and composite body, which are opposite in terms of existentiality and non-existentiality. The commentator then applies these definitions to four examples: animal (composite in both reality and sense), water (simple in both), the celestial sphere (simple by the first interpretation, composite by the second), and gold (composite by reality but simple by sense in the first interpretation, and the opposite in the second). This detailed classification is typical of kalām discussions on the nature of physical bodies, though the commentator later warns that such discussions are often borrowed from philosophy and are not accepted by the mutakallimūn without scrutiny.

Key Terms
بسيط
مركب
تفسير وجودي
تفسير عدمي
حقيقة
حس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'simple' and 'composite' have single, absolute definitions.
Correction: The passage shows that definitions depend on whether one considers reality or sense, and on the interpretation used.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many different interpretations arise for each of the simple and composite bodies when considering reality and sense?
A) Two interpretations
B) Four interpretations
C) One interpretation
D) Eight interpretations

Key Takeaway
The definitions of simple and composite bodies are relative to the interpretation (existential/non-existential) and consideration (reality/sense).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage states that gold is 'composite in reality, simple in sense'. When you evaluate your own character, do you often settle for a 'sense' impression—seeing yourself as simple and uncomplicated—while ignoring the deeper reality of your inner complexity and struggles? What specific step can you take today to honestly examine the 'real' composition of your heart?
?
The commentator shows that the same body can be classified differently depending on the interpretation and consideration applied. Think of a theological disagreement you hold strongly. Could it be that you and your opponent are operating under different 'existential' or 'non-existential' interpretations, or different 'real' versus 'sense' considerations? How might embracing this possibility soften your certainty and open a path to mutual understanding?

Four Definitions and Examples of Simple and Composite Bodies
Enumeration of four definitions and their application to examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four definitions of simple and composite bodies and apply them to examples like animal, water, celestial sphere, and gold.

Core Idea
The same body can be simple or composite depending on interpretation and consideration.

Building on the previous framework, this lesson presents the four definitions arising from combining the two interpretations and two considerations. The commentator then illustrates these definitions with four concrete examples from natural and celestial bodies, showing how the same object can be classified differently based on the chosen interpretation and consideration.

Guided Path
1
Read the four definitions
Read the passage listing the four definitions for simple and composite bodies. Notice they are opposites.
Simple: not composed in reality/sense, part equals whole; Composite: composed, part not equal.
2
Study the example of animal
Animal is composed in both reality and sense, part not equal, so it is composite by any interpretation.
3
Study the example of water
Water is not composed in either way and part equals whole, so it is simple by any interpretation.
4
Study the celestial sphere and gold
The celestial sphere is simple by the first interpretation but composite by the second. Gold is composite by reality but simple by sense in the first interpretation, and opposite in the second.
Note how classification switches.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه لا يكون مثل الكل في الاسم والحد لا في البسيط ولا في المركب. قال (والمأخوذ في كل من تفسيري كل قد يعتبر حسياً، وقد يعتبر حقيقياً. فبالاعتبار الحسي مركب حقيقة، والماء بسيط مطلقاً، والفلك بالتفسير الأول خاصة، والذهب بالتفسيرين مركب حقيقة، بسيط حساً). قد ذكر لكل من الجسم البسيط والجسم المركب تفسيرين أحدهما وجودي والآخر عدمي. فهو الآن يشير إلى أن ما جعل مأخذ التفسيرين – أعني التألف من الأجسام المختلفة الطبائع، وتساوي الجزء والكل في الاسم والحد – قد يعتبر من حيث الحقيقة وقد يعتبر من حيث الحس، فيحصل لكل من البسيط والمركب أربعة تفسيرات مختلفة، بالعموم والخصوص، متعاكسة في الوجودية والعدمية. فللبسيط ما لا يتألف من المختلفات حقيقة، ما لا يتألف منها حساً، ما يساوي جزؤه الكل حقيقة، ما يساويه حساً. وللمركب ما يتألف حقيقة، ما يتألف حساً، ما لا يساوي حقيقة، ما لا يساوي حساً. فالمأخوذ من المأخذ الأول للمركب وجودي، والبسيط عدمي، والمأخذ الثاني بالعكس. فمثل الحيوان لتألفه حساً وحقيقة من الأجسام المختلفة، وعدم مساواة جزئه الكل في الاسم والحد لا حساً ولا حقيقة كان مركباً بأي تفسير فسر، وبأي اعتبار أخذ. والماء لعدم تألفه منها، ولمساواة جزئه الكل فيهما كان بسيطاً كذلك. والفلك لعدم تألفه منها لا حساً ولا حقيقة، وعدم مساواة جزئه الكل كذلك كان بسيطاً على التفسير الأول بالاعتبارين، مركباً على التفسير الثاني بالاعتبارين. والذهب لتألفه من الأجسام المختلفة حقيقة لا حساً، ولمساواة جزئه الكل حساً لا حقيقة كان على التفسير الأول مركباً إذا أخذنا باعتبار الحقيقة، بسيطاً إذا أخذ باعتبار الحس، وعلى التفسير الثاني بالعكس.
Then it is not like the whole in name and definition, neither in the simple nor in the composite. He said: 'And what is taken from each of the two interpretations of each (body) may be considered sensibly, and may be considered really. Thus by sense-consideration it is composite in reality, and water is absolutely simple, and the celestial sphere specifically according to the first interpretation, and gold according to both interpretations is composite in reality, simple in sense.' He already mentioned for each of the simple body and the composite body two interpretations, one existential and the other non-existential. Now he indicates that what is made the basis of the two interpretations – namely, composition from bodies of different natures, and equality of the part and whole in name and definition – may be considered in terms of reality and may be considered in terms of sense. Thus for each of the simple and composite there arise four different interpretations, in terms of generality and specificity, opposite in existentiality and non-existentiality. For the simple: what is not composed of different things in reality, what is not composed of them in sense, what its part equals the whole in reality, what equals it in sense. For the composite: what is composed in reality, what is composed in sense, what is not equal in reality, what is not equal in sense. So what is taken from the first basis for the composite is existential, and for the simple non-existential; the second basis is opposite. For example, the animal, because it is composed sensibly and really of different bodies, and its part does not equal the whole in name and definition either sensibly or really, is composite regardless of which interpretation it is interpreted by and which consideration it is taken with. Water, because it is not composed of them, and its part equals the whole in both, is simple likewise. The celestial sphere, because it is not composed of them either sensibly or really, and its part does not equal the whole likewise, is simple according to the first interpretation with both considerations, composite according to the second interpretation with both considerations. Gold, because it is composed of different bodies really but not sensibly, and its part equals the whole sensibly but not really, is according to the first interpretation composite if we take it by reality-consideration, simple if taken by sense-consideration, and according to the second interpretation the opposite.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definitions of simple and composite bodies. It begins by quoting a statement from the matn: 'Then it is not like the whole in name and definition, neither in the simple nor in the composite.' Then the commentator explains that there are two interpretations for each body: one existential (based on actual composition) and one non-existential (based on lack thereof). These interpretations can be considered either from the perspective of reality (ḥaqīqatan) or from the perspective of sense perception (ḥissan). This yields four possible definitions for each simple and composite body, which are opposite in terms of existentiality and non-existentiality. The commentator then applies these definitions to four examples: animal (composite in both reality and sense), water (simple in both), the celestial sphere (simple by the first interpretation, composite by the second), and gold (composite by reality but simple by sense in the first interpretation, and the opposite in the second). This detailed classification is typical of kalām discussions on the nature of physical bodies, though the commentator later warns that such discussions are often borrowed from philosophy and are not accepted by the mutakallimūn without scrutiny.

Key Terms
بسيط
مركب
حقيقة
حس
تألف
تفسير وجودي
تفسير عدمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A body is either simply simple or simply composite, regardless of interpretation.
Correction: The passage shows that the same body can be simple or composite based on which interpretation and consideration are used, as seen with gold and the celestial sphere.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the classification of the celestial sphere under the second interpretation with both considerations?
A) Simple
B) Composite
C) Both simple and composite
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The classification of bodies as simple or composite is not absolute but depends on the interpretation (existential/non-existential) and the consideration (reality/sense).

?
Gold appears simple to the senses but is really composite. Is there a habit or action in your life that appears simple but is actually composed of multiple intentions? How can you purify your intentions to match the reality?

Warning that the investigations in this chapter are narratives from philosophy and not accepted by mutakallimun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the detailed classifications of bodies in this chapter are borrowed from philosophy and not accepted by the mutakallimun because they are based on corrupt or unestablished principles.

Core Idea
The classifications are philosophical, not Islamic.

The commentator issues a crucial warning before diving into the philosophical classifications of bodies. وليلم أن معظم مباحث الفصل حكاية عن الفلسفة مبنى على أصول فاسدة أو غير ثابتة. يريد أن أكثر المباحث التي تورد في الأقسام الأربعة من هذا الفصل حكاية الفلسفة غير مسلمة عند المتكلمين لابتنائها على أصول ثبت فسادها مثل كون الصانع موجباً لا مختاراً، وأن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد، أو لم تثبت صحتها مثل كون الأجسام مختلفة بالحقيقة ومركبة من الهيولي والصورة.
Let it be known that most of the investigations of the chapter are narratives from philosophy based on corrupt or unestablished principles. He means that most of the investigations that are presented in the four sections of this chapter are narratives from philosophy not accepted by the mutakallimun because they are based on principles whose corruption has been established, such as that the Maker is necessitating not free, and that the one does not issue from him except one, or whose soundness has not been established, such as that bodies are different in reality and composed of matter and form.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a cautionary note from the commentator about the preceding discussions. He states that most of the investigations (mabāḥith) in this chapter are borrowed from philosophy. They are based on principles that are either proven to be corrupt (fāsidah) or not established as sound (ghayr thābitah). Examples of corrupt principles include the philosophical doctrine that the Maker (al-Ṣāniʿ) acts by necessity (mūjib) rather than free choice (mukhtār), and that from the One only one can emanate (al-wāḥid lā yaṣduru ʿanhu illā wāḥid). Examples of unestablished principles include the idea that bodies are different in reality and composed of matter (hayūlā) and form (ṣūrah). The commentator thus warns that these philosophical premises are not accepted by the mutakallimūn, and the classifications derived from them should not be taken as authoritative Islamic doctrine.

Transition to Astronomy: The Ninth Sphere and Its Implications
Concludes sphere sounds, introduces second investigation and astronomy's benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological and methodological significance of the celestial spheres' sounds, the claim of a ninth sphere, and the role of astronomy in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Astronomy serves practical religious needs and deepens reflection on Allah's creation.

This passage marks a crucial transition from discussing the physical properties of celestial spheres to introducing the science of astronomy. The author first describes the wondrous sounds generated by the spheres' rotation, then presents the claim of a ninth sphere. He elaborates on the benefits of astronomy, emphasizing its relevance to religious practices and theological reflection, while cautioning against flawed narration. This lesson explores how the author justifies incorporating astronomical inquiry into kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of sphere sounds
Read the first sentence describing the physical qualities of the spheres and the sounds they produce. Notice the effect on human souls.
2
Examine the claim of the ninth sphere
Look at the phrase 'المحدد تاسع الأفلاك' and understand the debate about the number of spheres (seven, eight, or nine).
3
List the three benefits of astronomy
Identify the three ways astronomy benefits Islamic thought: practical religious rulings, reflection on creation, and refutation of falsehoods.
The author explicitly states three categories.
4
Understand the methodological critique
Note the author's criticism of al-Mawaqif for inattentive narration and his insistence on accurate transmission from the Almagest.
This shows the importance of careful scholarship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يكون من الصلابة واليبس والملاسة، وهي في دورانها يماس بعضها بعضاً، فيسمع منها المتلطفون بالحكمة والرياضة أصواتاً عجيبة غريبة موسيقية مطربة، وألحاناً ونغمات متناسبة مستحسنة تقف عندها القوى البدنية، وتتحير النفوس البشرية .
المبحث الثاني
المحدد تاسع الأفلاك في زعمهم
زعموا أن المحدد تاسع الأفلاك بمعنى قيام الدليل على وجود التسعة، وإن جوز بعضهم ردها إلى الثمانية بل السبعة .
قد انجر الكلام ههنا إلى ذكر جمل من علم الهيئة الباحث عن أحوال الأجسام البسيطة العلوية والسفلية من حيث كمياتها، وكيفياتها، وأوضاعها وحركاتها اللازمة لها، لأن بعض ذلك مما ينتفع به في الشرعيات، كتعدد المشارق والمغارب، واختلاف المطالع وأمر القبلة وأوقات الصلاة، وغير ذلك، وبعضه مما يعين على التفكر في خلق السموات والأرض المؤدي إلى مزيد خبرة ببالغ حكمة الصانع ، وباهر قدرته وبعضه مما يجب التنبه لفساده، فيحكى كذلك وهذا العلم فيما بينهم أيضاً، يذكر على طريق الحكاية، عن علم آخر فيه براهينه يسمونه المجسطي، فلا بأس إن اقتصرنا على مجرد الحكاية، لكن على وجهها إن شاء الله تعالى، لا كما وقع في المواقف. فيتعجب من له أدنى نظر في هذا الفن قلة اهتمام الحاكي بالمحكي ، ويتخذ ذلك مغمزاً على المتصدي لتحقيق من العلوم الإسلامية فنقول لما وجدوا الشمس والقمر وسائر الكواكب متحركة
What is of hardness, dryness, and smoothness, and they in their rotation touch one another, so those refined by wisdom and discipline hear from them wondrous, strange, musical, entertaining sounds, and harmonious and pleasing melodies and tunes, at which physical powers cease, and human souls become perplexed. The Second Investigation: The determiner of the ninth sphere according to their claim. They claimed that the determiner of the ninth sphere means the establishment of evidence for the existence of nine [spheres], although some allowed reducing them to eight or even seven. The discussion here has led to mentioning some [topics] from the science of astronomy (علم الهيئة), which investigates the states of simple bodies, celestial and terrestrial, in terms of their quantities, qualities, positions, and their necessary movements, because some of that is beneficial for legal [religious] matters, such as the multiplicity of points of sunrise and sunset, the variation of rising points, the matter of the qibla and prayer times, and other things; and some of it aids reflection on the creation of the heavens and the earth, leading to greater knowledge of the profound wisdom of the Maker and His overwhelming power; and some of it requires attention to its falsehood, so it is narrated as such. This science among them is also mentioned by way of narration from another science in which its proofs are found, which they call the Almagest (المجسطي). There is no harm if we confine ourselves to mere narration, but in its proper manner, God willing, not as occurred in al-Mawaqif (المواقف). One who has the slightest insight into this art is amazed at the lack of attention of the narrator to the narrated matter, and considers that a point of criticism against one who undertakes the investigation of Islamic sciences. So we say: since they found the sun, the moon, and all the planets moving...
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage serves as a transition from the previous investigation on the sounds of celestial spheres to the second investigation on the determination of the ninth sphere. It begins by describing the physical properties of the spheres (hardness, dryness, smoothness) and how their rotation causes them to touch, producing wondrous musical sounds that overwhelm human senses. Then it introduces the second investigation with the claim that the 'determiner of the ninth sphere' means proving the existence of nine spheres, though some allow eight or seven. The author explains that this discussion leads to aspects of astronomy (علم الهيئة), which studies simple celestial and terrestrial bodies in terms of quantities, qualities, positions, and necessary motions. He lists three benefits of astronomy: (1) practical religious applications like determining sunrise/sunset points, prayer times, and the qibla; (2) aiding reflection on creation to deepen knowledge of the Creator's wisdom and power; (3) identifying and refuting falsehoods. He notes that astronomy is transmitted from the Almagest and that it is permissible to narrate it accurately, unlike the flawed narration in al-Mawaqif, which he criticizes for the narrator's inattention. Finally, he begins his own argument with 'فنقول' (we say), starting from the observed motion of the sun, moon, and planets.

Proof that the Spheres are Nine
The author presents the core proof for nine spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how astronomers deduced the existence of nine celestial spheres from observed motions.

Core Idea
The number of spheres is deduced from observed motions, not arbitrary.

This lesson presents the core astronomical proof that led classical scholars to affirm nine spheres. The argument begins with careful observation of celestial motions and the fact that planets are embedded in spheres, leading to a logical conclusion about their number.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once to get the flow of the argument. Notice the sequence: observation of motions, then conclusion.
The passage uses technical terms like 'حركة يومية' (daily motion) and 'حركة بطيئة' (slow motion).
2
Identify the observations
The astronomers observed two types of motion: a daily motion from east to west and a slow motion from west to east. They also observed seven planets with distinct motions.
3
Understand the key principle
The passage states that planets are fixed in spheres, not floating freely. This is crucial because it leads to the necessity of separate spheres for each motion.
The principle is: two different motions cannot occur in one body at the same time.
4
See the conclusion
From the two primary motions and seven planetary motions, they concluded that there must be nine spheres: two for the primary motions and seven for the planets.
This is a deductive proof based on observed motions and the impossibility of multiple motions in one sphere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما وجدوا الشمس والقمر وسائر الكواكب متحركة بالحركة اليومية من المشرق إلى المغرب، ثم وجدوها بالنظر الدقيق متحركة حركة بطيئة من المغرب إلى المشرق، ووجدوا الكواكب السبعة أعني الشمس والقمر وزحل والمشتري والمريخ والزهرة وعطارد ذوي حركات غريبة مختلفة غير متشابهة بقياس بعضها إلى البعض، وكانت الكواكب عندهم مركوزة في الأفلاك لا كالحيتان في المياه، بنوا على ذلك أن الأفلاك الكلية الشاملة للأرض، الكائنة على مركزها تسعة اثنان للحركتين الأوليين وسبعة لحركات السبعة السيارة، لامتناع الحركتين المختلفتين في زمان واحد من جسم واحد. وأما في جانب الكثرة فلا قطع لجواز أن يكون كل من الثوابت على فلك وأن تكون الأفلاك غير الكوكبية كثيرة محيط بعضها بالبعض، لكنهم لم يذهبوا إلى ذلك لعدم الدليل ولأنهم لم يجدوا في السمويات فضلاً لا حاجة إليه، وجوز بعضهم أن تكون الأفلاك ثمانية تستند الحركة الأولى إلى مجموعها، لا إلى فلك خاص وذلك بأن تتصل بها نفس واحدة تحركها الحركة اليومية. قال صاحب التحفة: فيجوز أن تكون سبعة بأن تكون الثوابت ودوائر البروج على محدب فلك زحل، وتتعلق نفس بمجموع السبعة تحركها الحركة الأولى، وأخرى بالسابعة تحركها الحركة الأخرى. لكن بشرط أن نفرض دوائر البروج متحركة بالسريعة دون البطيئة لتنتقل الثوابت بها من برج إلى برج كما هو الواقع. قال ( وإنه لا كوكب عليه وإنه يتحرك من المشرق إلى المغرب على منطقة ).
When they found the sun, moon, and all the stars moving with the daily motion from east to west, then they found them by precise observation moving with a slow motion from west to east, and they found the seven planets—namely the sun, moon, Saturn, Jupiter, Mars, Venus, and Mercury—having strange, different, and dissimilar motions in relation to each other, and the planets in their view were fixed in the spheres, not like fish in water, they built upon that that the universal spheres encompassing the earth, centered upon it, are nine: two for the two primary motions and seven for the motions of the seven planets, because it is impossible for two different motions to occur in one body at one time. As for the side of multiplicity, there is no certainty about the permissibility that each of the fixed stars has a sphere and that the non-planetary spheres are many, surrounding one another, but they did not go to that due to lack of proof and because they found in the heavens no extravagance without need. And some permitted that the spheres be eight, with the first motion attributed to their totality, not to a particular sphere, and that by a single soul connecting to them moving them with the daily motion. The author of al-Tuhfah said: It is permissible that they be seven, with the fixed stars and the zodiacal circles on the convex of the sphere of Saturn, and a soul attaches to the totality of the seven moving them with the first motion, and another to the seventh moving it with the other motion. But on condition that we suppose the zodiacal circles to be moving with the fast motion without the slow, so that the fixed stars are transferred by them from one sign to another, as is actually the case. He said: And there is no planet upon it, and it moves from east to west on an orb.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a proof/argument explaining the reasoning behind the claim that the spheres are nine. The author begins by stating that when the astronomers observed the sun, moon, and other stars moving with a daily motion from east to west, and also found by precise observation a slow motion from west to east, and found the seven planets (sun, moon, Saturn, Jupiter, Mars, Venus, Mercury) having peculiar and dissimilar motions, and since the planets are embedded in the spheres (not like fish in water), they built on that the universal spheres encompassing the earth and centered on it are nine: two for the two primary motions (daily and slow) and seven for the motions of the seven planets. The reason is the impossibility of two different motions in one body at the same time. Then he discusses multiplicity: there is no certainty that each fixed star has its own sphere or that many non-planetary spheres exist surrounding each other, but they did not adopt this due to lack of evidence and because they found no unnecessary extravagance in the heavens. Some allowed the spheres to be eight, with the first motion attributed to the totality of the eight, not a specific sphere, by a single soul moving them with the daily motion. The author of al-Tuhfah suggests it could be seven, with the fixed stars and zodiac on the convex of Saturn's sphere, and one soul moves the seven with the first motion, and another soul moves the seventh with the other motion, but with the condition that the zodiac circles move with the fast motion without the slow, so the fixed stars are carried from one sign to another, as observed. Then a statement is quoted: 'And there is no planet upon it, and it moves from east to west on an orb.' This refers to the sphere of the fixed stars which has no planet but moves with the daily motion. The passage thus presents the standard Ptolemaic argument for nine spheres and then discusses possible reductions to eight or seven, showing the reasoning and limitations.

Alternative Views on the Number of Spheres
The author discusses alternative views: possible increase to many, reduction to eight or seven spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative possibilities for the number of spheres discussed by astronomers and their reasoning.

Core Idea
The number of spheres is not absolutely fixed; alternatives are considered based on evidence.

After establishing the standard proof for nine spheres, the author discusses other possibilities. Some scholars considered whether the number could be more or less, and they present arguments for eight or seven spheres, showing that the question was open to rational inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
This part discusses alternative views. First, the possibility of many more spheres for each fixed star, but that is rejected due to lack of evidence.
The principle of 'no extravagance without need' is invoked.
2
Understand the eight-sphere view
Some allowed that the eight spheres move together with the daily motion as a whole, not a separate ninth sphere.
This simplifies the system by attributing the daily motion to the aggregate of the eight spheres.
3
Study the seven-sphere view from al-Tuhfah
Al-Tuhfah proposed that the fixed stars and zodiac are on the convex of Saturn's sphere, reducing the count to seven, with two souls moving the group.
This requires a special condition about the motion of the zodiac circles.
4
Reflect on the methodology
Notice that the astronomers are using empirical observation and logical reasoning, not just authority. They consider multiple possibilities and choose based on evidence.
This reflects the rational approach of classical Islamic science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما وجدوا الشمس والقمر وسائر الكواكب متحركة بالحركة اليومية من المشرق إلى المغرب، ثم وجدوها بالنظر الدقيق متحركة حركة بطيئة من المغرب إلى المشرق، ووجدوا الكواكب السبعة أعني الشمس والقمر وزحل والمشتري والمريخ والزهرة وعطارد ذوي حركات غريبة مختلفة غير متشابهة بقياس بعضها إلى البعض، وكانت الكواكب عندهم مركوزة في الأفلاك لا كالحيتان في المياه، بنوا على ذلك أن الأفلاك الكلية الشاملة للأرض، الكائنة على مركزها تسعة اثنان للحركتين الأوليين وسبعة لحركات السبعة السيارة، لامتناع الحركتين المختلفتين في زمان واحد من جسم واحد. وأما في جانب الكثرة فلا قطع لجواز أن يكون كل من الثوابت على فلك وأن تكون الأفلاك غير الكوكبية كثيرة محيط بعضها بالبعض، لكنهم لم يذهبوا إلى ذلك لعدم الدليل ولأنهم لم يجدوا في السمويات فضلاً لا حاجة إليه، وجوز بعضهم أن تكون الأفلاك ثمانية تستند الحركة الأولى إلى مجموعها، لا إلى فلك خاص وذلك بأن تتصل بها نفس واحدة تحركها الحركة اليومية. قال صاحب التحفة: فيجوز أن تكون سبعة بأن تكون الثوابت ودوائر البروج على محدب فلك زحل، وتتعلق نفس بمجموع السبعة تحركها الحركة الأولى، وأخرى بالسابعة تحركها الحركة الأخرى. لكن بشرط أن نفرض دوائر البروج متحركة بالسريعة دون البطيئة لتنتقل الثوابت بها من برج إلى برج كما هو الواقع. قال ( وإنه لا كوكب عليه وإنه يتحرك من المشرق إلى المغرب على منطقة ).
When they found the sun, moon, and all the stars moving with the daily motion from east to west, then they found them by precise observation moving with a slow motion from west to east, and they found the seven planets—namely the sun, moon, Saturn, Jupiter, Mars, Venus, and Mercury—having strange, different, and dissimilar motions in relation to each other, and the planets in their view were fixed in the spheres, not like fish in water, they built upon that that the universal spheres encompassing the earth, centered upon it, are nine: two for the two primary motions and seven for the motions of the seven planets, because it is impossible for two different motions to occur in one body at one time. As for the side of multiplicity, there is no certainty about the permissibility that each of the fixed stars has a sphere and that the non-planetary spheres are many, surrounding one another, but they did not go to that due to lack of proof and because they found in the heavens no extravagance without need. And some permitted that the spheres be eight, with the first motion attributed to their totality, not to a particular sphere, and that by a single soul connecting to them moving them with the daily motion. The author of al-Tuhfah said: It is permissible that they be seven, with the fixed stars and the zodiacal circles on the convex of the sphere of Saturn, and a soul attaches to the totality of the seven moving them with the first motion, and another to the seventh moving it with the other motion. But on condition that we suppose the zodiacal circles to be moving with the fast motion without the slow, so that the fixed stars are transferred by them from one sign to another, as is actually the case. He said: And there is no planet upon it, and it moves from east to west on an orb.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a proof/argument explaining the reasoning behind the claim that the spheres are nine. The author begins by stating that when the astronomers observed the sun, moon, and other stars moving with a daily motion from east to west, and also found by precise observation a slow motion from west to east, and found the seven planets (sun, moon, Saturn, Jupiter, Mars, Venus, Mercury) having peculiar and dissimilar motions, and since the planets are embedded in the spheres (not like fish in water), they built on that the universal spheres encompassing the earth and centered on it are nine: two for the two primary motions (daily and slow) and seven for the motions of the seven planets. The reason is the impossibility of two different motions in one body at the same time. Then he discusses multiplicity: there is no certainty that each fixed star has its own sphere or that many non-planetary spheres exist surrounding each other, but they did not adopt this due to lack of evidence and because they found no unnecessary extravagance in the heavens. Some allowed the spheres to be eight, with the first motion attributed to the totality of the eight, not a specific sphere, by a single soul moving them with the daily motion. The author of al-Tuhfah suggests it could be seven, with the fixed stars and zodiac on the convex of Saturn's sphere, and one soul moves the seven with the first motion, and another soul moves the seventh with the other motion, but with the condition that the zodiac circles move with the fast motion without the slow, so the fixed stars are carried from one sign to another, as observed. Then a statement is quoted: 'And there is no planet upon it, and it moves from east to west on an orb.' This refers to the sphere of the fixed stars which has no planet but moves with the daily motion. The passage thus presents the standard Ptolemaic argument for nine spheres and then discusses possible reductions to eight or seven, showing the reasoning and limitations.

Uncertainty and the Principle of Economy in Astronomy
Acknowledging uncertainty and defending against unnecessary objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle of economy is used to defend astronomical models against speculative objections.

Core Idea
Astronomers avoid unnecessary assumptions; objections lacking evidence are not valid.

In this passage, the author explains that astronomers acknowledge uncertainty about the number of heavenly spheres. They do not claim absolute knowledge but rely on the principle of not positing any entity without a demonstrated benefit. This methodological stance protects against objections that are based on mere possibility rather than evidence.

Guided Path
1
Admitting uncertainty
Read the first part: the astronomers acknowledge they are not certain about the number of spheres or the arrangement of fixed stars.
This is an important admission that shows humility.
2
The principle of economy
The key phrase is 'عدم إثبات الفضل المستغنى عنه' – they do not posit a sphere without a benefit. Understand this principle.
This is similar to Occam's razor.
3
Deflecting the objection
The objection ('why not each star on its own sphere?') is rejected because it would posit a superfluous benefit. No evidence requires it.
Thus the objection has no force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كانوا معترفين بأنه لا قطع في جانب كثرة الأفلاك، وأنه لا يمتنع كون الثوابت على أفلاك شتى متفقة الحركات ، وأنهم إنما بنوا الكلام على عدم إثبات الفضل المستغنى عنه، فلا جهة للاعتراض بأنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون كل من الثوابت على فلك، وأن يكون بعضها تحت السيارات أو فيما بينها.
And since they acknowledge that there is no certainty regarding the abundance of spheres, and that it is not impossible that the fixed stars are on many spheres with consistent motions, and that they based their discourse on not establishing a superfluous benefit, there is no room for the objection: why is it not allowed that each of the fixed stars be on a sphere, and that some of them be below the wanderers or in between them?
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a methodological defense. The astronomers admit that they are not certain about the exact number of spheres; it is possible that each fixed star has its own sphere, and some could be below the planets. However, they argue that since their procedure is to not posit any sphere without a demonstrated benefit, such possibilities do not constitute a valid objection. The argument is based on the principle of economy: do not multiply entities unnecessarily.

الثوابت

الفضل المستغنى عنه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: They acknowledge uncertainty and rely on the principle of economy.

Key Takeaway
Uncertainty does not invalidate a model; methodological principles like economy can defend against speculative objections.

Determining the Order of the Planets
Sanusi explains the bases for planetary order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the empirical and rational methods used by Ptolemaic astronomers to determine the order of the planetary spheres, including observations of eclipses, parallax, and aesthetic reasoning.

Core Idea
Planetary order was deduced from eclipses, parallax, and harmonious arrangement.

Before the detailed structure of spheres could be described, astronomers needed to decide which sphere lay above which. Sanusi explains how they used observable phenomena like lunar eclipses and parallax, and even aesthetic preference, to establish the sequence. This passage shows that the order was not arbitrary but based on multiple lines of evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument from lunar eclipses
Notice how the moon's ability to eclipse other planets and some fixed stars indicates its sphere is the lowest. This is a key observational basis.
The sun is an exception; it is only eclipsed by the moon.
2
Understand the role of parallax
Parallax is used to determine relative distances: objects with parallax are closer. The sun shows parallax, placing it below the superior planets but above Venus and Mercury.
3
Consider aesthetic reasoning
The sun is placed in the middle of the planets by preference for good order, like a pendant in a necklace. This shows that beauty and harmony were considered valid arguments.
The text says 'استحساناً' (preference).
4
Address potential objections
The author preemptively answers why fixed stars cannot be below planets or interspersed: because astronomers avoid unnecessary multiplicity and have no certainty on that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني أنهم وجدوا القمر يكسف (۱) سائر السيارات، ومن الثوابت ما يكون على ممره، فحكموا بأن فلكه تحت الكل ، وهكذا الحكم في البواقي إلا الشمس ، فإنها لا يكسفها غير القمر ولا يدرك كسفها بشيء من الكواكب، لاحترافهـا عنـد مقارنتها، فالحكم بكونها فوق الزهرة وعطارد استحساناً لما فيه من حسن الترتيب، وجودة النظام، حيث يكون النير الأعظم في الوسط من السيارات بمنزلة شمسة القلادة، وقد تأكد هذا الاستحسان بمناسبات أخر. وزعم بعضهم أنه رأى الزهرة كشامة على صفحة الشمس، والحكم بكونها تحت الثلاثة العلوية . أعني زحل والمشتري والمريخ، مأخوذ من اختلاف المنظر، وهو بعد ما بين طرفي الخطين المارين بمركز الكوكب الواصلين إلى فلك البروج الخارج أحدهما من مركز العالم، والآخر موضع الناظر. فإن وجوده يدل على القرب منا، وعدمه على البعد ، وقد وجد للشمس دون العلوية والثوابت، فعلم أنها تحتها، ولم يعرف وجوده للزهرة وعطارد، لأنه إنما يعرف بإله لهم تسمى ذات الشعبتين تنصب في سطح نصف النهار ، والزهرة وعطارد لكونهما حوالي الشمس دائماً، لا يصلان إلى نصف النهار ظاهرين، ولما كانوا معترفين بأنه لا قطع في جانب كثرة الأفلاك، وأنه لا يمتنع كون الثوابت على أفلاك شتى متفقة الحركات ، وأنهم إنما بنوا الكلام على عدم إثبات الفضل المستغنى عنه، فلا جهة للاعتراض بأنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون كل من الثوابت على فلك، وأن يكون بعضها تحت السيارات ؛ أو فيما بينها .
قال (وأفلاكها الكلية ممثلات لكون مناطقها على منطقة البروج ] . يعني أن الفلك الكلي لكل من السبعة السيارة يسمى ممثل ذلك الكوكب بمعنى كونه ممثلاً لفلك البروج ، أي موافقاً له بالمركز والمنطقة والقطبين .
تفصيل الأفلاك الجزئية التي يشتمل عليها الأفلاك الكلية
قال وكل جوف ممثل القمر فلك آخر مركزه الأرض يسمى المائل، ثم في تخن المائل، وكل من الممثلات الغير القمر فلك شامل للأرض، خارج مركزه عن مركزها يسمى في عطارد مديراً، وفي باقي المتحيرة حاملاً يماس محدبه محدب المائل، أو الممثل بنقطة تسمى الأوج، ومقعره بنقطة تسمى الحضيض، ويبقى الفضل جسمين مستديرين على مركز العالم يسميان بالمتممين، يتدرج كل منهما من غلظ بقدر ما بين المركزين إلى رقة ينتهي عند نقطتي التماس على التبادل، بمعنى أن رقة الحاوي منهما عند الأوج، وغلظه عند الحضيض، والمحوى بالعكس، وفي ثخن المدير فلك آخر خارج المركز يسمى الحامل، ينفصل عن المدير، كالمدير عن الممثل فيكون لعطارد أوجان وحضيضان وأربع متممات والمائل في المتحيرة اسم للحامل، ثم في كل ثخن كل حامل كرة تسمى فلك التدوير، أحد طرفي قطرها يماس محدب الحامل، والآخر مقعره، والكوكب مفرق يماس سطحه سطحه والشمس في الخارج المركز كالتدوير في الحامل ] .
يريد الإشارة إلى تفصيل الأفلاك الجزئية التي يشتمل عليها الأفلاك الكلية وقد أرشدهم إلى ذلك ما أدركوا بالرصد للسيارات، من اختلاف الأوضاع والحركات، فمن الأفلاك الجزئية ممثل القمر ويسمى الجوزهرى لكونه المحرك للجوهريين ومشعر فيها ومنها حائل القمر وهو فلك في جوف ممثل القمر مركزه مركز العالم ، يسمى بذلك لكون منطقته مائلة عن منطقة البروج ميلاً ثابتاً لا يتغير (فالجوزهري) (۱) يماس بمحدبه مقعر ممثل عطارد، وبمقعره محدب المائل المماس بمقعره محدب كرة النار ومنها الأفلاك الخارجة المراكز والخارج المركز فلك محيط بالأرض خارج مركزه عن مركزها، ويكون في ثخن (۳) فلك،
Meaning that they found that the moon eclipses the rest of the planets, and some fixed stars are on its path, so they judged that its sphere is under all [spheres]. And such is the judgment for the rest except the sun, for nothing but the moon eclipses it, nor is its eclipse perceived by any planet, due to its being in conjunction [with the moon]. So the judgment that it is above Venus and Mercury is a preference because of the good arrangement and excellence of order, as the greatest luminary is in the middle of the planets like the pendant of a necklace. And this preference was confirmed by other congruities. And some claimed that he saw Venus as a mole on the face of the sun. And the judgment that it is under the three superior [planets], namely Saturn, Jupiter, and Mars, is taken from the parallax, which is the distance between the two ends of the two lines passing through the center of the planet, reaching the zodiac, one issued from the center of the world, the other from the position of the observer. For its existence indicates proximity to us, and its absence indicates distance. And it was found for the sun but not for the superior [planets] and fixed stars, so it was known that it is under them. And its existence was not known for Venus and Mercury because it is only known by an instrument called 'the one with two branches' erected in the plane of the meridian, and Venus and Mercury, being always around the sun, do not reach the meridian appearing. And since they acknowledged that there is no certainty in favor of multiplicity of spheres, and that it is not impossible that the fixed stars are on various spheres with agreeing motions, and that they only based their statement on not proving the superfluous that is unnecessary, there is no ground for objection that why is it not permissible that each of the fixed stars be on a sphere, and that some of them be under the planets or among them.
He said: 'And their universal spheres are parallels because their [zones] are on the zone of the zodiac.' Meaning that the universal sphere for each of the seven planets is called the parallel of that planet, meaning that it is parallel to the zodiac, i.e., conforming to it in center, zone, and poles.
Detail of the partial spheres contained in the universal spheres.
He said: 'And every interior of the moon's parallel is another sphere whose center is the earth, called the inclined [sphere]. Then in the thickness of the inclined, and each of the non-moon parallels is a sphere encompassing the earth, its center outside its center called in Mercury the director, and in the rest of the wandering [planets] the carrier, its convexity touches the convexity of the inclined, or the parallel at a point called the apogee, and its concavity at a point called the perigee. And the remainder remains two round bodies on the center of the world called the completers, each gradually increasing from thickness to thinness according to the distance between the two centers, ending at the two points of contact alternately, meaning that the thinness of the container of them is at the apogee and its thickness at the perigee, and the contained is opposite. And in the thickness of the director, there is another sphere outside the center called the carrier, separate from the director, as the director is from the parallel, so for Mercury there are two apogees and two perigees and four completers. And the inclined in the wandering [planets] is a name for the carrier. Then in each thickness of each carrier is a sphere called the epicycle, one end of its diameter touches the convexity of the carrier, the other its concavity, and the planet is in its thickness, its surface touching its surface. And the sun is in the eccentric like the epicycle in the carrier.'
He intends to indicate the detail of the partial spheres contained in the universal spheres. And what they perceived by observation of the planets, from the diversity of positions and motions, guided them to that. So from the partial spheres is the moon's parallel, also called the jawzahri because it moves the two lunar nodes and is manifest in them. And from them is the moon's barrier, which is a sphere in the interior of the moon's parallel, its center the center of the world, so called because its zone is inclined from the zodiac by a fixed inclination that does not change. And (al-jawzahri) touches with its convexity the concavity of Mercury's parallel, and with its concavity the convexity of the inclined which touches with its concavity the convexity of the sphere of fire. And from them are the eccentrics, and the eccentric is a sphere surrounding the earth, its center outside its center, and it is in the thickness of a sphere.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the Ptolemaic model of planetary spheres, excerpted from a theological discussion likely using astronomical order as evidence for divine wisdom. The author begins by explaining how astronomers determined the order of the planets: they observed that the moon eclipses other planets and some fixed stars, so its sphere is lowest. The sun never eclipses except by the moon, and it is placed above Venus and Mercury by aesthetic preference (the greatest light in the middle). The judgment that the sun is below the superior planets (Saturn, Jupiter, Mars) is based on parallax: the sun shows parallax, indicating it is closer than the fixed stars but farther than those planets? Actually, the text says parallax was found for the sun but not for the superior planets and fixed stars, so the sun is under them? Wait: 'فعلم أنها تحتها' meaning 'so it was known that it (the sun) is under them' — so the sun is below the superior planets? That seems odd. But the argument is that parallax exists for the sun (closer to us) and not for the superior planets (farther), thus the sun is under them. The author then addresses a potential objection: since they acknowledge that there is no certainty in the multiplicity of spheres and that it is possible that fixed stars are on different spheres, there is no ground to object that fixed stars could be under planets. Then the author defines 'universal spheres' as parallels (ممثلات) for each planet, meaning they are concentric with the zodiac (same center, zone, poles). Then he gives a detailed description of the partial spheres within these universal spheres. For the moon, there is the 'inclined sphere' (falk maa'il). For other planets, inside the universal parallel there is an eccentric sphere called 'director' (mudir) for Mercury and 'carrier' (hamil) for others. This eccentric touches the parallel at apogee and perigee, leaving two crescent-shaped 'completers' (mutammimat) that fill the gaps. Within the director's thickness is another eccentric called 'carrier' for Mercury, giving two apogees and perigees. Then within each carrier is an epicycle (falk al-tadwir) where the planet rides. The sun is in an eccentric like the epicycle in the carrier. The author concludes that these details were derived from observations of planetary positions and motions. He lists more partial spheres: the moon's parallel (also called jawzahri), the moon's barrier (haafil al-qamar), and eccentrics.

اختلاف المنظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The planetary order is a matter of religious doctrine.
Correction: The text clearly states that the order is based on empirical observation and aesthetic preference, not on divine revelation. The astronomers acknowledged uncertainty and avoided unnecessary assumptions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the sun placed above Venus and Mercury?
A) Because it never eclipses them
B) Because of aesthetic preference and good order
C) Because of parallax
D) Because it is the greatest luminary

Key Takeaway
The order of planetary spheres was deduced from a combination of observation (eclipses, parallax) and reasoning (aesthetic harmony), not from divine revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, astronomers used 'istihsan' (aesthetic preference) to place the sun above Venus and Mercury when direct evidence was lacking. In your life, are there beliefs or decisions you hold based on what 'feels right' or seems harmonious, rather than clear evidence? How can you tell the difference, and what is the cost of being wrong?
?
The scholars acknowledged they had no certainty about the arrangement of the fixed stars and avoided unnecessary assumptions. What assumptions in your understanding of your faith, your relationships, or your purpose might be 'superfluous'—held without evidence? Are you willing to examine them honestly, even if it means letting them go?
?
If the greatest luminary (the sun) is placed in the middle of the planets like a pendant on a necklace, what does that say about the central role of guidance in your life? Is there a 'sun'—a principle, a person, or a practice—that you have placed at the center of your existence? Or have you allowed lesser things to occupy that position?

Method of Learning
beginner
3 min
The Universal Spheres (ممثلات)
Sanusi defines the universal sphere for each planet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of a universal sphere (ممثل) for each planet as a perfect sphere parallel to the zodiac, sharing its center, zone, and poles.

Core Idea
Each planet's universal sphere is a perfect copy of the zodiac.

After discussing the ordfer of planets, Sanusi introduces the first layer of their spheres: the universal sphere. This sphere, called the 'parallel' (ممثل), is a perfect sphere that exactly mirrors the zodiac in terms of center, zone, and poles. It serves as the outermost boundary for each planet's system.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the universal sphere
Sanusi states that the universal sphere for each planet is called its 'ممثل' (parallel) because it is parallel to the zodiac: same center, zone, and poles.
The word 'ممثل' literally means 'representative' or 'parallel'.
2
Visualize the parallelism
Imagine two spheres nested perfectly with the same center. The outer one is the zodiac, the inner one (for the planet) is its universal sphere. Both share the same equator (zone) and axis (poles).
This means the universal sphere is concentric and coaxial with the zodiac.
3
Note the significance
The universal sphere provides a stable reference frame. Later, astronomers will add additional spheres inside it to explain planetary motions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني أنهم وجدوا القمر يكسف (۱) سائر السيارات، ومن الثوابت ما يكون على ممره، فحكموا بأن فلكه تحت الكل ، وهكذا الحكم في البواقي إلا الشمس ، فإنها لا يكسفها غير القمر ولا يدرك كسفها بشيء من الكواكب، لاحترافهـا عنـد مقارنتها، فالحكم بكونها فوق الزهرة وعطارد استحساناً لما فيه من حسن الترتيب، وجودة النظام، حيث يكون النير الأعظم في الوسط من السيارات بمنزلة شمسة القلادة، وقد تأكد هذا الاستحسان بمناسبات أخر. وزعم بعضهم أنه رأى الزهرة كشامة على صفحة الشمس، والحكم بكونها تحت الثلاثة العلوية . أعني زحل والمشتري والمريخ، مأخوذ من اختلاف المنظر، وهو بعد ما بين طرفي الخطين المارين بمركز الكوكب الواصلين إلى فلك البروج الخارج أحدهما من مركز العالم، والآخر موضع الناظر. فإن وجوده يدل على القرب منا، وعدمه على البعد ، وقد وجد للشمس دون العلوية والثوابت، فعلم أنها تحتها، ولم يعرف وجوده للزهرة وعطارد، لأنه إنما يعرف بإله لهم تسمى ذات الشعبتين تنصب في سطح نصف النهار ، والزهرة وعطارد لكونهما حوالي الشمس دائماً، لا يصلان إلى نصف النهار ظاهرين، ولما كانوا معترفين بأنه لا قطع في جانب كثرة الأفلاك، وأنه لا يمتنع كون الثوابت على أفلاك شتى متفقة الحركات ، وأنهم إنما بنوا الكلام على عدم إثبات الفضل المستغنى عنه، فلا جهة للاعتراض بأنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون كل من الثوابت على فلك، وأن يكون بعضها تحت السيارات ؛ أو فيما بينها .
قال (وأفلاكها الكلية ممثلات لكون مناطقها على منطقة البروج ] . يعني أن الفلك الكلي لكل من السبعة السيارة يسمى ممثل ذلك الكوكب بمعنى كونه ممثلاً لفلك البروج ، أي موافقاً له بالمركز والمنطقة والقطبين .
تفصيل الأفلاك الجزئية التي يشتمل عليها الأفلاك الكلية
قال وكل جوف ممثل القمر فلك آخر مركزه الأرض يسمى المائل، ثم في تخن المائل، وكل من الممثلات الغير القمر فلك شامل للأرض، خارج مركزه عن مركزها يسمى في عطارد مديراً، وفي باقي المتحيرة حاملاً يماس محدبه محدب المائل، أو الممثل بنقطة تسمى الأوج، ومقعره بنقطة تسمى الحضيض، ويبقى الفضل جسمين مستديرين على مركز العالم يسميان بالمتممين، يتدرج كل منهما من غلظ بقدر ما بين المركزين إلى رقة ينتهي عند نقطتي التماس على التبادل، بمعنى أن رقة الحاوي منهما عند الأوج، وغلظه عند الحضيض، والمحوى بالعكس، وفي ثخن المدير فلك آخر خارج المركز يسمى الحامل، ينفصل عن المدير، كالمدير عن الممثل فيكون لعطارد أوجان وحضيضان وأربع متممات والمائل في المتحيرة اسم للحامل، ثم في كل ثخن كل حامل كرة تسمى فلك التدوير، أحد طرفي قطرها يماس محدب الحامل، والآخر مقعره، والكوكب مفرق يماس سطحه سطحه والشمس في الخارج المركز كالتدوير في الحامل ] .
يريد الإشارة إلى تفصيل الأفلاك الجزئية التي يشتمل عليها الأفلاك الكلية وقد أرشدهم إلى ذلك ما أدركوا بالرصد للسيارات، من اختلاف الأوضاع والحركات، فمن الأفلاك الجزئية ممثل القمر ويسمى الجوزهرى لكونه المحرك للجوهريين ومشعر فيها ومنها حائل القمر وهو فلك في جوف ممثل القمر مركزه مركز العالم ، يسمى بذلك لكون منطقته مائلة عن منطقة البروج ميلاً ثابتاً لا يتغير (فالجوزهري) (۱) يماس بمحدبه مقعر ممثل عطارد، وبمقعره محدب المائل المماس بمقعره محدب كرة النار ومنها الأفلاك الخارجة المراكز والخارج المركز فلك محيط بالأرض خارج مركزه عن مركزها، ويكون في ثخن (۳) فلك،
Meaning that they found that the moon eclipses the rest of the planets, and some fixed stars are on its path, so they judged that its sphere is under all [spheres]. And such is the judgment for the rest except the sun, for nothing but the moon eclipses it, nor is its eclipse perceived by any planet, due to its being in conjunction [with the moon]. So the judgment that it is above Venus and Mercury is a preference because of the good arrangement and excellence of order, as the greatest luminary is in the middle of the planets like the pendant of a necklace. And this preference was confirmed by other congruities. And some claimed that he saw Venus as a mole on the face of the sun. And the judgment that it is under the three superior [planets], namely Saturn, Jupiter, and Mars, is taken from the parallax, which is the distance between the two ends of the two lines passing through the center of the planet, reaching the zodiac, one issued from the center of the world, the other from the position of the observer. For its existence indicates proximity to us, and its absence indicates distance. And it was found for the sun but not for the superior [planets] and fixed stars, so it was known that it is under them. And its existence was not known for Venus and Mercury because it is only known by an instrument called 'the one with two branches' erected in the plane of the meridian, and Venus and Mercury, being always around the sun, do not reach the meridian appearing. And since they acknowledged that there is no certainty in favor of multiplicity of spheres, and that it is not impossible that the fixed stars are on various spheres with agreeing motions, and that they only based their statement on not proving the superfluous that is unnecessary, there is no ground for objection that why is it not permissible that each of the fixed stars be on a sphere, and that some of them be under the planets or among them.
He said: 'And their universal spheres are parallels because their [zones] are on the zone of the zodiac.' Meaning that the universal sphere for each of the seven planets is called the parallel of that planet, meaning that it is parallel to the zodiac, i.e., conforming to it in center, zone, and poles.
Detail of the partial spheres contained in the universal spheres.
He said: 'And every interior of the moon's parallel is another sphere whose center is the earth, called the inclined [sphere]. Then in the thickness of the inclined, and each of the non-moon parallels is a sphere encompassing the earth, its center outside its center called in Mercury the director, and in the rest of the wandering [planets] the carrier, its convexity touches the convexity of the inclined, or the parallel at a point called the apogee, and its concavity at a point called the perigee. And the remainder remains two round bodies on the center of the world called the completers, each gradually increasing from thickness to thinness according to the distance between the two centers, ending at the two points of contact alternately, meaning that the thinness of the container of them is at the apogee and its thickness at the perigee, and the contained is opposite. And in the thickness of the director, there is another sphere outside the center called the carrier, separate from the director, as the director is from the parallel, so for Mercury there are two apogees and two perigees and four completers. And the inclined in the wandering [planets] is a name for the carrier. Then in each thickness of each carrier is a sphere called the epicycle, one end of its diameter touches the convexity of the carrier, the other its concavity, and the planet is in its thickness, its surface touching its surface. And the sun is in the eccentric like the epicycle in the carrier.'
He intends to indicate the detail of the partial spheres contained in the universal spheres. And what they perceived by observation of the planets, from the diversity of positions and motions, guided them to that. So from the partial spheres is the moon's parallel, also called the jawzahri because it moves the two lunar nodes and is manifest in them. And from them is the moon's barrier, which is a sphere in the interior of the moon's parallel, its center the center of the world, so called because its zone is inclined from the zodiac by a fixed inclination that does not change. And (al-jawzahri) touches with its convexity the concavity of Mercury's parallel, and with its concavity the convexity of the inclined which touches with its concavity the convexity of the sphere of fire. And from them are the eccentrics, and the eccentric is a sphere surrounding the earth, its center outside its center, and it is in the thickness of a sphere.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the Ptolemaic model of planetary spheres, excerpted from a theological discussion likely using astronomical order as evidence for divine wisdom. The author begins by explaining how astronomers determined the order of the planets: they observed that the moon eclipses other planets and some fixed stars, so its sphere is lowest. The sun never eclipses except by the moon, and it is placed above Venus and Mercury by aesthetic preference (the greatest light in the middle). The judgment that the sun is below the superior planets (Saturn, Jupiter, Mars) is based on parallax: the sun shows parallax, indicating it is closer than the fixed stars but farther than those planets? Actually, the text says parallax was found for the sun but not for the superior planets and fixed stars, so the sun is under them? Wait: 'فعلم أنها تحتها' meaning 'so it was known that it (the sun) is under them' — so the sun is below the superior planets? That seems odd. But the argument is that parallax exists for the sun (closer to us) and not for the superior planets (farther), thus the sun is under them. The author then addresses a potential objection: since they acknowledge that there is no certainty in the multiplicity of spheres and that it is possible that fixed stars are on different spheres, there is no ground to object that fixed stars could be under planets. Then the author defines 'universal spheres' as parallels (ممثلات) for each planet, meaning they are concentric with the zodiac (same center, zone, poles). Then he gives a detailed description of the partial spheres within these universal spheres. For the moon, there is the 'inclined sphere' (falk maa'il). For other planets, inside the universal parallel there is an eccentric sphere called 'director' (mudir) for Mercury and 'carrier' (hamil) for others. This eccentric touches the parallel at apogee and perigee, leaving two crescent-shaped 'completers' (mutammimat) that fill the gaps. Within the director's thickness is another eccentric called 'carrier' for Mercury, giving two apogees and perigees. Then within each carrier is an epicycle (falk al-tadwir) where the planet rides. The sun is in an eccentric like the epicycle in the carrier. The author concludes that these details were derived from observations of planetary positions and motions. He lists more partial spheres: the moon's parallel (also called jawzahri), the moon's barrier (haafil al-qamar), and eccentrics.
