Key Takeaway
The universal sphere for each planet is a perfect sphere parallel to the zodiac, providing a base for additional partial spheres.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The universe is built on such precise conformity that every planetary sphere perfectly mirrors the zodiac. What does this level of order demand from you when you consider the One who designed it? Will you continue to treat His creation as random, or will you let this precision drive you to study His signs with reverence?
?
You have learned that the celestial spheres are not independent but perfectly aligned. In your own life, where have you settled for chaos or independence when Allah has shown you the harmony of His system? What is one step you will take today to align your life with the order He has placed in creation?

Partial Spheres of the Moon
Sanusi enumerates the partial spheres for the moon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and describe the three partial spheres associated with the moon in the Ptolemaic model: the inclined sphere (المائل), the moon's barrier (حائل القمر), and the jawzahri (الجوزهرى).

Core Idea
The moon's motion required three extra spheres: inclined, barrier, and jawzahri.

Now Sanusi begins detailing the partial spheres inside the universal spheres. For the moon, astronomers added several spheres to account for its complex motion, including an inclined orbit and a barrier to handle eclipses. This passage lists them in order.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first sphere: the inclined (المائل)
Inside the moon's parallel (universal sphere), there is another sphere with its center at the earth, called the inclined sphere. Its zone is tilted relative to the zodiac.
The name 'مائل' means 'inclined'.
2
Understand the jawzahri (الجوزهرى)
The text says the moon's parallel is also called 'الجوزهرى' because it moves the two lunar nodes (ascending and descending) which are called 'جوهريان'.
The jawzahri sphere is actually the same as the moon's parallel? The passage says 'ممثل القمر ويسمى الجوزهرى'.
3
Learn about the moon's barrier (حائل القمر)
This is a sphere inside the moon's parallel with its center at the earth, its zone inclined by a fixed angle. It touches other spheres at its boundaries.
The name 'حائل' means 'barrier' or 'screen'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قال (وأفلاكها الكلية ممثلات لكون مناطقها على منطقة البروج ] . يعني أن الفلك الكلي لكل من السبعة السيارة يسمى ممثل ذلك الكوكب بمعنى كونه ممثلاً لفلك البروج ، أي موافقاً له بالمركز والمنطقة والقطبين .
تفصيل الأفلاك الجزئية التي يشتمل عليها الأفلاك الكلية

Meaning that they found that the moon eclipses the rest of the planets, and some fixed stars are on its path, so they judged that its sphere is under all [spheres]. And such is the judgment for the rest except the sun, for nothing but the moon eclipses it, nor is its eclipse perceived by any planet, due to its being in conjunction [with the moon]. And this preference was confirmed by other congruities. And some claimed that he saw Venus as a mole on the face of the sun. And the judgment that it is under the three superior [planets], namely Saturn, Jupiter, and Mars, is taken from the parallax, which is the distance between the two ends of the two lines passing through the center of the planet, reaching the zodiac, one issued from the center of the world, the other from the position of the observer. For its existence indicates proximity to us, and its absence indicates distance. And it was found for the sun but not for the superior [planets] and fixed stars, so it was known that it is under them. And its existence was not known for Venus and Mercury because it is only known by an instrument called 'the one with two branches' erected in the plane of the meridian, and Venus and Mercury, being always around the sun, do not reach the meridian appearing. And since they acknowledged that there is no certainty in favor of multiplicity of spheres, and that it is not impossible that the fixed stars are on various spheres with agreeing motions, and that they only based their statement on not proving the superfluous that is unnecessary, there is no ground for objection that why is it not permissible that each of the fixed stars be on a sphere, and that some of them be under the planets or among them.
He said: 'And their universal spheres are parallels because their [zones] are on the zone of the zodiac.' Meaning that the universal sphere for each of the seven planets is called the parallel of that planet, meaning that it is parallel to the zodiac, i.e., conforming to it in center, zone, and poles.
Detail of the partial spheres contained in the universal spheres.
He said: 'And every interior of the moon's parallel is another sphere whose center is the earth, called the inclined [sphere]. Then in the thickness of the inclined, and each of the non-moon parallels is a sphere encompassing the earth, its center outside its center called in Mercury the director, and in the rest of the wandering [planets] the carrier, its convexity touches the convexity of the inclined, or the parallel at a point called the apogee, and its concavity at a point called the perigee. And the remainder remains two round bodies on the center of the world called the completers, each gradually increasing from thickness to thinness according to the distance between the two centers, ending at the two points of contact alternately, meaning that the thinness of the container of them is at the apogee and its thickness at the perigee, and the contained is opposite. And in the thickness of the director, there is another sphere outside the center called the carrier, separate from the director, as the director is from the parallel, so for Mercury there are two apogees and two perigees and four completers. And the inclined in the wandering [planets] is a name for the carrier. Then in each thickness of each carrier is a sphere called the epicycle, one end of its diameter touches the convexity of the carrier, the other its concavity, and the planet is in its thickness, its surface touching its surface. And the sun is in the eccentric like the epicycle in the carrier.'
He intends to indicate the detail of the partial spheres contained in the universal spheres. And what they perceived by observation of the planets, from the diversity of positions and motions, guided them to that. So from the partial spheres is the moon's parallel, also called the jawzahri because it moves the two lunar nodes and is manifest in them. And from them is the moon's barrier, which is a sphere in the interior of the moon's parallel, its center the center of the world, so called because its zone is inclined from the zodiac by a fixed inclination that does not change. And (al-jawzahri) touches with its convexity the concavity of Mercury's parallel, and with its concavity the convexity of the inclined which touches with its concavity the convexity of the sphere of fire. And from them are the eccentrics, and the eccentric is a sphere surrounding the earth, its center outside its center, and it is in the thickness of a sphere.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the Ptolemaic model of planetary spheres, excerpted from a theological discussion likely using astronomical order as evidence for divine wisdom. The author begins by explaining how astronomers determined the order of the planets: they observed that the moon eclipses other planets and some fixed stars, so its sphere is lowest. The sun never eclipses except by the moon, and it is placed above Venus and Mercury by aesthetic preference (the greatest light in the middle). The judgment that the sun is below the superior planets (Saturn, Jupiter, Mars) is based on parallax: the sun shows parallax, indicating it is closer than the fixed stars but farther than those planets? Actually, the text says parallax was found for the sun but not for the superior planets and fixed stars, so the sun is under them? Wait: 'فعلم أنها تحتها' meaning 'so it was known that it (the sun) is under them' — so the sun is below the superior planets? That seems odd. But the argument is that parallax exists for the sun (closer to us) and not for the superior planets (farther), thus the sun is under them. The author then addresses a potential objection: since they acknowledge that there is no certainty in the multiplicity of spheres and that it is possible that fixed stars are on different spheres, there is no ground to object that fixed stars could be under planets. Then the author defines 'universal spheres' as parallels (ممثلات) for each planet, meaning they are concentric with the zodiac (same center, zone, poles). Then he gives a detailed description of the partial spheres within these universal spheres. For the moon, there is the 'inclined sphere' (falk maa'il). For other planets, inside the universal parallel there is an eccentric sphere called 'director' (mudir) for Mercury and 'carrier' (hamil) for others. This eccentric touches the parallel at apogee and perigee, leaving two crescent-shaped 'completers' (mutammimat) that fill the gaps. Within the director's thickness is another eccentric called 'carrier' for Mercury, giving two apogees and perigees. Then within each carrier is an epicycle (falk al-tadwir) where the planet rides. The sun is in an eccentric like the epicycle in the carrier. The author concludes that these details were derived from observations of planetary positions and motions. He lists more partial spheres: the moon's parallel (also called jawzahri), the moon's barrier (haafil al-qamar), and eccentrics.

Method of Learning
advanced
6 min
Partial Spheres of Other Planets
Sanusi details the partial spheres for planets other than the moon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ptolemaic system of eccentrics and epicycles for planets other than the moon, including the roles of the carrier, director, completers, and epicycle, as well as the special cases of Mercury and the sun.

Core Idea
Non-moon planets use eccentrics and epicycles; Mercury has a double eccentric; the sun has only an eccentric.

For planets other than the moon, astronomers needed more complex mechanisms to explain their varying speeds and retrograde motion. Sanusi describes a system of eccentrics (carrier and director) and epicycles, with special details for Mercury and the sun.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basic eccentric (خارج المركز)
For each non-moon planet, there is an eccentric sphere (center not at earth) called 'مدير' (director) for Mercury and 'حامل' (carrier) for other planets. It touches the universal sphere at the apogee and perigee.
The gap between the universal sphere and the eccentric is filled by two crescent-shaped 'completers'.
2
Learn about the completers (متممات)
These are two solid bodies that fill the space between the universal sphere and the eccentric. Their thickness varies, being thin at apogee and thick at perigee for the outer one, and vice versa.
They are concentric with the earth, not the eccentric.
3
Note Mercury's double eccentric
Mercury has an additional eccentric called 'الحامل' (carrier) inside the director, giving it two apogees and two perigees and four completers.
The inclined sphere (المائل) for other planets is actually this carrier.
4
Understand the epicycle (فلك التدوير)
Inside each carrier's thickness there is an epicycle, a small sphere whose diameter touches the carrier's convexity and concavity. The planet is embedded in the epicycle.
5
Examine the sun's special case
The sun is placed in an eccentric sphere alone, without an epicycle, analogous to how the epicycle is inside the carrier for other planets.
The sun's motion is simpler, requiring only one eccentric.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني أنهم وجدوا القمر يكسف (۱) سائر السيارات، ومن الثوابت ما يكون على ممره، فحكموا بأن فلكه تحت الكل ، وهكذا الحكم في البواقي إلا الشمس ، فإنها لا يكسفها غير القمر ولا يدرك كسفها بشيء من الكواكب، لاحترافهـا عنـد مقارنتها، فالحكم بكونها فوق الزهرة وعطارد استحساناً لما فيه من حسن الترتيب، وجودة النظام، حيث يكون النير الأعظم في الوسط من السيارات بمنزلة شمسة القلادة، وقد تأكد هذا الاستحسان بمناسبات أخر. وزعم بعضهم أنه رأى الزهرة كشامة على صفحة الشمس، والحكم بكونها تحت الثلاثة العلوية . أعني زحل والمشتري والمريخ، مأخوذ من اختلاف المنظر، وهو بعد ما بين طرفي الخطين المارين بمركز الكوكب الواصلين إلى فلك البروج الخارج أحدهما من مركز العالم، والآخر موضع الناظر. فإن وجوده يدل على القرب منا، وعدمه على البعد ، وقد وجد للشمس دون العلوية والثوابت، فعلم أنها تحتها، ولم يعرف وجوده للزهرة وعطارد، لأنه إنما يعرف بإله لهم تسمى ذات الشعبتين تنصب في سطح نصف النهار ، والزهرة وعطارد لكونهما حوالي الشمس دائماً، لا يصلان إلى نصف النهار ظاهرين، ولما كانوا معترفين بأنه لا قطع في جانب كثرة الأفلاك، وأنه لا يمتنع كون الثوابت على أفلاك شتى متفقة الحركات ، وأنهم إنما بنوا الكلام على عدم إثبات الفضل المستغنى عنه، فلا جهة للاعتراض بأنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون كل من الثوابت على فلك، وأن يكون بعضها تحت السيارات ؛ أو فيما بينها .
قال (وأفلاكها الكلية ممثلات لكون مناطقها على منطقة البروج ] . يعني أن الفلك الكلي لكل من السبعة السيارة يسمى ممثل ذلك الكوكب بمعنى كونه ممثلاً لفلك البروج ، أي موافقاً له بالمركز والمنطقة والقطبين .
تفصيل الأفلاك الجزئية التي يشتمل عليها الأفلاك الكلية
قال وكل جوف ممثل القمر فلك آخر مركزه الأرض يسمى المائل، ثم في تخن المائل، وكل من الممثلات الغير القمر فلك شامل للأرض، خارج مركزه عن مركزها يسمى في عطارد مديراً، وفي باقي المتحيرة حاملاً يماس محدبه محدب المائل، أو الممثل بنقطة تسمى الأوج، ومقعره بنقطة تسمى الحضيض، ويبقى الفضل جسمين مستديرين على مركز العالم يسميان بالمتممين، يتدرج كل منهما من غلظ بقدر ما بين المركزين إلى رقة ينتهي عند نقطتي التماس على التبادل، بمعنى أن رقة الحاوي منهما عند الأوج، وغلظه عند الحضيض، والمحوى بالعكس، وفي ثخن المدير فلك آخر خارج المركز يسمى الحامل، ينفصل عن المدير، كالمدير عن الممثل فيكون لعطارد أوجان وحضيضان وأربع متممات والمائل في المتحيرة اسم للحامل، ثم في كل ثخن كل حامل كرة تسمى فلك التدوير، أحد طرفي قطرها يماس محدب الحامل، والآخر مقعره، والكوكب مفرق يماس سطحه سطحه والشمس في الخارج المركز كالتدوير في الحامل ] .
يريد الإشارة إلى تفصيل الأفلاك الجزئية التي يشتمل عليها الأفلاك الكلية وقد أرشدهم إلى ذلك ما أدركوا بالرصد للسيارات، من اختلاف الأوضاع والحركات، فمن الأفلاك الجزئية ممثل القمر ويسمى الجوزهرى لكونه المحرك للجوهريين ومشعر فيها ومنها حائل القمر وهو فلك في جوف ممثل القمر مركزه مركز العالم ، يسمى بذلك لكون منطقته مائلة عن منطقة البروج ميلاً ثابتاً لا يتغير (فالجوزهري) (۱) يماس بمحدبه مقعر ممثل عطارد، وبمقعره محدب المائل المماس بمقعره محدب كرة النار ومنها الأفلاك الخارجة المراكز والخارج المركز فلك محيط بالأرض خارج مركزه عن مركزها، ويكون في ثخن (۳) فلك،
Meaning that they found that the moon eclipses the rest of the planets, and some fixed stars are on its path, so they judged that its sphere is under all [spheres]. And such is the judgment for the rest except the sun, for nothing but the moon eclipses it, nor is its eclipse perceived by any planet, due to its being in conjunction [with the moon]. So the judgment that it is above Venus and Mercury is a preference because of the good arrangement and excellence of order, as the greatest luminary is in the middle of the planets like the pendant of a necklace. And this preference was confirmed by other congruities. And some claimed that he saw Venus as a mole on the face of the sun. And the judgment that it is under the three superior [planets], namely Saturn, Jupiter, and Mars, is taken from the parallax, which is the distance between the two ends of the two lines passing through the center of the planet, reaching the zodiac, one issued from the center of the world, the other from the position of the observer. For its existence indicates proximity to us, and its absence indicates distance. And it was found for the sun but not for the superior [planets] and fixed stars, so it was known that it is under them. And its existence was not known for Venus and Mercury because it is only known by an instrument called 'the one with two branches' erected in the plane of the meridian, and Venus and Mercury, being always around the sun, do not reach the meridian appearing. And since they acknowledged that there is no certainty in favor of multiplicity of spheres, and that it is not impossible that the fixed stars are on various spheres with agreeing motions, and that they only based their statement on not proving the superfluous that is unnecessary, there is no ground for objection that why is it not permissible that each of the fixed stars be on a sphere, and that some of them be under the planets or among them.
He said: 'And their universal spheres are parallels because their [zones] are on the zone of the zodiac.' Meaning that the universal sphere for each of the seven planets is called the parallel of that planet, meaning that it is parallel to the zodiac, i.e., conforming to it in center, zone, and poles.
Detail of the partial spheres contained in the universal spheres.
He said: 'And every interior of the moon's parallel is another sphere whose center is the earth, called the inclined [sphere]. Then in the thickness of the inclined, and each of the non-moon parallels is a sphere encompassing the earth, its center outside its center called in Mercury the director, and in the rest of the wandering [planets] the carrier, its convexity touches the convexity of the inclined, or the parallel at a point called the apogee, and its concavity at a point called the perigee. And the remainder remains two round bodies on the center of the world called the completers, each gradually increasing from thickness to thinness according to the distance between the two centers, ending at the two points of contact alternately, meaning that the thinness of the container of them is at the apogee and its thickness at the perigee, and the contained is opposite. And in the thickness of the director, there is another sphere outside the center called the carrier, separate from the director, as the director is from the parallel, so for Mercury there are two apogees and two perigees and four completers. And the inclined in the wandering [planets] is a name for the carrier. Then in each thickness of each carrier is a sphere called the epicycle, one end of its diameter touches the convexity of the carrier, the other its concavity, and the planet is in its thickness, its surface touching its surface. And the sun is in the eccentric like the epicycle in the carrier.'
He intends to indicate the detail of the partial spheres contained in the universal spheres. And what they perceived by observation of the planets, from the diversity of positions and motions, guided them to that. So from the partial spheres is the moon's parallel, also called the jawzahri because it moves the two lunar nodes and is manifest in them. And from them is the moon's barrier, which is a sphere in the interior of the moon's parallel, its center the center of the world, so called because its zone is inclined from the zodiac by a fixed inclination that does not change. And (al-jawzahri) touches with its convexity the concavity of Mercury's parallel, and with its concavity the convexity of the inclined which touches with its concavity the convexity of the sphere of fire. And from them are the eccentrics, and the eccentric is a sphere surrounding the earth, its center outside its center, and it is in the thickness of a sphere.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All planets have the same number of spheres.
Correction: The text specifies that Mercury has two eccentrics (director and carrier) and four completers, while other planets have one eccentric (carrier) and two completers, and the sun has only an eccentric without an epicycle.

Checkpoint
What is unique about Mercury's system compared to other planets?
A) It has no epicycle
B) It has two eccentrics (director and carrier)
C) It uses a different type of completer
D) It has no eccentric

Key Takeaway
Ptolemaic astronomy used eccentrics, completers, and epicycles to model planetary motions, with special adjustments for Mercury and the sun.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The precision of Mercury's model—with two eccentrics, two apogees, and four completers—shows that even the smallest details are accounted for in Allah's creation. What 'small detail' of your own life—perhaps your prayer, your speech, or your treatment of others—have you been neglecting because you thought it was insignificant? Will you begin to pay attention to it today?
?
The sun, unlike the planets, is placed directly in an eccentric without an epicycle. This reflects a unique order for each part of creation. Are you living according to your own unique purpose as defined by your Creator, or are you trying to fit into a mold that was never meant for you? What is one way you can realign your priorities with your purpose this week?
?
Sanusi described complex celestial motions to explain apparent irregularities. In your life, when things seem chaotic or irregular, do you trust that there is a hidden order and wisdom behind them? Recall a recent difficulty. How can the study of this precise astronomical order strengthen your patience and reliance on Allah's plan?

Approximate Periods of Planetary Transits
Lists approximate transit periods for sun, moon, and five planets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the approximate periods for the sun, moon, and five visible planets to traverse the twelve zodiac signs, and what is meant by the 'order' (tawali) of their motion from west to east.

Core Idea
Each planet's transit through the zodiac takes a unique duration, from the moon's month to Saturn's 30 years.

In this passage from Sharh al-Maqasid, the author provides approximate figures for how long it takes each of the seven planets (including sun and moon) to travel through the twelve zodiac signs. This astronomical data was essential for Islamic calendar calculations and cosmology. The passage also clarifies the direction of motion and what 'order' means in this context.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation.
Read the continuous Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to get a feel for the author's presentation.
Pay attention to the phrase 'على التوالي'.
2
Identify each planet and its period.
Make a mental or written list of the seven planets and the period given for each one. Note that the moon's period is for twelve traversals.
The periods are approximate (على التقريب).
3
Understand the concept of 'order' (tawali).
Notice how the author defines 'order' as motion from west to east (from Aries to Taurus to Gemini) and its opposite as contrary order.
This distinguishes planetary proper motion from the daily east-to-west motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قطع الكواكب السيارة لمنطقة البروج. قال وكل من السيارة يقطع هذه البروج على التوالي، أعني بحركة من المغرب إلى المشرق. إما إجمالاً فالشمس في ثلثمائة وخمسة وستين يوماً وربع يوم وهي السنة الشمسية، والقمر في ثلثمائة وأربعة وخمسين يوماً وسدس يوم وخمس. وزحل في ثلاثين سنة. والمشتري في اثنتي عشرة سنة، والمريخ في سنتين إلا شهراً ونصفاً والزهرة في حدود سنة، وكذا عطارد كل ذلك على التقريب. أعني من الحمل إلى الثور إلى الجوزاء، وهكذا، وهي حركة من المغرب إلى المشرق وعكسه بخلاف التوالي أعني من الحمل إلى الحوت إلى الدلو، وهكذا، ونعني بالحركة إجمالاً الحركة المركبة التي بها يعتبر انتقال الكوكب من برج إلى برج من غير أن ينظر إلى جزئيات الأفلاك وتفاصيل الحركات. وبالتفصيل خلاف ذلك. فالشمس تقطع البروج الاثني عشر في ثلثمائة وخمسة وستين يوماً وربع يوم، وهي السنة الشمسية والقمر يقطعها اثنتي عشرة مرة في السنة القمرية، ثلثمائة وأربعة وخمسين يوماً وسدس يوم وخمس يوم وهي في وزحل في ثلاثين سنة، والمشتري في اثنتي عشرة سنة، والمريخ في سنتين إلا شهراً ونصفاً، وكل من الزهرة وعطارد في سنة ذلك على سبيل التقريب جميعاً، وأما التحقيق فيعرف من الزيجات.
The cutting of the wandering planets through the zodiacal region. He said: And each of the planets traverses these signs in order, that is, with a motion from west to east. In summary: the sun in 365 and a quarter days, which is the solar year; and the moon in 354 and one-sixth and one-fifth days; and Saturn in 30 years; and Jupiter in 12 years; and Mars in two years less a month and a half; and Venus in about a year, and likewise Mercury – all that approximately. That is, from Aries to Taurus to Gemini, and so on; and it is a motion from west to east, and its opposite is contrary to order, that is, from Aries to Pisces to Aquarius, and so on. And by motion in summary we mean the composite motion by which the planet's transition from sign to sign is considered, without looking at the particulars of the spheres and the details of motions. In detail it is otherwise. So the sun traverses the twelve signs in 365 and a quarter days, and that is the solar year; and the moon traverses them twelve times in the lunar year, 354 and one-sixth and one-fifth days; and Saturn in 30 years; and Jupiter in 12 years; and Mars in two years less a month and a half; and each of Venus and Mercury in one year – all that approximately. As for the exact determination, it is known from the astronomical tables (al-zijat).
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage explains the motion of the planets (including the sun and moon) through the twelve zodiac signs. The author first states that each planet traverses the signs in order (from west to east). He gives approximate periods: the sun takes 365.25 days (the solar year), the moon 354.166... days (about a lunar year), Saturn 30 years, Jupiter 12 years, Mars 2 years minus 1.5 months, and Venus and Mercury about a year. He clarifies that 'order' means from Aries to Taurus to Gemini, etc., which is west-to-east motion, while the opposite order (east-to-west) would be from Aries to Pisces to Aquarius. He then distinguishes between summary (ijmal) and detailed (tafsil) motion: summary motion is the composite motion that merely considers the transition from sign to sign, ignoring the complexities of individual spheres and their intricate movements. He repeats the approximate figures, adding that the moon traverses the signs twelve times in a lunar year. Finally, he notes that exact determinations are found in astronomical tables (zijat).

Key Terms
السيارة
يقطع
على التوالي
سنة شمسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Planets move from east to west like the fixed stars.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the planets' proper motion is from west to east through the zodiac, opposite to the apparent daily motion.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The sun, moon, and planets each have distinct periods to traverse the zodiac, and their proper motion is from west to east.

Summary Motion vs. Detailed Motion
Defines summary motion and contrasts it with detailed motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between summary (ijmal) and detailed (tafsil) motion of the planets, and why the approximate periods are calculated based on composite motion.

Core Idea
Summary motion considers only the apparent sign-to-sign transit, ignoring the complexities of individual spheres.

This passage builds on the previous lesson's data by explaining two levels of analysis: summary motion (al-haraka al-murakkaba) and detailed motion. The author clarifies that the approximate periods given are based on summary motion, which overlooks the intricate movements of the celestial spheres. The exact calculations are left to astronomical tables (al-zijat).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage.
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the definition of summary motion.
Note the words 'إجمالاً' and 'تفصيلاً'.
2
Understand the definition of summary motion.
The author defines it as the composite motion that considers a planet's transition from sign to sign without regarding the specific motions of the spheres.
This is the motion used for the approximate periods.
3
Note the role of astronomical tables.
The passage ends by stating that exact determinations are found in the zijat, implying the periods given are only approximate.
This humility acknowledges the limitations of simple models.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قطع الكواكب السيارة لمنطقة البروج. قال وكل من السيارة يقطع هذه البروج على التوالي، أعني بحركة من المغرب إلى المشرق. إما إجمالاً فالشمس في ثلثمائة وخمسة وستين يوماً وربع يوم وهي السنة الشمسية، والقمر في ثلثمائة وأربعة وخمسين يوماً وسدس يوم وخمس. وزحل في ثلاثين سنة. والمشتري في اثنتي عشرة سنة، والمريخ في سنتين إلا شهراً ونصفاً والزهرة في حدود سنة، وكذا عطارد كل ذلك على التقريب. أعني من الحمل إلى الثور إلى الجوزاء، وهكذا، وهي حركة من المغرب إلى المشرق وعكسه بخلاف التوالي أعني من الحمل إلى الحوت إلى الدلو، وهكذا، ونعني بالحركة إجمالاً الحركة المركبة التي بها يعتبر انتقال الكوكب من برج إلى برج من غير أن ينظر إلى جزئيات الأفلاك وتفاصيل الحركات. وبالتفصيل خلاف ذلك. فالشمس تقطع البروج الاثني عشر في ثلثمائة وخمسة وستين يوماً وربع يوم، وهي السنة الشمسية والقمر يقطعها اثنتي عشرة مرة في السنة القمرية، ثلثمائة وأربعة وخمسين يوماً وسدس يوم وخمس يوم وهي في وزحل في ثلاثين سنة، والمشتري في اثنتي عشرة سنة، والمريخ في سنتين إلا شهراً ونصفاً، وكل من الزهرة وعطارد في سنة ذلك على سبيل التقريب جميعاً، وأما التحقيق فيعرف من الزيجات.
The cutting of the wandering planets through the zodiacal region. He said: And each of the planets traverses these signs in order, that is, with a motion from west to east. In summary: the sun in 365 and a quarter days, which is the solar year; and the moon in 354 and one-sixth and one-fifth days; and Saturn in 30 years; and Jupiter in 12 years; and Mars in two years less a month and a half; and Venus in about a year, and likewise Mercury – all that approximately. That is, from Aries to Taurus to Gemini, and so on; and it is a motion from west to east, and its opposite is contrary to order, that is, from Aries to Pisces to Aquarius, and so on. And by motion in summary we mean the composite motion by which the planet's transition from sign to sign is considered, without looking at the particulars of the spheres and the details of motions. In detail it is otherwise. So the sun traverses the twelve signs in 365 and a quarter days, and that is the solar year; and the moon traverses them twelve times in the lunar year, 354 and one-sixth and one-fifth days; and Saturn in 30 years; and Jupiter in 12 years; and Mars in two years less a month and a half; and each of Venus and Mercury in one year – all that approximately. As for the exact determination, it is known from the astronomical tables (al-zijat).
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage explains the motion of the planets (including the sun and moon) through the twelve zodiac signs. The author first states that each planet traverses the signs in order (from west to east). He gives approximate periods: the sun takes 365.25 days (the solar year), the moon 354.166... days (about a lunar year), Saturn 30 years, Jupiter 12 years, Mars 2 years minus 1.5 months, and Venus and Mercury about a year. He clarifies that 'order' means from Aries to Taurus to Gemini, etc., which is west-to-east motion, while the opposite order (east-to-west) would be from Aries to Pisces to Aquarius. He then distinguishes between summary (ijmal) and detailed (tafsil) motion: summary motion is the composite motion that merely considers the transition from sign to sign, ignoring the complexities of individual spheres and their intricate movements. He repeats the approximate figures, adding that the moon traverses the signs twelve times in a lunar year. Finally, he notes that exact determinations are found in astronomical tables (zijat).

The Twenty-Four Partial Spheres and Simple Motions
Enumerates the twenty-four partial spheres and corresponding simple motions for each planet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical astronomers derived the twenty-four partial spheres and simple motions from observed planetary irregularities.

Core Idea
Planetary irregularities forced astronomers to assign multiple spheres per planet.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary explains the astronomical basis for the twenty-four partial spheres. The author shows how the apparent irregularities in planetary motion—speed changes, retrograde, stations—led astronomers to posit additional spheres beyond the daily and fixed-star spheres. The enumeration for each planet gives the total.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: a conclusion first, then the reasoning, then the detailed enumeration.
The passage is compact; take your time with each phrase.
2
Identify the assumptions
The text says the basis is 'belief in regularity in the heavens despite observation of many variations'. Reflect on why astronomers insisted on regularity: they assumed celestial motions must be uniform and circular. The variations forced them to add more spheres.
This is a key methodological point in Ptolemaic astronomy.
3
List spheres per planet
Go through each planet as mentioned: Sun, Moon, the superior planets and Venus, and Mercury. Write down the types of spheres assigned to each. Use the Arabic terms to reinforce vocabulary.
Count the spheres: Sun=3, Moon=4, each superior+Venus=3, Mercury=4. The total of spheres listed for planets is 23, but with the daily motion and fixed-star spheres the full count becomes 25. The text asserts 24; this slight discrepancy is a known point of debate.
4
Contemplate the total
The passage concludes that the partial spheres are twenty-four. Consider why this number matters: it is not arbitrary but a direct outcome of trying to explain observed motions with uniform circular motion.
You do not need to resolve the count issue; the main lesson is the method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فظهر أن جزئيات الأفلاك أربعة وعشرون، وكذا بسائط الحركات، ومبناه على اعتقاد الانتظام في السموات مع مشاهدة الكثير من الاختلافات مثل السرعة، والبطء بعد التوسط والرجعة والوقوف بعد الاستقامة. قوله فظهر عليه لما أثبتوا فلكاً للحركة اليومية، وآخر لحركة الثوابت، وتأملوا في أحوال السبعة السيارة واختلافاتها، فحكموا بأن للشمس ممثلاً، وخارج مركز وتدويراً، وللقمر مائلاً وممثلاً، وخارج مركز وتدويراً، ولكل من العلوية والزهرة ممثلاً، وخارج مركز وتدويراً، ولعطارد ممثلاً وتدويراً ومديراً وخارج مركز وتدويراً. ولكل من المذكورات حركة خاصة صارت الأفلاك الجزئية أربعة وعشرين وكذا الحركات البسيطة.
So it became apparent that the partial spheres are twenty-four, and likewise the simple motions. And its basis is the belief in regularity in the heavens despite the observation of many variations such as speed, slowness after moderation, retrograde motion, and stationary after direct motion. His statement: 'So it became apparent to him'—when they established a sphere for the daily motion and another for the motion of the fixed stars, and they contemplated the conditions of the seven wandering planets and their variations, they ruled that for the Sun there is a representative, an eccentric, and an epicycle; for the Moon an inclined, a representative, an eccentric, and an epicycle; for each of the superior [planets] and Venus a representative, an eccentric, and an epicycle; for Mercury a representative, an epicycle, a deferent, and an eccentric. And for each of these there is a particular motion, so the partial spheres became twenty-four, and likewise the simple motions.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents the conclusion that the partial celestial spheres are twenty-four in number, corresponding to the simple motions. The author explains the basis: astronomers (falāsifa) assumed regularity in heavenly motions despite apparent irregularities like speed variations, retrograde motion, and stationarity. To account for these, they posited additional spheres: daily motion sphere, fixed-star sphere, and for each planet a combination of eccentric, epicycle, etc. Specifically: Sun: representative, eccentric, epicycle; Moon: inclined, representative, eccentric, epicycle; Saturn, Jupiter, Mars (the 'superior') and Venus: each representative, eccentric, epicycle; Mercury: representative, epicycle, deferent, eccentric. Each has its own motion, totaling twenty-four partial spheres and simple motions.

Key Terms

بسائط الحركات
خارج المركز
تدوير
مدير
ممثل
مائل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The number 24 is a random arbitrary number chosen by astronomers.
Correction: The number 24 is derived from careful observation and deduction: each observed motion requires a separate sphere, and the total is a direct outcome of the spheres assigned to each planet and the two primary spheres.

The Principle of Uniformity in Celestial Motions
Sets up the principle of uniformity and introduces eccentrics/epicycles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why medieval astronomers introduced eccentrics and epicycles to preserve the principle of uniform circular motion while explaining apparent irregularities.

Core Idea
Apparent irregularities in the heavens were explained without abandoning uniformity.

This passage sets out the foundational assumption of medieval astronomy: celestial motions are perfectly uniform. When astronomers observed phenomena like changes in speed or retrograde motion, they did not abandon uniformity; instead, they introduced geometrical devices—eccentrics and epicycles—to account for the observations while preserving the core principle. Let us examine how and why they did so.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key assumption
Read the first part of the passage and note what astronomers believed about celestial motions: they are regular, with no stopping, retrograde, speed changes, or deviation.
This assumption was inherited from Aristotle and Ptolemy.
2
Observe the irregularities
The passage mentions that when astronomers perceived something contrary to uniformity, they sought a cause that would not violate the principle.
3
Recognize the devices
Two devices are named: the eccentric (khārij al-markaz) and the epicycle (tadwīr). The eccentric explains speed variation; the epicycle explains retrograde, direct, and stationary motion.
The passage focuses on the eccentric for speed, but the epicycle is also mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومبنى ذلك على أنهم اعتقدوا في الفلكيات أنها منتظمة في حركاتها، لا يعرض لها وقوف، أو رجوع، أو سرعة، أو بطء، أو انحراف عن سننها، فحين أدركوا شيئاً من ذلك، أثبتوا له سبباً لا يخل بهذا الانتظام كإثبات الخارج المركز أو التدوير للاختلاف بالسرعة والبطء وإثبات التدوير للرجعة والاستقامة والوقف مثلاً إذا كان الكوكب متحركاً حركة متشابهة على محیط فلك خارج مركزه عن مركز العالم الذي نحن بقربه، تكون حركته بالقياس إلى مركز العالم مختلفة، ويكون في القطعة التي هي أبعد منه بطيئة، وفي التي هي أقرب سريعة، لأن القسى المتساوية المختلفة بالبعد والقرب، يرى البعيد منها أصغر من القريب وتوضيحه : أنه إذا أخرج خطيمر بالمركزين، ويصل إلى الأوج والحضيض ، ثم قام عليه عمود يمر بمركز العالم، ويصل إلى المحيط من الجانبين، انقسم الممثل بهذا الخط قسمين متساويين وخارج المركز قسمين مختلفين، أعظمهما ما يكون في النصف الأوجي النصف الأوجي منه من الممثل، وأصغرهما ما يكون في النصف الحضيضي منه ، والكوكب لا يقطع كل نصف، إلا بقطع ما فيه من الخارج، فيكون زمان قطع النصف الأوجي أكثر ، والحضيضي أقل على تفاوت المسافتين. أعني القسمين من الخارج المركز ، فترى الحركة في النصف الأوجي أبطأ، وفي النصف الحضيضي. أسرع ، وعند طرفي الخط متوسطة.
And the basis of that is that they believed concerning celestial objects that they are regular in their motions, that no stopping, retrograde, speed, slowness, or deviation from their course occurs to them. So when they perceived something of that, they established a cause that does not violate this regularity, such as establishing the eccentric or the epicycle for the variation in speed and slowness, and establishing the epicycle for retrograde, direct, and stationary. For example, if a planet moves with a uniform motion on the circumference of a sphere whose center is offset from the center of the world which we are near, its motion relative to the center of the world is different. It will be slow in the part furthest from it and fast in the part closest, because equal arcs differing in distance and proximity cause the far one to appear smaller than the near one. Its clarification: when a line is drawn through the two centers, reaching the apogee and perigee, and then a perpendicular is erected passing through the center of the world and reaching the circumference on both sides, the representative [sphere] is divided by this line into two equal sections, and the eccentric into two unequal sections, the larger of which is in the apogee half of the representative, and the smaller in the perigee half. The planet does not cross each half except by crossing what is in the eccentric, so the time for crossing the apogee half is greater, and the perigee half less, according to the difference of the two distances—I mean the two sections of the eccentric. So you see the motion in the apogee half slower, and in the perigee half faster, and at the ends of the line it is moderate.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage explains the rationale behind eccentrics and epicycles in preserving the regularity of celestial motions. Astronomers observed apparent irregularities but assumed underlying uniformity. They posited eccentrics (khārij al-markaz) to explain variations in speed: a planet moving uniformly on an eccentric sphere appears to vary in speed from Earth's perspective because equal arcs at different distances subtend different angles. The author gives a geometric demonstration: drawing a line through both centers (of the world and of the eccentric) defines the apogee (a'la) and perigee (asfal). A perpendicular through the world's center divides the representative sphere into equal halves but the eccentric into unequal halves. The planet takes longer to traverse the larger (apogee) half of the eccentric, so its apparent motion is slower there. At the ends of the line, the motion is moderate. This shows how eccentrics account for speed differences without violating the principle of uniform circular motion.

Key Terms
خارج المركز (khārij al-markaz)
تدوير (tadwīr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that astronomers believed planets actually moved irregularly and just added epicycles arbitrarily.
Correction: The passage clarifies that astronomers firmly believed in uniformity and only added devices to explain apparent irregularities without violating that principle.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did astronomers believe about celestial motions?
A) They are irregular and unpredictable
B) They are perfectly regular with no stopping, retrograde, or speed changes
C) They are only regular for fixed stars
D) They believed retrograde motion was real

Key Takeaway
Medieval astronomers assumed uniform circular motion and introduced eccentrics and epicycles to explain apparent irregularities without abandoning that principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How an Eccentric Explains Variations in Speed
Geometric demonstration of how an eccentric produces apparent speed variation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the geometric reasoning by which an eccentric sphere produces apparent differences in speed from the Earth's perspective.

Core Idea
An off-center sphere creates slower and faster apparent motions through geometry alone.

In the previous lesson we learned that eccentrics were used to explain changes in speed. Now we dive into the detailed geometric demonstration: how a planet moving uniformly on an eccentric sphere appears to move slower when farther from Earth and faster when closer. This demonstration uses the apogee, perigee, and the division of the eccentric into unequal arcs.

Guided Path
1
Visualize the eccentric sphere
Imagine a sphere whose center is not at Earth's center. The planet moves uniformly on its circumference. Earth is at some point inside the sphere.
The offset center is the key.
2
Follow the geometric construction
The passage describes drawing a line through both centers (Earth and eccentric) to define the apogee (farthest) and perigee (nearest). Then a perpendicular is drawn through Earth's center to the circumference.
This divides the representative sphere into equal halves, but the eccentric into unequal halves.
3
Understand the time difference
The planet takes longer to cross the larger (apogee) half of the eccentric because it must traverse a longer arc while moving uniformly. Thus its apparent motion is slower in that half.
At the ends of the line (apogee and perigee), the motion appears moderate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومبنى ذلك على أنهم اعتقدوا في الفلكيات أنها منتظمة في حركاتها، لا يعرض لها وقوف، أو رجوع، أو سرعة، أو بطء، أو انحراف عن سننها، فحين أدركوا شيئاً من ذلك، أثبتوا له سبباً لا يخل بهذا الانتظام كإثبات الخارج المركز أو التدوير للاختلاف بالسرعة والبطء وإثبات التدوير للرجعة والاستقامة والوقف مثلاً إذا كان الكوكب متحركاً حركة متشابهة على محیط فلك خارج مركزه عن مركز العالم الذي نحن بقربه، تكون حركته بالقياس إلى مركز العالم مختلفة، ويكون في القطعة التي هي أبعد منه بطيئة، وفي التي هي أقرب سريعة، لأن القسى المتساوية المختلفة بالبعد والقرب، يرى البعيد منها أصغر من القريب وتوضيحه : أنه إذا أخرج خطيمر بالمركزين، ويصل إلى الأوج والحضيض ، ثم قام عليه عمود يمر بمركز العالم، ويصل إلى المحيط من الجانبين، انقسم الممثل بهذا الخط قسمين متساويين وخارج المركز قسمين مختلفين، أعظمهما ما يكون في النصف الأوجي النصف الأوجي منه من الممثل، وأصغرهما ما يكون في النصف الحضيضي منه ، والكوكب لا يقطع كل نصف، إلا بقطع ما فيه من الخارج، فيكون زمان قطع النصف الأوجي أكثر ، والحضيضي أقل على تفاوت المسافتين. أعني القسمين من الخارج المركز ، فترى الحركة في النصف الأوجي أبطأ، وفي النصف الحضيضي. أسرع ، وعند طرفي الخط متوسطة.

They posited eccentrics (khārij al-markaz) to explain variations in speed: a planet moving uniformly on an eccentric sphere appears to vary in speed from Earth's perspective because equal arcs at different distances subtend different angles. The author gives a geometric demonstration: drawing a line through both centers (of the world and of the eccentric) defines the apogee (a'la) and perigee (asfal). A perpendicular through the world's center divides the representative sphere into equal halves but the eccentric into unequal halves. The planet takes longer to traverse the larger (apogee) half of the eccentric, so its apparent motion is slower there. At the ends of the line, the motion is moderate. This shows how eccentrics account for speed differences without violating the principle of uniform circular motion.

Key Terms
خارج المركز (khārij al-markaz)
أوج (awj)
حضيض (ḥaḍīḍ)
ممثل (mumaththal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the eccentric itself causes the planet to actually speed up or slow down.
Correction: The planet's motion is uniform on the eccentric; the apparent speed variation arises solely from the geometry of viewing from an off-center point.

Checkpoint
According to the geometric demonstration, why does the planet take longer to cross the apogee half?
A) The planet actually moves slower there
B) The apogee half of the eccentric is an arc of larger length
C) The perpendicular line blocks the motion
D) The epicycle overrides the eccentric

Key Takeaway
The geometric demonstration shows that uniform motion on an eccentric sphere yields apparent speed variation without violating the principle of uniformity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that the same uniform motion appears slow when far and fast when near. In your spiritual life, what 'distance' from God might be causing you to perceive His work as slow or uneven? How can you adjust your perspective to trust that He is moving uniformly, even when it seems otherwise?
?
The geometric demonstration required a mental shift—seeing the underlying uniformity behind apparent variation. What is one area of your life where you need to 'draw a perpendicular'—step back and see the bigger picture—to recognize God's consistent wisdom behind your fluctuating circumstances?

Why Astronomical Proofs Are Conjectural
Taftazani critiques the astronomers' proofs as conjectural

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's argument that astronomical models are based on conjecture, not demonstration, because the observed effects could have multiple causes.

Core Idea
Observations alone do not prove a unique cause.

This lesson examines a pivotal critique from Taftazani. He challenges the astronomers' claim to have proven the existence of spheres and motions through observation. The fault lies in assuming that the observed differences must be caused by the proposed model, whereas they could be due to other unknown causes. Thus, the inference is conjectural at best.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice Taftazani's use of the terms 'hads' (conjecture) and 'istidlal' (deduction).
Pay attention to the phrase 'إلا إذا علم المساواة' — the key condition.
2
Understand the inference pattern
The passage describes a common reasoning: from the existence of a necessary concomitant (lazim) to the existence of the subject (malzum). This is valid only if the lazim is exclusive to that malzum.
This is the logical structure of proof through the necessary concomitant.
3
Identify the condition of equivalence
Taftazani states that for the inference to be sound, one must know 'al-musawah' — that the lazim is uniquely tied to that malzum. Without this, the proof fails.
Equivalence means that the effect cannot be produced by any other cause.
4
Apply the critique
The astronomers do not know this equivalence because there is no necessity or proof that the observed differences cannot be due to other causes. Therefore, their 'demonstrations' are mere conjecture.
This is a methodological point relevant to all empirical arguments in theology.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this critique affects the reliability of astronomical models and the broader kalam approach to empirical knowledge.
This is a key distinction between certain (burhani) and probable (dhanni) knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما ذكره عن طريق الحدس لا الاستدلال قال ( وأمثال هذه البيانات) لیست استدلالاً بوجود الملازم على الملزوم كما هو الظاهر بل تحدساً]. قد يتوهم أن إثبات الأفلاك الجزئية، والحركات البسيطة على الوجوه المخصوصة بناء على ما يشاهد ويدرك بالرصد من الاختلافات اللازمة على تقدير ثبوتها إثبات للملزوم، بناء على وجود لازمه ، وليس بمستقيم إلا إذا علم المساواة ، وليست بمعلومة، إذ لا ضرورة ولا برهان على امتناع أن تكون تلك الاختلافات لأسباب أخر.
What he mentioned is by way of conjecture, not deduction. He said: 'And such demonstrations are not deduction from the existence of the necessary concomitant to the subject, as is apparent, but rather conjecture.' It might be imagined that proving the partial spheres and simple motions in the particular ways based on what is observed and perceived by observation of the necessary differences on the assumption of their existence is a proof of the subject based on the existence of its necessary concomitant. But it is not sound unless equivalence is known, and it is not known, for there is no necessity or proof that those differences cannot be due to other causes.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage critiques the epistemological status of the astronomical models. The author (Taftazani) states that these 'demonstrations' are based on conjecture (hads), not true deduction (istidlal). The inference from observed phenomena to the proposed causes is not logically necessary because the same phenomena could result from different causes. For a valid proof that a specific cause (the subject) produced an effect (the necessary concomitant), one must know that there is no alternative cause (equivalence). Since that equivalence is not known, the proof is not demonstrative. This is a key methodological point within Islamic kalam: empirical observation does not yield certain knowledge of unobservable causes.

Key Terms
حدس
استدلال
ملازم
ملزوم
مساواة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Observational evidence alone constitutes a proof for the existence of unobserved entities.
Correction: Observation only proves the existence of the observed effect; inferring a unique cause requires knowledge that no other cause could produce that effect, which is often unknown.

Checkpoint
Why does Taftazani reject the astronomers' proofs as conjectural?
A) Because they rely on insufficient observational data.
B) Because they assume a unique cause for observed effects without proving equivalence.
C) Because the Ptolemaic models contradict revelation.
D) Because the astronomers contradicted each other.

Key Takeaway
Empirical inferences are only certain if the observed effect can be uniquely tied to one cause; otherwise, they remain conjectural.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a time when you were absolutely certain that you knew the cause of an event in your life—perhaps a success or failure. Now consider the possibility, even if only hypothetically, that your certainty was based on conjecture, not demonstration. Does this realization unsettle you? What would it mean for you to hold your conclusions with more intellectual humility?
?
Do you ever assume that your limited observations of a person's actions give you complete knowledge of their character? How does Taftazani's critique challenge you to be more cautious in your judgments about others, especially when you might be inferring motives or intentions?
?
This lesson teaches that empirical evidence alone does not guarantee a unique cause. In your own spiritual life, where might you be making the same logical error—assuming that because you see a certain outcome (e.g., hardship or ease), you know its exact cause (e.g., divine punishment or reward) without considering alternative explanations? What would it look like for you to trust Allah's wisdom while acknowledging your own limited knowledge?

Hads in Astronomical Proofs: Objection and Answer
Objection and answer concerning astronomical proofs and hads.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of hads (intuitive inference) in astronomical arguments and how the author defends its validity against a logical objection.

Core Idea
Astronomical proofs rely on intuitive inference (hads), not pure demonstrative reasoning.

This passage presents a common objection against using astronomical observations to infer celestial truths: that it is not a valid demonstrative inference (istidlal) but rather mere conjecture. The author answers by clarifying that such inferences are based on hads (intuitive inference), which is accepted as decisive by the intellect. We will explore both the objection and the response to understand the methodology of kalam in engaging with astronomy.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection that astronomical data only provide evidence of a concomitant (lazim), not the accompanied (malzum). Understand the logical structure at play.
Pay attention to the terms lazim, malzum, and musawah.
2
Understand the logical requirement
Why does the objector argue that equality (musawah) is needed? Without it, the inference is not demonstrative.
Equality here means a one-to-one correspondence between the observed effect and the assumed cause.
3
Read the answer: reclassification as hads
The author responds that these are hadsi premises: the intellect judges decisively without a middle term. This is a different kind of inference.
Hads is intuitive inference, not formal demonstration.
4
Examine the examples and implications
Note the examples: eclipses admit hads, while cases like the Milky Way show disagreement where hads fails.
This shows that astronomers themselves recognize the role of hads.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ( وأمثال هذه البيانات) ليست استدلالاً بوجود الملازم على الملزوم كما هو الظاهر بل تحدساً]. قد يتوهم أن إثبات الأفلاك الجزئية، والحركات البسيطة على الوجوه المخصوصة بناء على ما يشاهد ويدرك بالرصد من الاختلافات اللازمة على تقدير ثبوتها إثبات للملزوم، بناء على وجود لازمه ، وليس بمستقيم إلا إذا علم المساواة ، وليست بمعلومة، إذ لا ضرورة ولا برهان على امتناع أن تكون تلك الاختلافات لأسباب أخر. والجواب أنها مقدمات حدسية، حيث يحكم العقل الجازم بانتظام السماويات بثبوتها عند إدراك الاختلافات من غير ملاحظة وسط وترتيب، وهم معترفون بذلك مصرحون به في أمر الخسوف والكسوف ونحوهما ، ولهذا اختلفوا وترددوا فيما لم يحكم الحدس به كالمجرة ومحور القمر . وأن اختلاف الشمس بالسرعة والبطء مبني على التدوير أو الخارج المركز وإن حركات أوجات الممثلاث بأنفسها أو بالفلك الثامن .
He said: 'And such data are not a demonstrative inference (istidlāl) from the existence of the concomitant (lāzim) to the accompanied (malzūm) as appears, but rather intuitive inference (hads).' One might mistakenly think that establishing the particular spheres and the simple motions in specific ways based on what is observed and perceived by observation of the necessary differences, on the assumption of their establishment, is an establishment of the accompanied based on the existence of its concomitant. But it is not valid unless equality (musāwāh) is known, and it is not known, for there is no necessity (ḍarūrah) or proof (burhān) that those differences cannot be from other causes. And the answer: They are hadsī premises, for the decisive intellect judges the order of the celestial [bodies] by their establishment when perceiving the differences, without observing a middle term or arrangement. And they admit this and state it explicitly concerning lunar eclipses, solar eclipses, and the like. For this reason they differed and hesitated regarding that which hads did not judge, such as the Milky Way and the moon's axis. And that the sun's variation in speed and slowness is based on the epicycle or eccentric, and the motions of the apsides of the concentric spheres themselves or by the eighth sphere.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer about the use of hads (intuitive inference) in astronomical demonstrations. The author first states a view: that such data are not demonstrative (istidlal) based on the existence of a concomitant (lazim) to the accompanied (malzum), but rather are hads. Then he presents an objection: one might think that establishing the particular spheres and simple motions based on observed differences is establishing the malzum based on the existence of its lazim, but this is only valid if equality (musawa) is known, which is not known since there is no necessity or proof that these differences cannot arise from other causes. The answer is that these are hadsi premises, where the decisive intellect judges the order of the celestial bodies by their establishment when perceiving the differences, without observing a middle term or arrangement. The philosophers admit this and state it explicitly in matters like lunar and solar eclipses and the like. Thus they differ and hesitate in cases where hads does not judge, like the Milky Way and the moon's axis. And that the sun's variation in speed and slowness is based on the epicycle or eccentric, and the motions of the apsides themselves or the eighth sphere.

Key Terms
حدس (hads)
استدلال (istidlāl)
ملازم (lāzim)
ملزوم (malzūm)
مساواة (musāwāh)
برهان (burhān)
مقدمات حدسية (muqaddimāt hadsiyyah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Astronomical proofs are purely demonstrative (burhani) and based on strict logical inference.
Correction: They often rely on intuitive inference (hads), where the decisive intellect judges directly without a middle term.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what is the basis for the astronomical proofs discussed?
A) Demonstrative proof (burhān)
B) Intuitive inference (hads)
C) Scriptural authority (naql)
D) Pure speculation (ray')

Key Takeaway
Astronomical arguments rely on hads (intuitive inference) rather than strict demonstrative inference, yet are considered valid by decisive judgment of the intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your belief in the existence of God, is it based on a strict demonstration (burhan) or an immediate intuitive judgment (hads)? How does this passage challenge you to be honest about the epistemic basis of your most foundational beliefs?

The philosophers admitted that their astronomical conclusions were based on hads, not certainty. In your own life, have you ever mistaken a hads-based conviction for a proven certainty? What would it mean for you to humbly admit the limits of your reasoning?

The passage mentions that the intellect judges 'without observing a middle term.' Think of a time when you felt an immediate conviction about a religious truth. Was it truly free from prior assumptions? How do you test whether that conviction is valid?

The Moon's Phases: An Example of Hads
Author gives the moon's phases as a well-known example of hadsi reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phases of the moon serve as a concrete example of hads (intuitive inference) in Islamic philosophical reasoning.

Core Idea
The moon's phases are not merely observed but inferred through hads.

This passage presents a well-known example from the philosophers to illustrate hads (intuitive inference). By examining the changing shapes of the moon's light, we see how rational inference goes beyond mere observation to uncover the hidden cause: the moon's illumination by the sun. Let's explore this example step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the description of the moon's phases.
Notice the sequence of phases: conjunction, crescent, full moon, back to conjunction.
2
Identify the key inference
The passage states that the moon is dark in itself and illuminated by the sun. This is the core inference (hads) that explains all the observed phases.
Without this inference, the changing shapes would remain a mystery.
3
Connect to the concept of hads
Reflect on why this inference is considered hads rather than simple observation. The observer sees the shapes but infers the cause (sun illumination) through rational insight.
This example is categorized as 'well-known intuitives' (hadsīyāt mashhūra).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه [ قال (كما تحدسوا) في الحدسيات المشهورة عنده اختلاف التشكلات نور القمر على حسب اختلاف أوضاعه من الشمس من نفسه مظلم يستضيء بالشمس ، فعند الاجتماع يكون وجهه المظلم إلينا وهو المحاق، وإذا انحرف إلينا من جهة المضيء قدر ما يرى فهو الهلال، ثم يتزايد إلى أن يبلغ الاستقبال، فيصير وجهه المضيء كله إلينا وهو البدر، ثم ينحرف فيأخذ النور في النقصان إلى المحاق ]. أوردها هنا من الحدسيات المشهورة فيما بينهم إضاءة القمر بالشمس،
That he said: 'As they intuitively inferred (tahaddasū) concerning the well-known intuitives among him: the variation of the light-shapes of the moon according to the variation of its positions from the sun; it is in itself dark, illuminated by the sun. At conjunction (ijtimā‘), its dark face faces us, which is the new moon (maḥāq). When it deviates toward us from the illuminated side enough to be seen, it is the crescent (hilāl). Then it increases until it reaches opposition (istiqbāl), so its whole illuminated face faces us, which is the full moon (badr). Then it deviates, and the light begins to decrease until the new moon.' He mentioned here from the well-known intuitives among them: the illumination of the moon by the sun.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage provides an example of a well-known hadsiya (intuitive inference) among the philosophers: the illumination of the moon by the sun. The text describes the varying shapes of the moon's light according to its different positions relative to the sun. The moon is inherently dark and receives light from the sun. At conjunction (ijtima‘), its dark side faces us—this is the new moon (mahaq). When it deviates toward us from the illuminated side enough to be seen, it becomes the crescent (hilal). Then it increases until opposition (istiqbal), when its entire illuminated side faces us—the full moon (badr). Then it deviates, and the light decreases until the new moon again. The author mentions that this was presented among the well-known hadsiyat among them: the illumination of the moon by the sun.

بدر (badr)

Correction: The passage presents them as an intuitive inference (hads) that the moon is dark and illuminated by the sun, based on observed patterns.

Checkpoint

Astronomical Premises as Conjectural (Hadsiyyat)
The author defends the use of hads in astronomy with lunar illumination example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why astronomical premises are considered conjectural (hadsiyyat) and how the example of lunar illumination demonstrates this.

Core Idea
Conjectural premises are accepted by philosophers for astronomical reasoning.

In this passage, the author defends the use of conjectural premises in astronomy. He explains that the decisive intellect judges celestial regularities by perceiving differences without a formal syllogistic arrangement, and even the philosophers acknowledge this. The lunar phases serve as a clear example of such conjectural knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author defines conjectural premises and then gives the lunar example.
Pay attention to the quote about lunar phases; it is a well-known conjectural matter.
2
Identify the definition of hadsiyyat
The author states that conjectural premises are judgments by the decisive intellect about the regularity of celestial phenomena based on perceiving differences, without a middle term or arrangement.
This definition highlights the direct intuitive grasp, not a syllogism.
3
See how the philosophers use them
The philosophers admit using hads for eclipses, but hesitate for phenomena like the Milky Way. This shows the scope and limitation of conjectural premises.
The example of lunar illumination is given as a well-known conjectural matter.
4
Analyze the lunar example
The moon is dark in itself and illuminated by the sun. Its phases (new moon, crescent, full moon) are explained by its position relative to the sun. This is a classic hadsiyyat.
The example is used to defend the method against objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب أنها مقدمات حدسية، حيث يحكم العقل الجازم بانتظام السماويات بثبوتها عند إدراك الاختلافات من غير ملاحظة وسط وترتيب، وهم معترفون بذلك مصرحون به في أمر الخسوف والكسوف ونحوهما، ولهذا اختلفوا وترددوا فيما لم يحكم الحدس به كالمجرة ومحور القمر. وأن اختلاف الشمس بالسرعة والبطء مبني على التدوير أو الخارج المركز وإن حركات أوجات الممثلاث بأنفسها أو بالفلك الثامن. أنه قال كما تحدسوا في الحدسيات المشهورة عنده اختلاف التشكلات نور القمر على حسب اختلاف أوضاعه من الشمس من نفسه مظلم يستضيء بالشمس، فعند الاجتماع يكون وجهه المظلم إلينا وهو المحاق، وإذا انحرف إلينا من جهة المضيء قدر ما يرى فهو الهلال، ثم يتزايد إلى أن يبلغ الاستقبال، فيصير وجهه المضيء كله إلينا وهو البدر، ثم ينحرف فيأخذ النور في النقصان إلى المحاق. أوردها هنا من الحدسيات المشهورة فيما بينهم إضاءة القمر بالشمس
And the answer is that they are conjectural premises, since the decisive intellect judges the regularity of celestial phenomena by their existence upon perceiving differences without observing a middle term and arrangement, and they (the philosophers) acknowledge this and declare it in the matter of lunar and solar eclipses and the like. For this reason they differed and hesitated concerning what conjecture did not judge, such as the Milky Way and the axis of the moon. And that the variation of the sun in speed and slowness is based on epicycles or eccentric spheres, and that the motions of the apsides of the similar spheres are by themselves or by the eighth sphere. That he said: 'As you conjecture in the well-known conjectural matters according to him: the variation of the formations of the moon's light according to the variation of its positions relative to the sun; it is dark in itself and is illuminated by the sun. At conjunction its dark face faces us, which is the new moon; when it deflects toward us from the illuminated side by an amount that is seen, it is the crescent; then it increases until it reaches opposition, so its entire illuminated face faces us, which is the full moon; then it deflects and the light decreases until the new moon.' He mentioned here among the well-known conjectural matters among them the illumination of the moon by the sun.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection. The author clarifies that the premises used to argue for certain astronomical conclusions are conjectural (hadsiyyat). He explains that the decisive intellect rules on the regularity of celestial phenomena by apprehending differences without explicit syllogistic arrangement, and the philosophers themselves admit this. They use hads for phenomena like eclipses but differ on matters not grasped by hads, like the Milky Way. He then quotes the original author's statement about lunar phases as an example of such conjectural knowledge: the moon is dark and illuminated by the sun, and its phases are explained by its position relative to the sun. The author concludes that this example is among the well-known conjectural matters.

المحاق

Correction: The author shows they are accepted by the philosophers and necessary for astronomical reasoning, as illustrated by the lunar phase example.

Checkpoint

A) They are certain and demonstrative.

Key Takeaway
Conjectural premises are a legitimate form of reasoning in astronomy, as shown by the accepted explanation of lunar phases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Imagined Horizon Circle
Sanusi defines the horizon circle and its three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the horizon circle in Islamic astronomy and its three types relative to the celestial equator.

Core Idea
The horizon is not a physical line but an imagined circle dividing the sky into visible and hidden.

In this passage, the author explains the first of the imagined circles: the horizon. He defines it as the circle separating what is visible from what is hidden for a given location. He then describes three cases: when the horizon's poles align with the celestial poles, it coincides with the equator; otherwise, it intersects at right angles (right sphere) or non-right angles (inclined horizon). These distinctions are essential for understanding celestial observations and their applications in determining the qibla and day length.

Guided Path
1
Identify the horizon circle
Read the definition of the horizon as an imagined circle separating visible and hidden parts of the sky. Note its poles: zenith and nadir.
2
Understand the three types
Focus on the conditional statement: if poles are celestial poles, horizon coincides with equator; otherwise, it cuts at right angles (right sphere) or non-right angles (inclined).
These correspond to different latitudes on Earth.
3
Reflect on the name 'imagined'
The author emphasizes these are imagined (متوهمة) circles, not physical. Consider why this is important for theoretical astronomy.

قال (المبحث الرابع) المبحث الرابع في الدوائر المتوهمة» توهموا لكل موضع من الأرض دائرة على الفلك، فاصلة بين الظاهر منه والخفي، سموها دائرة الأفق، وقطباها سمت الرأس والقدم. فإن كانا قطبي العالم أفق انطبق الأفق على المعدل، وإلا كان مقاطعاً له، إما على زوايا قوائم، ويسمى الاستواء، أو غير قوائم، ويسمى الأفق الماثل، وأخرى مارة بسمتي الرأس والقدم وقطبي العالم سموها دائرة نصف النهار، وقطباها نقطتا المشرق والمغرب]. هذه دوائر توهموها بملاحظة السفليات ينتفع بها في استخراج القبلة واختلاف البلاد في طول النهار وقصره، وغير ذلك من الخواص، فمنها دائرة الأفق الفاصلة وهي الفلك والخفي منه فإن اعتبرت بالنسبة إلى مركز بين الظاهر من الأرض فأفق حقيقي، والدائرة عظيمة، أو إلى وجه الأرض، فأفق حسي، والدائرة قريبة من العظيمة وهما متوازيان وقطباهما سمت الرأس وسمت القدم. أعني طرفي خط يم على استقامة قامة الشخص بمركز الأرض، وينفذ في الجهتين إلى محيط الكل، والظاهر بالأفق الحسي أقل من الخفي بقدر ما يقتضيه نصف قطر الأرض، وإنما يحس بالتفاوت في فلك الشمس، وما دونها. إذ ليس للأرض بالقياس إلى ما فوقها قدر محسوس، والدوائر الصغار الموازية للأفق فوق الأرض تسمى مقنطرات الارتفاع، وتحتها مقنطرات الانحطاط، فإن كان قطبا الأفق،
He said (the fourth investigation): The fourth investigation on the imagined circles. They imagined for every place on earth a circle on the sphere, separating between what is visible and what is hidden from it, they called it the horizon circle, and its poles are the zenith and nadir. If they are the poles of the world, the horizon coincides with the equator; otherwise it intersects it, either at right angles, and it is called the right sphere, or at non-right angles, and it is called the inclined horizon. And another passing through the zenith and nadir and the poles of the world, they called it the meridian circle, and its poles are the east and west points. These are circles they imagined by observing the lower (bodies); they are useful for deriving the qibla and the variation of countries in the length and shortness of the day, and other properties. Among them is the separating horizon circle, which is the sphere and what is hidden from it. If it is considered relative to the center between what is apparent from the earth, it is a true horizon, and the circle is great; or relative to the surface of the earth, it is a sensory horizon, and the circle is close to great, and they are parallel, and their poles are the zenith and nadir. I mean the two ends of a line standing upright from the stature of the person to the center of the earth, and penetrating in both directions to the circumference of the whole. And what is apparent by the sensory horizon is less than what is hidden by the amount required by the radius of the earth. And the difference is only perceived in the sphere of the sun and what is below it, for the earth relative to what is above it has no perceptible magnitude. And the small circles parallel to the horizon above the earth are called the small circles of altitude, and below them the small circles of declination. If the poles of the horizon...
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of imaginary celestial circles used in astronomy. It begins by introducing the 'fourth investigation' on imagined circles (الدوائر المتوهمة). The author explains that for every location on earth, a circle on the celestial sphere is imagined that separates the visible part from the invisible; this is the horizon (الأفق), with poles at the zenith (سمت الرأس) and nadir (سمت القدم). If these poles coincide with the celestial poles, the horizon aligns with the celestial equator (المعدل); otherwise it cuts it at right angles (called the right sphere or الاستواء) or at non-right angles (called the inclined horizon or الأفق الماثل). Another circle passes through the zenith, nadir, and the celestial poles; this is the meridian (دائرة نصف النهار), with poles at the east and west points. These circles are imagined by observing the lower bodies (السفليات) and are useful for determining the qibla direction and differences in day length. The author then distinguishes between the true horizon (أفق حقيقي) and the sensory horizon (أفق حسي). The true horizon is a great circle with its center at the center of the earth; the sensory horizon is based on the observer's position on the earth's surface and is slightly smaller. The two are parallel. The sensory horizon conceals a portion of the sky equal to the earth's radius, but this is only perceptible for celestial bodies within the moon's sphere (the sublunar region). For distant fixed stars, the earth's radius is negligible. Finally, small circles parallel to the horizon above the earth are called small circles of altitude (مقنطرات الارتفاع) and below them small circles of declination (مقنطرات الانحطاط). The passage is cut off at 'if the poles of the horizon...'

Key Terms

أفق
سمت الرأس والقدم
معدل
استواء
أفق ماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The horizon is a physical line on the sky.
Correction: The author emphasizes it is an imagined (متوهمة) circle, a mental construct for practical purposes.

Checkpoint
What happens if the horizon's poles (zenith and nadir) are the same as the celestial poles?
A) The horizon becomes invisible.
B) The horizon coincides with the celestial equator.
C) The horizon cuts the equator at right angles.
D) The horizon becomes inclined.

Key Takeaway
The horizon is an imagined circle whose type—coinciding with the equator, right sphere, or inclined—depends on the location's latitude.

The Meridian and Types of Horizon
Sanusi defines the meridian, distinguishes true and sensory horizons, and mentions small circles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meridian circle and the distinction between the true and sensory horizon, along with the small circles of altitude and declination.

Core Idea
The meridian runs through zenith, nadir, and celestial poles; the true horizon is a great circle, while the sensory horizon is slightly smaller.

After discussing the horizon, the author introduces the meridian circle, which passes through the zenith, nadir, and celestial poles, with poles at east and west. He then elaborates on the horizon, distinguishing between the true horizon (a great circle centered at Earth's center) and the sensory horizon (based on the observer's surface). Finally, he mentions small circles parallel to the horizon: altitude circles above and declination circles below. These concepts are crucial for astronomical calculations.

Guided Path
1
Define the meridian
Find the definition of the meridian circle and its poles. Understand its orientation relative to the horizon and celestial poles.
2
Distinguish true vs sensory horizon
Note the difference: true horizon is a great circle from Earth's center, while sensory is from the observer's surface, slightly smaller and parallel.
The difference is only perceptible for nearby celestial bodies like the sun.
3
Introduce the small circles
Recognize the terms for small circles parallel to the horizon: altitude circles above and declination circles below.
These are used to measure celestial positions.
