Determining Latitude and Longitude
Describes the method for determining latitude and longitude of a location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars determined the latitude and longitude of a location using equinox observations and reference meridians.

Core Idea
Latitude from sun altitude at equinox; longitude from time difference to western edge.

Classical astronomers needed precise methods to locate cities. This passage from al-Maqasid explains two key techniques: finding latitude from the sun's noon altitude on an equinox, and finding longitude by comparing local noon to a prime meridian in the west. The text also highlights different opinions on the prime meridian's location.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the two main procedures described.
The passage is short but dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the latitude method
Identify the steps: measure the noon sun altitude on an equinox, subtract from 90 degrees, the remainder is the latitude. Ask yourself why an equinox day is chosen.
On equinox, the sun is directly over the equator, so altitude = 90° - latitude.
3
Understand the longitude method and the prime meridian debate
Longitude is defined as the arc (time difference) between local noon and noon at the westernmost inhabited region. Note the two opinions: some say the coast of the western sea, others (Ptolemy) say the Canary Islands. The distance between them is 10 degrees (≈220 parasangs).
The Greeks used a western prime meridian because it was closer to them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخذنا ارتفاع الشمس في نصف النهار يوم الاعتدال الربيعي أو الخريفي وألقيناه من تسعين كان الباقي عرض البلد، وأما طول البلد فهو قوس من النهار ما بين نصف نهار البلد، ونصف نهار آخر العمارة في المغرب. واعتبر اليونانيون من المغرب، لكونه أقرب نهايتي العمارة إليهم، وآخر العمارة عند بعضهم ساحل البحر الغربي، وعند بطليموس الجزائر الخالدات الواغلة في البحر، وبينهما عشر درجات وهي قريبة من مائتين وعشرين فرسخاً.
We take the altitude of the sun at noon on the day of the spring or autumn equinox and subtract it from ninety; the remainder is the latitude of the city. As for the longitude of the city, it is an arc of the day between the noon of the city and the noon of the farthest inhabited [region] in the west. The Greeks considered [the starting point] from the west, because it is the nearer of the two ends of habitation to them. The farthest habitation according to some is the coast of the western sea, and according to Ptolemy it is the Eternal Islands penetrating into the sea, and between them are ten degrees, which are close to two hundred and twenty parasangs.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage explains how to determine the latitude and longitude of a location. The author begins by describing the method for finding latitude: on the day of the spring or autumn equinox, when day and night are equal, measure the altitude of the sun at noon, subtract it from 90 degrees, and the remainder is the latitude. Next, he explains that longitude is the difference in time (as an arc) between local noon and noon at the westernmost inhabited point. The Greeks used the western edge of the inhabited world as the prime meridian because it was closest to them. Some define that western edge as the coast of the western sea, while Ptolemy defines it as the Eternal Islands (Canary Islands) further into the ocean. The distance between these two references is ten degrees, about 220 parasangs.

الاعتدال الربيعي
الاعتدال الخريفي
بطليموس
الجزائر الخالدات
فرسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Latitude can be measured directly from the sun at any time of year.
Correction: Latitude is best measured on an equinox because the sun is over the equator, making the calculation simple: altitude subtracted from 90°.

Checkpoint
How is the latitude of a city determined according to the passage?
A) By measuring the length of the longest day.
B) By subtracting the noon sun altitude on an equinox from 90 degrees.
C) By comparing with the longitude of the Canary Islands.
D) By using the difference between local noon and noon at the western edge.

Key Takeaway
Classical latitude uses equinox sun altitude; longitude uses time difference to a western prime meridian, with differing opinions on its exact location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Greeks chose their prime meridian based on proximity to themselves. Are you choosing your sources of truth based on convenience and nearness, or on objective authority?
?
This method of finding latitude requires precise measurement at a precise moment. Where in your life do you need to be more precise in your acts of worship or seeking knowledge?
?
Different scholars disagreed on the exact location of the western edge. How do you handle disagreements among reliable scholars in matters of faith? Do you follow without thought, or do you seek to understand the basis of each view?

The Empirical Basis for the Four Elements
The author reports al-Taftazani's argument that the four elements are empirically known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the four elements are known through empirical observation, not rational deduction.

Core Idea
Elements are discovered, not deduced.

In this passage, the author critiques the attempt to derive the four elements through pure reason. He reports that Imam al-Taftazani declared such rational division impossible. Instead, the actual basis is empirical: composition and analysis of physical bodies reveal these four as fundamental. Let's read the passage to see this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the author's reference to al-Taftazani's discussion and the phrase 'rational division'.
Pay attention to the contrast between rational division and empirical investigation.
2
Identify the claim about rational division
The author says enumerating elements by rational division is impossible. Notice the term 'تقسيم عقلي' and why it fails.
Rational division cannot guarantee an exhaustive list.
3
Understand the empirical alternative
The author then gives the empirical method: composition (تركيب) and analysis (تحليل) show that bodies are built from and break down into these four. This is the basis for the doctrine.
Empirical observation is the source of knowledge here.
4
Reflect on the methodological point
This passage highlights a key methodological principle: some truths about the physical world are a posteriori, not a priori. The four elements are not deduced from first principles but discovered through investigation.
This aligns with the empirical turn in later Islamic thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في ذلك كلام طويل أورده الإمام في المباحث مع جمل من الاعتراضات عليه. ثم قال : والحق أن من حاول بيان الحصر للعناصر بتقسيم عقلي، فقد حاول ما لا يمكنه الوفاء به. نعم الناس لما بحثوا بطريق التركيب والتحليل، وجدوا تركيب الكائنات مبتدأ من هذه الأربعة، وتحليلها منتهياً إليها. فلاجرم زعموا أن العناصر هي هذه الأربعة.
On that, the Imam (al-Taftazani) has a long discussion in al-Mabahith along with numerous objections against it. Then he said: The truth is that whoever attempts to enumerate the elements by means of rational division has attempted something he cannot fulfill. Indeed, when people investigated by way of composition and analysis, they found the composition of beings beginning from these four, and their analysis ending at them. Hence, they claimed that the elements are these four.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a methodological argument about the basis for the doctrine of four elements. The author reports that the Imam (referring to al-Taftazani in al-Mabahith) addressed this topic at length, including objections. Then the Imam states a key principle: it is impossible to arrive at an exhaustive list of the elements through purely rational division (taqsīm 'aqlī). Rather, the empirical methods of composition (tarkīb) and analysis (taḥlīl) reveal that all physical bodies are composed from four basic constituents and decompose back into them. Therefore, the claim that there are four elements is grounded in observation, not a priori reasoning. The author emphasizes that rational division cannot guarantee completeness, so the basis is a posteriori.

Correction: The text asserts they are known by empirical observation of composition and analysis, not by rational division.

Checkpoint

A) Rational division
B) Empirical composition and analysis
C) Revelation
D) Consensus of philosophers

Key Takeaway
The four elements are empirically derived from composition and analysis, not from rational division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief purely because it seemed logically necessary, without checking it against the actual evidence from revelation or observation? What would it look like to 'investigate by composition and analysis' in that area of your faith?
?
The passage shows that claiming knowledge without evidence is an impossible task. Right now, is there a conviction you hold that you have not personally verified? Will you commit to examining it today using the sources and reflection, rather than relying on blind acceptance?
?
The author emphasizes that discovery comes from breaking things down and building them up. What spiritual concept—such as tawhid, worship, or sincerity—could you 'analyze' and 'compose' in your life to gain a deeper, lived understanding rather than a theoretical one?

The Layers of Fire, Air, Water, and Earth
Describes the layers of fire, air, water, and earth in cosmology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical kalam cosmological model of the layers of fire, air, water, and earth, including the spherical nature of fire and the four sublayers of air.

Core Idea
The elements are arranged in layers: fire and air are spherical, with air having four distinct sublayers.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents a detailed cosmological description of the layers of the four classical elements: fire, air, water, and earth. The author explains the unique properties of each layer, such as fire's ability to transform adjacent matter and the spherical shape of fire and air. Understanding this model is important for grasping the framework within which later theological arguments about the createdness of the world were made.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for orientation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get a sense of the overall description. Notice the enumerative style and the technical terms for layers.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the main structure is complete.
2
Identify the layers of each element
From the passage, list the layers for air (four), water (one), and earth (three). Note that fire is described as having a single layer with a spherical shape.
Air's layers: smoky, pure, extremely cold, heated by reflection.
3
Understand the special properties of fire and air
Fire is extremely transformative (إحالة) and spherical. Air is spherical in convexity but has no concavity; its parts are easily separated due to moisture.
The passage also mentions an argument and objection about air having natural places.
4
Consider the alternative view on fire
The passage notes that some scholars consider fire to be air heated by the motion of the celestial sphere. In that view, fire would not be perfectly spherical near the pole and equator.
This shows that the author engages with differing opinions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (المبحث الثالث) المبحث الثالث في النار وعوامل توهجها. النار طبقة واحدة شديدة القوة على الإحالة صحيحة الاستدارة بسطحها إلا من يجعلها هواء يتسخن بحركة الفلك متحركة بالتبعية، لما يشاهد من حركات الشهب، وذوات الأذناب على نهج حركة الفلك، ولا كذلك حال الهواء مع النار لسهولة انفصاله برطوبته، ولعدم بقاء أجزائه على أوضاعها، وقد يحتج بأن لكل جزء منها جزءاً من الفلك بمنزلة المكان الطبيعي . . ويعترض بأن ذلك مع تشابه أجزائها، وكذا أجزاء الفلك غير معقول والهواء صحيح الاستدارة محدبه، لا مقعرة له أربع طبقات الدخانية وتحتها الصرفة ثم الزمهريرية الشديدة البرودة بمجاورة الأبخرة، ثم المتسخنة بانعكاس الأشعة والماء طبقة واحدة، والأرض ثلاث طبقات المنكشفة للأشعة، ثم الطينية، ثم الصرفة. لما كانت النار شديدة الإحالة لما جاورها إلى جوهرها لقوة كيفية الحرارة النارية وشدتها كانت لها طبقة واحدة وهي صحيحة الاستدارة بمحدبها ومقعرها، لبقائها على مقتضى طبعها إلا عند من يجعل النار عبارة عن هواء مسخن بحركة الفلك فلا محالة ترقى في الموضع القريب من القطب لبطء الحركة، وتغلظ فيما يلي المنطقة لسرعتها، فلا يكون مقعر النار صحيح الاستدارة، ثم لا
He said (the third section): The third section is about fire and the factors of its ignition. Fire is a single layer, extremely powerful in transforming [things] to its essence, spherical in its surface except for those who make it air heated by the motion of the sphere, moving by following [that motion], as seen in the movements of meteors and comets in the manner of the sphere's motion. But the case of air with fire is not like that due to the ease of its separation by its moisture, and because its parts do not remain in their positions. It may be argued that each part of it has a part of the sphere as a natural place... And it is objected that that, given the similarity of its parts and likewise the parts of the sphere, is unintelligible. Air is spherical in its convexity, having no concavity. It has four layers: the smoky, then below it the pure, then the extremely cold (zamharīrī) due to proximity to vapors, then the heated by reflection of rays. Water is one layer. Earth is three layers: the exposed to rays, then the clayey, then the pure. Since fire is extremely transformative of what is adjacent to it into its substance due to the strength of the quality of fire's heat and its intensity, it has a single layer, and it is spherical in its convexity and concavity, because it remains according to the requirement of its nature, except for those who make fire [be] air heated by the motion of the sphere, for then it necessarily rises in the place near the pole due to slowness of motion, and thickens in what is adjacent to the equator due to its speed, so the concavity of the fire would not be spherical. Then not...
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a cosmological description of the layers of the four elements: fire, air, water, and earth. It begins by stating that the third section discusses fire and its ignition. Fire is described as a single layer, extremely powerful in transforming adjacent matter into its own essence (إحالة). It is spherical in its surface, but some hold that fire is actually air heated by the motion of the celestial sphere (الفلك). Fire moves by following the sphere's motion, as evidenced by meteors and comets moving in the direction of the sphere. Air behaves differently from fire because it easily separates due to its moisture and its parts do not remain in place. An argument is presented that each part of air has a part of the sphere as its natural place, but this is objected to on grounds that it is unintelligible given the similarity of parts. Air is spherical in its convexity (محدبه) but has no concavity; it has four layers: the smoky (الدخانية), then the pure (الصرفة) below it, then the extremely cold (الزمهريرية) due to proximity to vapors, then the heated layer by reflection of rays (المتسخنة بانعكاس الأشعة). Water is a single layer, and earth has three layers: the exposed to rays, then the clayey, then the pure. The text then reiterates that fire's intense heat gives it a single layer that is spherical in both convexity and concavity, as it remains according to its nature. However, for those who define fire as heated air, it would rise near the pole and thicken near the equator, making the concavity not perfectly spherical. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing in the next page.

Critique of al-Iji's Reduction of Elemental Layers
Author criticizes al-Iji's reduction of elemental layers from nine to seven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's critique of al-Iji's seven-layer model of the elements and why it is considered inconsistent.

Core Idea
Al-Iji's seven-layer model fails because it misidentifies the freezing layer as pure air.

In this passage, the author first outlines his own understanding of how the elemental layers form, totaling nine layers. He then presents the alternative view of ‘Adud al-Din al-Iji, who reduces the layers to seven by omitting water and reclassifying air. The author's critique exposes an internal inconsistency in al-Iji's model, particularly regarding the nature of the freezing layer (zamharir). This lesson will help you follow the logic of the argument and see how careful reasoning is applied to cosmological models.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal English translation. Notice the flow from the author's own model to the critique.
The Arabic may have OCR errors; rely on the translation for clarity.
2
Identify the author's nine-layer model
The passage begins by describing how the layers form: the sea dries up, leaving some water, then a muddy layer, then a pure layer near the center, totaling nine. Pause to list these layers mentally.
The author does not enumerate all nine explicitly, but the context implies them.
3
Understand al-Iji's seven-layer model
Al-Iji drops water as a spherical layer and makes air three: upper (mixed with fire), middle (zamharir, which he calls pure air), and lower (vaporous mixed with air and water). Identify this structure.
Al-Iji's justification is that water does not remain spherical and encompassing.
4
Grasp the author's critique
The author argues that the layer beneath the highest air, being far from earth and water, cannot be freezing (zamharir), and moreover zamharir is not pure air. This shows a logical contradiction in al-Iji's model.
The critique relies on the physical properties of coldness and purity.
5
Reflect on the methodological point
Consider why the author considers this error significant: it demonstrates that cosmological models must be internally consistent and grounded in observable characteristics.

وتجففت بحر الشمس والكواكب، وبقي بعضها تحت الماء، والثانية الطينية الممتزجة من الماء والتراب . والثالثة الصرفة القريبة من المركز، فتكون طبقات العناصر تسعاً، وجعلها صاحب المواقف (۱) سبعاً لأنه أسقط الماء لعدم بقائه على الكرية والإحاطة عن الطبقات وجعل الهواء ثلاثاً أعلاها المخلوطة من النارية والهوائية، وتحتها الزمهريرية وفسرها بالهواء الصرف، وتحتها البخارية المخلوطة من الهوائية (۲) والمائية. ولا أدري كيف خفى عليه أن ما تحت الأعلى مع بعده عن مجاورة الأرض والماء لا يكون زمهريراً وإن الزمهرير لا يكون هواء صرفاً .
And the sea of the sun and planets dried up, and some of it remained under water. The second [layer] is the muddy one mixed from water and earth. And the third is the pure one near the center. So the layers of the elements become nine. The author of al-Mawaqif made them seven because he dropped water for its not remaining spherical and encompassing among the layers, and he made air three: its highest mixed from fire and air, beneath it the freezing one he interpreted as pure air, and beneath it the vaporous mixed from air and water. I do not know how it was hidden from him that what is beneath the highest, with its distance from proximity to earth and water, is not freezing, and that the freezing is not pure air.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument about the number of elemental layers in the cosmos. The author first describes how the layers form: after the seas dry up, some remain under water, then a muddy layer from water and earth, then a pure layer near the center, totaling nine layers. He then criticizes the view of ‘Adud al-Din al-Iji (the author of al-Mawaqif), who reduced the layers to seven by omitting water because it does not remain in a spherical shape and by treating air as three layers: an upper mixed layer of fire and air, a middle freezing layer (zamharir) which he calls pure air, and a lower vaporous layer mixed from air and water. The author objects that al-Iji is mistaken: the layer beneath the highest, being far from earth and water, cannot be freezing (zamharir), and furthermore, zamharir itself is not pure air. The critique follows al-Iji's own logic and exposes an inconsistency.

Proof of Earth's Sphericity and Centrality with Objection
Proof of Earth's sphericity and centrality with objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the astronomical arguments used by classical scholars to prove the Earth's sphericity and centrality, and the logical objection to such empirical reasoning.

Core Idea
Astronomical observations of rising times and pole heights reveal the Earth's convexity.

The author presents a detailed astronomical proof for the Earth being round and central, based on variations in stellar risings and pole elevations. He then addresses an objection that such observations do not guarantee a unique cause. This lesson will walk you through the proof and the logical challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two main arguments
The author provides two astronomical arguments: one based on longitudinal (east-west) differences in rising times, and another based on latitudinal (north-south) changes in pole height. Notice how each eliminates flatness and concavity.
These are empirical proofs.
3
Understand the objection
The objection states that observing a correlation does not prove a specific cause; alternative explanations could exist. The author concedes the argument is based on conjecture, not certainty.
This reflects a methodological awareness.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how this passage shows that classical Muslim scholars used rational and empirical reasoning alongside revelation in cosmology.
Contrast with modern misconceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأرض في الوسط، بأنها لو لم تكن كذلك، لزم أن يرى الكوكب في بعض البقاع أصغر لبعده عن السماء، وفي البعض أكبر لقربه منها والواقع بخلافه، ومثل استدلالهم على كرية الأرض، بأنه لو كان امتدادها الطولي، أعني ما بين المشرق والمغرب على استقامة لكان طلوع الكواكب على سكانها، وكذا غروبها عنهم في آن واحد أو على تقعير لكان الطلوع على المغربين قبله على المشرقين في مساكن متفقة العرض، وكذا الغروب فيها، وليس كذلك، بل الطلوع والغروب للمشرقيين قبلهما للمغربيين بحكم ارصاد الحوادث الفلكية من الخسوفات القمرية وغيرها. فأن أوساطها إنما تتفق في آن واحد لا محالة، وهي مختلفة بالنسبة إلى أول الليل حتى لو كانت للمغربي بعد مضي ساعتين كانت للمشرقي بعد مضي ثلاث ساعات إن كان ما بين نصفي نهاريهما خمس عشرة درجة، وبين مسكنيهما المتفقي العرض ألف ميل وعلى هذا النسق يتعين التحديب، ولو كان الامتداد العرضي. أعني ما بين الجنوب والشمال على استقامة، لبقي ارتفاع أحد القطبين، وانحطاط الآخر على حاله بالنسبة إلى السائر كم سارا وعلى تقعير لانتقص ارتفاع القطب الظاهر، وانحطاط الآخر بالنسبة إلى السائر إلى جهة القطب الظاهر وبالعكس للسائر إلى جهة القطب الخفي، والوجود بخلاف ذلك، إذ يزداد ارتفاع القطب الشمالي، وانحطاط الجنوبي للواغلين في الشمال، وبالعكس للواغلين في الجنوب بحسب وغولهما، فتعين التحديب في هذين الامتدادين، وكذا في سائر الامتدادات التي في سموت بين السمتين لتركب الاختلافين حسب ما يقتضيه التحديب دون الاستقامة أو التقعير. وإذ ثبت استدارة القدر المكشوف حدس منه أن الباقي كذلك. واعترض بأنه يجوز أن يكون وجود الأمور المذكورة على النهج المذكور مبنياً على سبب آخر غير الاستدارة والتوسط، وحاصله أن ما ذكرتم استدلال بوجود المسبب على وجود سبب معين، ولا يتم إلا إذا بين انتفاء سبب آخر. ولو سلّم. فما ذكر لا يفيد إلا الاستدارة والتوسط بحسب الحس دون الحقيقة، ولا محيص إلا بالرجوع إلى أن ذلك حدس كما في استضاءة القمر بالشمس.
The Earth is in the center, because if it were not so, it would be necessary that a planet be seen in some regions smaller due to its distance from the sky, and in others larger due to its proximity to it, but the reality is contrary to that. And likewise their argument for the sphericity of the Earth: that if its longitudinal extension, i.e., between east and west, were straight, then the rising of the planets upon its inhabitants, and likewise their setting from them, would be at one moment; or if concave, then the rising would be upon the westerners before that upon the easterners in dwellings of agreeing latitude, and likewise the setting in them, but it is not so. Rather, the rising and setting for the easterners is before that for the westerners, by the ruling of astronomical observations of lunar eclipses and others. For their midpoints necessarily agree at one moment, and they differ with respect to the beginning of the night, such that if it is for the westerner after two hours have passed, it is for the easterner after three hours have passed if the distance between their two noons is fifteen degrees, and between their two dwellings of agreeing latitude a thousand miles. And on this pattern convexity is determined. And if the latitudinal extension, i.e., between south and north, were straight, then the elevation of one pole and the depression of the other would remain the same relative to the traveler as much as he travels; and if concave, then the elevation of the visible pole would decrease and the depression of the other relative to the traveler toward the visible pole, and vice versa for the traveler toward the hidden pole. But existence is contrary to that, because the elevation of the north pole and the depression of the south pole increase for those penetrating into the north, and vice versa for those penetrating into the south, according to their penetration. So convexity is determined in these two extensions, and likewise in all other extensions that are in directions between two directions, due to the composition of the two differences according to what convexity requires, not straightness or concavity. And since the curvature of the revealed portion is established, it is conjectured from it that the remainder is likewise. And it is objected: it is possible that the existence of the mentioned things in the mentioned manner is based on a cause other than curvature and centrality, and its summary is that what you have mentioned is an inference from the existence of an effect to the existence of a specific cause, and it is not complete unless the nonexistence of another cause is shown. And even if it is conceded, what is mentioned only conveys curvature and centrality according to sense, not in reality, and there is no escape except by recourse to the fact that it is conjecture, as in the illumination of the moon by the sun.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the Earth being spherical and central, followed by an objection and a minimal response. The author first presents two astronomical arguments: one based on the longitudinal (east-west) extension and one on the latitudinal (north-south) extension. For the longitudinal argument, if the Earth were flat, the rising and setting of stars would be simultaneous for all observers, or if concave, easterners would see risings later than westerners. Observations show that easterners see risings earlier, implying convexity. The latitudinal argument uses the change in visible pole height as one travels north or south; if the Earth were flat, pole height would not change, but it does, again indicating convexity. From the curvature of the visible part, the author infers the whole Earth is curved. The objection then states that such observations could have another cause, so the inference from effect to cause is not certain unless alternative causes are ruled out. The author concedes that the argument only provides sensory evidence, not absolute certainty, and ultimately it is a conjecture (hads) similar to how we infer the moon's illumination from the sun.

التوسط
التحديب
تقعير
استقامة
حدس

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Classical Muslim scholars believed the Earth was flat based on scripture.
Correction: This passage shows they used empirical astronomical observations to argue for sphericity and centrality.

Checkpoint

A) Earth is flat
B) Earth is concave
C) Earth is convex
D) Earth is central

Key Takeaway
The Earth's curvature is inferred from systematic variations in astronomical observations, but this inference is probabilistic, not demonstrative.

Compounds Without Mixture: Atmospheric Phenomena
Classification of vapor and smoke phenomena.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Islamic natural philosophy explains weather phenomena such as rain, snow, hail, fog, and thunder as transformations of vapor and smoke.

Core Idea
Vapor and smoke explain rain, snow, hail, fog, thunder, and lightning.

This passage from al-Taftazani's Sharh al-Maqasid provides a systematic classification of weather phenomena based on the behavior of ascending vapor and smoke. It integrates observation with a rational framework, showing how various precipitation and atmospheric events arise from condensation, cold, and trapped smoke. As you read, notice how each phenomenon is linked to a specific condition of vapor or smoke.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions of compounds
Read the opening sentence to see that the author is discussing 'the third division' – compounds without mixture. Understand that this lesson focuses on the first type: atmospheric phenomena.
This sets the context for the detailed description that follows.
2
Trace the fate of ascending vapor
Follow the vapor as it rises. It can become air, or reach the frigid zone and condense into clouds, then rain. Note the subcases: snow if cold strikes before droplet formation, hail if after. If it doesn't reach the frigid zone, it becomes fog, hoarfrost, or dew.
Each outcome depends on the vapor's altitude and temperature conditions.
3
Understand the role of smoke
Learn how smoke accompanying vapor can become trapped in clouds, causing violent tearing that produces thunder (sound), lightning (subtle fire), or a thunderbolt (dense fire).
This explains electrical storms in a natural-philosophical way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (القسم الثالث) في المركبات التي لا مزاج لها وهي أنواع : النوع الأول : ما يحدث فوق الأرض البخار المتصاعد قد يلطف فيصبح هواء، وقد يبلغ الطبقة الزمهريرية فيتكاثف فيجتمع سحاباً ويتقاطر مطراً، وربما يصيبه برد قبل تشكله قطرات فينزل ثلجاً، أو بعده فبرداً، وقد لا يبلغ فيصير ضباباً إن كثر، ونزل صقيعاً، أو طلاً إن قل وتكاثف برد الليل، وربما ينعقد البخار الكثير سحاباً ماطراً بتكاثفه بالبرد، وأن يتصعد إلى الزمهريرية لمانع، وقد يتصاعد مع البخار دخان، فيحتبس في السحاب فيمزقه بعنف إلى فوق إن بقي على حرارته، و إلى تحت إن أصابه برد، فيحدث من تمزيقه ومصاكته إياه صوت هو الرعد، ونارية لطيفة هي البرق، أو كثيفة هي الصاعقة، وقد يشتعل الدخان
He said: (The third division) on compounds that have no mixture, and they are types. The first type: what occurs above the earth: the ascending vapor may become rarefied and become air; or it may reach the frigid zone and condense, gathering as clouds and falling drop by drop as rain; sometimes cold strikes it before it forms droplets, so it falls as snow; or after, so it falls as hail; or it may not reach, so it becomes fog if abundant, and falls as hoarfrost, or as dew if scanty and condensed by the cold of night; and sometimes abundant vapor forms itself into rain-bearing clouds by its condensation due to cold, and that it ascends to the frigid zone because of an impediment; and with the vapor there may ascend smoke, which is trapped in the cloud and tears it violently upward if it retains its heat, or downward if cold strikes it; and from its tearing and friction with it, there occurs a sound which is thunder, and a subtle fire which is lightning, or a dense one which is the thunderbolt; and the smoke may ignite.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a classification and description of the first type of compounds without mixture (i.e., simple composite bodies not involving mixing of humors). The author, al-Taftazani, explains the phenomena of atmospheric vapors. He states that ascending vapor may become rarefied into air, or reach the cold (frigid) layer and condense into clouds, then rain. If cold strikes before droplet formation, it forms snow; after, hail. If it doesn't reach that layer, it becomes fog, hoarfrost, or dew depending on amount and nighttime cold. He also describes how smoke accompanying vapor can become trapped in clouds, causing violent tearing that produces thunder (sound), lightning (subtle fire), or thunderbolt (dense fire). Finally, he mentions that smoke may ignite. This is a natural-philosophical explanation typical of Islamic kalam and falsafa.

Explanations as Conjectures
States that explanations are conjectures based on intuition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the author acknowledges natural explanations as conjectures based on intuition and experience, not certain truths.

Core Idea
Natural explanations are probable, not certain, in contrast to theological knowledge.

This short passage is a crucial methodological note. The author reports that scholars acknowledge their accounts of celestial and terrestrial phenomena are not certain but are built on intuition and experience. This sets the stage for understanding the epistemological humility required in natural philosophy within the kalam framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Note the key terms: 'ẓunūn' (conjectures), 'ḥads' (intuition), and 'tajriba' (experience).
The passage is only one sentence but carries significant weight.
2
Understand the key terms
Define 'ẓunūn' as conjectures or probable knowledge, 'ḥads' as intuitive reason, and 'tajriba' as empirical experience. Recognize that these terms indicate uncertainty.
These terms contrast with 'yaqīn' (certainty) used in theological matters.
3
Recognize the epistemological contrast
Notice that the author is distancing natural explanations from the certainty of religious knowledge. This admission is an honest acknowledgment of the limits of human inquiry into nature.
This is a key point in kalam: only revelation and rational proofs about God yield certainty.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this acknowledgment matters. It prevents conflating probable natural accounts with definitive theological truths and shows the author's methodological rigor.
This passage helps avoid the misconception that the author is rejecting natural explanations entirely.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He said: Then they acknowledge that what they mentioned concerning the celestial and terrestrial effects are conjectures based on intuition and experience.

Commentary
This short passage serves as a methodological note. The author reports that the scholars (or perhaps the philosophers) acknowledge that the explanations given for celestial and terrestrial phenomena are not certain but are conjectures built on intuition (ḥads) and experience (tajriba). This is a crucial admission in the context of kalam, where the certainty of theological knowledge is contrasted with the probable nature of natural philosophy. The passage indicates that the previous detailed explanations of earthquakes and springs are not definitive truths but likely hypotheses.

Correction: The author is not rejecting them; he is clarifying their epistemological status: they are probable conjectures, not certain truths, but still valid as explanations.

Definition of Mizaj: Intermediate Similar Quality
Definition and defense of temperament (mizaj) via subdivision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of temperament (mizaj) as an intermediate similar quality arising from elemental interaction, and the rationale behind its definitional components.

Core Idea
Temperament is a unitary quality from balanced interaction, not mere mixture.

This lesson explores the precise definition of temperament as used in Islamic philosophy and theology. The author clarifies that similarity of quality means actual likeness in every part, not just perceptual indistinguishability. He then presents his preferred definition via subdivision and defends its components, especially the term 'intermediate,' which distinguishes temperament from its accidental accompaniments.

Guided Path
1
Understand similarity of quality
Read the opening lines where the author explains that true similarity means likeness in every part, not just sensory indistinguishability.
This sets up why a unitary quality (kayfiyya wahidaniyya) is needed.
2
Learn the need for true similarity
If similarity were only perceptual, there would be no real action and passion, and no unitary quality preparing the composite for form and soul.
3
Read the definition of mizaj
The author gives his preferred definition via subdivision: 'an intermediate, similar quality arising from the interaction of elements.'
Notice the method of tafri' (subdivision) rather than direct definition.
4
Examine the objection and answer
An objector asks why 'intermediate' is needed. The author answers it is to exclude accompaniments like colors, tastes, and smells.
This clarifies that 'intermediate' is not redundant but exclusionary.
5
Appreciate the role of 'similar'
The term 'similar' is for precision (tahqiq), not avoidance. The author notes that 'tangible' could suffice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد بتشابه الكيفية تماثلها في كل جزء مركب أو بسيط بحيث لا يتفاوت إلا بالعدد . إذ لو كان هي الكيفيات العنصرية بعينها، وكانت التشابه في الحس لفرط الامتزاج، وعدم التمييز لم يكن هناك فعل وانفعال، ولم تتحقق كيفية وحدانية بها يستعد المركب بفيضان صورة ونفس عليه . شروع في رابع الأقسام التي رتب عليها الكلام في فصل مباحث الأجسام على التفصيل وهو في المركبات التي لها مزاج، وفيه مقدمة لبيان حقيقة المزاج وأقسامه، وثلاثة مباحث للإشارة إلى الأقسام الثلاثة للمتزج أعني المعدن والنبات والحيوان ـ وقد سبق أن الكلام في ذلك مبني على قانون الفلسفة، وإنما آثر في تفسير المزاج، طريق التفريع على طريق التعريف بأن يقول هو كيفية متوسطة متشابهة حادثة من تفاعل العناصر المجتمعة المتصغرة الأجزاء بقواها المنكسرة، سورة (۱) كل من كيفياتها الأربع ، لأن ذكر المتوسطة والمتشابهة، إنما يحس بعد ذكر أجزاء العناصر واجتماعها وكيفياتها، وفي رعاية ذلك فوات حسن انتظام اللفظ ووضوح المعنى. فإن قيل : أي حاجة إلى ذكر المتوسطة . قلنا : الاحتراز عن توابع المزاج كالألوان والطعوم والروائح . لأن معنى التوسط أن يكون أقرب إلى كل من الكيفيتين المتضادتين مما يقابلها . بمعنى أن يستحر بالقياس إلى الجزء البارد ويستبرد بالقياس إلى الجزء الحار، وكذا في الرطوبة واليبوسة. وأما ذكر التشابه على ما سيجيء من معناه ، فللتحقيق دون الاحتراز، ولو ذكر بدلهما الملموسة لكفي وحسن التحديد.
And what is meant by the similarity of the quality is its likeness in every simple or compound part, such that they do not differ except in number. For if they were the elemental qualities themselves and the similarity were perceptual due to extreme mixture and lack of distinction, then there would be no action and passion, and there would not be realized a unitary quality by which the composite is prepared for the emanation of a form and soul upon it. Commencement of the fourth division upon which the discourse is arranged in the chapter on investigations of bodies in detail, which is concerning compounds that have a temperament. It includes an introduction to clarify the reality of temperament and its divisions, and three investigations indicating the three divisions of what has temperament, namely mineral, plant, and animal. And it has preceded that the discourse on that is built upon the law of philosophy. He preferred in the interpretation of temperament the method of subdivision over the method of definition, so he says: It is an intermediate, similar quality arising from the interaction of the gathered elements whose parts have become small with their broken powers, the form of each of their four qualities—because the mention of 'intermediate' and 'similar' can only be sensed after mentioning the parts of the elements, their gathering, and their qualities, and in observing that, the beauty of verbal order and clarity of meaning are lost. If it is said: What need is there to mention 'intermediate'? We say: To avoid the accompaniments of temperament such as colors, tastes, and smells. For the meaning of intermediateness is that it be nearer to each of the two opposite qualities than what is opposite to it—that is, it is considered hot relative to the cold part and cold relative to the hot part, and likewise in moisture and dryness. As for mentioning 'similar' according to what will come of its meaning, it is for precision, not for avoidance. And if one had mentioned instead of them the tangible, it would suffice and the definition would be good.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and methodological discussion. It begins by clarifying the meaning of 'similarity of the quality' (tashabuh al-kayfiyya): it is the likeness (tamathul) of the quality in every part of the compound or simple, differing only in number. The author explains that if the qualities were the elemental qualities themselves and similarity were merely perceptual due to extreme mixing, then there would be no real action and passion (fi'l wa infi'al), nor would there be a unitary quality preparing the composite to receive a form and soul. This explains why true similarity is required. Next, the author introduces a new section: the fourth division of the investigation of bodies, concerning compounds with temperament (mizaj). He mentions that the discussion follows philosophical principles. He then explains his preferred method for defining mizaj: he uses the method of subdivision (tafri') rather than direct definition. The definition he gives is: 'an intermediate, similar quality arising from the interaction of elements whose parts have become small and whose powers are broken, the form of each of their four qualities.' He justifies this method by saying that mentioning 'intermediate' and 'similar' only makes sense after mentioning the elements, their gathering, and their qualities; otherwise, verbal order and clarity suffer. Then an objection is raised: 'What need is there to mention intermediate?' The answer: 'To avoid accompaniments like colors, tastes, and smells.' The meaning of 'intermediate' is explained as being nearer to each opposite quality than to what is opposite it; e.g., it is hot relative to the cold part and cold relative to the hot part, similarly for moisture and dryness. As for mentioning 'similar', it is for precision (tahqiq) rather than avoidance; the author notes that if one had instead mentioned 'tangible' (al-malmusa), the definition would suffice and be good.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'similarity' in mizaj means perceptual indistinguishability, i.e., the qualities appear similar to the senses due to extreme mixing.
Correction: The author clarifies that similarity means actual ontological likeness in every part, not just perceptual. Otherwise, there would be no real action/passion and no unitary quality.

Key Takeaway

The Four Levels of Temperament and Two Perspectives
The author defines the four levels of temperament and the two modes of comparison.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four levels of temperament (type, kind, individual, organ) and how each is compared externally and internally.

Core Idea
Temperament is analyzed at four levels, each with external and internal comparisons.

Before diving into examples, the author establishes a framework: temperament is considered at four levels—type, kind, individual, and organ. At each level, we can compare the item either to others outside its category or to its own internal subdivisions. This lesson unpacks that framework, which is essential for understanding the detailed examples that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four levels
The passage lists four levels: النوع (type), الصنف (kind), الشخص (person/individual), and العضو (organ). Notice that each is a distinct category of temperament analysis.
These levels progress from most general to most specific.
2
Understand the two perspectives
The author states that each level can be considered either in relation to what is external (بالقياس إلى الخارج) or internal (بالقياس إلى الداخل). External means comparing to other items at the same level; internal means comparing to its own subdivisions.
This dual analysis applies to all four levels.
3
See the pattern in the examples
Although the passage continues with examples, focus here on the initial definition. The examples will reinforce this framework.

النوع، وكذا لزيد مزاج هو أليق به من حيث هو هذا الشخص المعين، أي أنسب بالصفات المختصة به من أمزجة أفراد ذلك الصنف، وهو المزاج الذي يجب أن يكون زيد عليه، ليكون موجوداً حياً صحيحاً. ثم لاخفاء في أن له All of that [is] from the person, or from the organ, and each of that is considered either in relation to the external, meaning for the type to the rest of the types, and for the kind to the rest of the kinds of that type, and for the person to the rest of the persons of that kind, and for the organ to the rest of the organs of the body; or in relation to the internal. Meaning for the type to what it has of kinds, and for the kind to what it has of persons, and for the person to what occurs to it of states, and likewise for the organ. For example, for the human body there is a temperament that is more suitable to it insofar as it is human than the temperament of any type assumed, such that if it changes or corrupts, the actions specific to this type become disordered. And it has degrees oscillating between two extremes of excess and deficiency, which is expressed as the width (sa'a) of the temperament; because it is certain that not all individuals of humanity are on one temperament, and also not every temperament is suitable for the human form. So let us suppose that its heat does not exceed twenty nor fall below ten, but oscillates between them. So if it exceeds twenty, the mixture would not be human but a horse; and if it falls below ten, it would not be human but a rabbit. Then inevitably there is a mean between these two extremes—I mean excess and deficiency—that is more suitable to it insofar as it is human than the temperament of any individual assumed of human individuals, and it is the best of human temperaments and the closest to balance (i'tidal) at the age when growth reaches its peak. And though it is not the absolute balance which they have judged impossible to exist, its existence is rare, for it exists only in one individual whom the physicians make a standard, to which other individuals are measured. Similarly, for the Turk there is a specific temperament more suitable to it insofar as it is Turk among the temperaments of the rest of the kinds of humanity; it has a width, i.e., a range such that if the person departs from it he would not be a Turk but another kind; and it has a mean that is more suitable to any individual assumed of Turk individuals, [being] the best of the temperaments of the kind, though it is not necessarily the best of the temperaments of the type. Similarly, for Zayd there is a temperament more suitable to him insofar as he is this specific person, i.e., more appropriate to the qualities specific to him than the temperaments of individuals of that kind; and it is the temperament that Zayd must have in order to be existent, alive, and healthy. Then there is no concealment that it has a range, necessarily, for his temperament as a youth is different from his temperament as an old man or a boy or an adult; and it has two extremes of excess and deficiency that it does not exceed, necessarily, because not every temperament is suitable for him, given his specification with a particular temperament; and between them is a mean which, if obtained for Zayd, he would be in the best state he ought to be in, meaning that the temperament he has at that time is more suitable for his actions than the temperament he has at other times. Similarly, for the heart there is a temperament more suitable to it than the temperaments of the rest of the organs of the body; it has a width with two extremes: if it exceeds them it would not be a heart; and a mean which, if obtained for the heart, it would be in the best state it ought to be in.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage defines and exemplifies the four levels of temperament: type (naw'), kind (sinf), individual (shakhs), and organ ('udw). The author begins by stating that each of these can be considered in two ways: externally (relative to other items at the same level) and internally (relative to its own subdivisions). He then provides detailed examples. For the human body type, he explains that there is a specific temperament most suitable for it, with a range between excess and deficiency. He illustrates with a hypothetical numerical scale for heat: if heat exceeds 20, the mixture becomes a horse; if below 10, a rabbit; the mean is best. Similarly, he discusses the temperament for the Turk as a kind, for Zayd as a person, and for the heart as an organ, each with its own range and mean. The emphasis is that each level has a specific temperament that is most appropriate, and deviations result in a different entity at that level.

Temperament of the Human Type: Range and Mean
The author illustrates the temperament of the human type with a numerical example of heat range.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the human type has a specific temperament with a range (width) and a mean, illustrated by a numerical example.

Core Idea
The human type has a temperament with a range between excess and deficiency, and a mean that is best.

After establishing the framework, the author provides a concrete example: the human type. He explains that the human body has a temperament most suitable for it, with a range (sa'a) between excess and deficiency. Using a hypothetical numerical scale, he shows how deviations produce different species. This lesson delves into that example to clarify the concept of 'width' and 'mean' at the type level.

Guided Path
1
Read the example carefully
The author uses a numerical scale for heat: assume human heat oscillates between 10 and 20. Above 20 becomes a horse, below 10 becomes a rabbit.
This is a hypothetical illustration, not a literal claim.
2
Identify the 'width' (سعة)
The range between 10 and 20 is the 'width' of the temperament for the human type. Within this range, different individuals can vary but remain human.
3
Find the mean (واسطة)
The author states that there is a mean between the extremes (excess and deficiency) that is most suitable for being human. This mean is the best temperament for the type.
This mean is rare and often used as a standard by physicians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جميع من ذلك الشخص، أو من العضو، وكل من ذلك يعتبر إما بالقياس إلى الخارج، أعني للنوع إلى سائر الأنواع ، وللصنف إلى سائر الأصناف من ذلك النوع ، وللشخص إلى سائر الأشخاص من ذلك الصنف، وللعضو إلى سائر الأعضاء البدن ، وإما بالقياس إلى الداخل. أعني للنوع إلى ما له من الأصناف ، وللصنف إلى ما له من الأشخاص وللشخص إلى ما يعرض له من الأحوال، وكذا للعضو مثلاً للبدن الإنساني مزاج هو أليق به من حيث أنه إنسان من مزاج أي نوع فرض، بحيث إذا تغيَّر أو فسد اختلت الأفعال المختصة بهذا النوع، وله مراتب يتردد فيها بين طرفي إفراط وتفريط يعبر عنها بسعة المزاج، للقطع بأن ليس أفراد الإنسان على مزاج واحد، وليس أيضاً كل مزاج صالحاً للصورة الإنسانية ، فلنفرض أن حرارته لا تزيد على عشرين، ولا تنقص عن عشرة، بل تتردد بينهما، فإذا زادت على عشرين لم يكن الممتزج إنساناً، بل فرساً، وإذا نقصت من عشرة لم يكن إنساناً، بل أرنباً، ثم لا محالة تكون هناك واسطة بين هذين الطرفين. أعني الإفراط والتفريط هي أليق به من حيث أنه إنسان من مزاج. أي فرد فرض من أفراد الإنسان، ويكون أفضل أمزجة الإنسان وأقربها إلى الاعتدال في سن بلغ فيه النشو غايته، وهو وإن لم يكن الاعتدال لكنه الحقيقي الذي حكموا بامتناع وجوده يعز وجوده، إذ لا يوجد إلا في شخص واحد تجعله الأطباء دستوراً، يقاس إليه سائر الأشخاص، وكذا للتركي مزاج خاص هو أليق به من حيث أنه تركي في أمزجة سائر أصناف الانسان له عرض أي سعة لو خرج الشخص عنها لم يكن تركياً، بل صنفاً آخر وله واسطة هي أليق به من فرد فرض من أفراد التركي أفضل أمزجة الصنف، وإن لم يلزم أن تكون أفضل أمزجة النوع، وكذا لزيد مزاج هو أليق به من حيث هو هذا الشخص المعين، أي أنسب بالصفات المختصة به من أمزجة أفراد ذلك الصنف، وهو المزاج الذي يجب أن يكون زيد عليه، ليكون موجوداً حياً صحيحاً. ثم لاخفاء في أن له سعة ضرورة أن مزاجه وهو شاب غير مزاجه وهو شيخ أو صبي أو كهل، ولها طرفا إفراط وتفريط لا تتعداهما ضرورة أن ليس كل مزاج صالحاً له مع اختصاصه بمزاج معين وبينهما واسطة إذا حصلت لزيد كان على أفضل ما ينبغي أن يكون عليه بمعنى أن المزاج الذي له في ذلك الوقت أصلح لأفعاله من المزاج الذي له في سائر أوقاته ، وكذا للقلب مزاج هو أليق به من أمزجة سائر أعضاء البدن . عريض له طرفان إذا تجاوزهما لم يكن القلب، وواسطة إذا حصلت للقلب كان على أفضل ما ينبغي أن يكون عليه
All of that [is] from the person, or from the organ, and each of that is considered either in relation to the external, meaning for the type to the rest of the types, and for the kind to the rest of the kinds of that type, and for the person to the rest of the persons of that kind, and for the organ to the rest of the organs of the body; or in relation to the internal. Meaning for the type to what it has of kinds, and for the kind to what it has of persons, and for the person to what occurs to it of states, and likewise for the organ. For example, for the human body there is a temperament that is more suitable to it insofar as it is human than the temperament of any type assumed, such that if it changes or corrupts, the actions specific to this type become disordered. And it has degrees oscillating between two extremes of excess and deficiency, which is expressed as the width (sa'a) of the temperament; because it is certain that not all individuals of humanity are on one temperament, and also not every temperament is suitable for the human form. So let us suppose that its heat does not exceed twenty nor fall below ten, but oscillates between them. So if it exceeds twenty, the mixture would not be human but a horse; and if it falls below ten, it would not be human but a rabbit. Then inevitably there is a mean between these two extremes—I mean excess and deficiency—that is more suitable to it insofar as it is human than the temperament of any individual assumed of human individuals, and it is the best of human temperaments and the closest to balance (i'tidal) at the age when growth reaches its peak. And though it is not the absolute balance which they have judged impossible to exist, its existence is rare, for it exists only in one individual whom the physicians make a standard, to which other individuals are measured. Similarly, for the Turk there is a specific temperament more suitable to it insofar as it is Turk among the temperaments of the rest of the kinds of humanity; it has a width, i.e., a range such that if the person departs from it he would not be a Turk but another kind; and it has a mean that is more suitable to any individual assumed of Turk individuals, [being] the best of the temperaments of the kind, though it is not necessarily the best of the temperaments of the type. Similarly, for Zayd there is a temperament more suitable to him insofar as he is this specific person, i.e., more appropriate to the qualities specific to him than the temperaments of individuals of that kind; and it is the temperament that Zayd must have in order to be existent, alive, and healthy. Then there is no concealment that it has a range, necessarily, for his temperament as a youth is different from his temperament as an old man or a boy or an adult; and it has two extremes of excess and deficiency that it does not exceed, necessarily, because not every temperament is suitable for him, given his specification with a particular temperament; and between them is a mean which, if obtained for Zayd, he would be in the best state he ought to be in, meaning that the temperament he has at that time is more suitable for his actions than the temperament he has at other times. Similarly, for the heart there is a temperament more suitable to it than the temperaments of the rest of the organs of the body; it has a width with two extremes: if it exceeds them it would not be a heart; and a mean which, if obtained for the heart, it would be in the best state it ought to be in.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage defines and exemplifies the four levels of temperament: type (naw'), kind (sinf), individual (shakhs), and organ ('udw). The author begins by stating that each of these can be considered in two ways: externally (relative to other items at the same level) and internally (relative to its own subdivisions). He then provides detailed examples. For the human body type, he explains that there is a specific temperament most suitable for it, with a range between excess and deficiency. He illustrates with a hypothetical numerical scale for heat: if heat exceeds 20, the mixture becomes a horse; if below 10, a rabbit; the mean is best. Similarly, he discusses the temperament for the Turk as a kind, for Zayd as a person, and for the heart as an organ, each with its own range and mean. The emphasis is that each level has a specific temperament that is most appropriate, and deviations result in a different entity at that level.

اعتدال
إنسان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that there is only one valid temperament for all humans, or that any human temperament is acceptable.
Correction: The passage explains that the human type has a range (10-20 in the example) but also a specific mean that is ideal. Not every temperament within the range is equally suitable; the mean is the best.

Checkpoint
In the author's example, what happens if the heat of a mixture exceeds 20?
A) It becomes an excellent human.
B) It becomes a horse.
C) It becomes a rabbit.
D) It becomes a Turk.

Key Takeaway
The human type has a temperament with a defined range and a rare mean that is the best and used as a standard.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The mean temperament exists in only one person, whom physicians use as a standard. If you were measured against that perfect human temperament—not just physically, but in your whole character—how close would you be? What is one area where you are outside the range, and what would it take to move toward the mean?
?
The passage says the best temperament is at the peak of growth (bulūgh al-nushuw ghāyatahu). Have you reached your own peak? Or are you still growing? What habits are keeping you from reaching your fullest balance, and will you commit to changing one of them this week?
?
You are not the standard individual; you are measured against someone else. Does that thought humble you or provoke you to strive? In your daily life, who do you take as your standard—and is that person truly worthy of being your measure?

Method of Learning
intermediate
7 min
Temperament of Kind, Individual, and Organ
The author gives examples of temperament for the Turk kind, Zayd as an individual, and the heart as an organ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how temperament applies to kind (e.g., Turk), individual (e.g., Zayd), and organ (e.g., heart), each with its own range and mean.

Core Idea
Each subdivision (kind, individual, organ) has a temperament unique to it, with its own width and ideal mean.

After the detailed example for the human type, the author applies the same logic to lower levels: kind (the Turk), individual (Zayd), and organ (the heart). Each has a temperament most suitable to it, a range that defines its identity, and a mean that represents its best state. This lesson explores these examples, showing how the framework scales down.

Guided Path
1
Examine the example of kind (Turk)
The author says there is a temperament more suitable for the Turk kind. It has a width; if a person departs from it, he would not be a Turk but another kind. There is also a mean that is best for that kind.
This mean is the best for the kind, but not necessarily the best for the type (human).
2
Examine the example of individual (Zayd)
Zayd as an individual has a temperament most suitable for his specific person. It has a range (his temperament changes with age) and a mean that is best for him at each stage.
The mean for Zayd is the temperament most conducive to his actions at that time.
3
Examine the example of organ (heart)
The heart has a temperament most suitable for it compared to other organs. It has two extremes beyond which it would not be a heart, and a mean that is best for the heart.
This mirrors the pattern seen at higher levels.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that the best temperament for a kind is also the best for the type, or that individuals of the same ethnic group share the exact same temperament.
Correction: The author clarifies that the best temperament for a kind (e.g., Turk) may not be the best for the type (human), and individuals within a kind have their own specific temperament.

According to the passage, if a person's temperament departs from the width of the Turk kind, he would become:
A) A different kind
B) A different type
C) A different person
D) A different organ

Key Takeaway
Temperament scales down from type to kind to individual to organ, each with its own suitable range and ideal mean.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your temperament as an individual has a width that changes with age. Have you been forcing yourself to be the same person you were five years ago, ignoring the natural shift in your balance? What would it look like for you to accept and embrace the temperament that is most suitable for you today, rather than fighting it?
?
The heart has its own optimal temperament. Do you know what throws your heart off balance—what excess or deficiency disturbs its peace? What is one concrete step you can take this week to restore your heart to its proper state?
?
The best temperament for the Turk kind is not automatically the best for humans overall. In what areas of your life are you holding yourself to an ideal meant for someone else (a different 'kind' or 'type') rather than striving for the mean that fits your unique individual constitution?

Method of Learning

Summary and Objection on Organ Temperament Classification
Summary of temperament hierarchy and objection on organ classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the temperament of an organ is not included in the general widths of type, kind, and person, and how the objection and its answer clarify the scope of medical consideration.

Core Idea
Organ temperament is excluded because the body's balance is a whole, not a single organ.

This passage consolidates the earlier classification of temperaments and addresses a potential objection. Having established that the temperament of a type encompasses its kinds, and so on down to the person, the author now explains why the organ is not a separate category in this framework. The objection and its answer reveal the practical reasoning of physicians.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the objection begins with 'فَإِنْ قِيلَ' and the answer with 'قُلْنَا'.
2
Identify the summary of temperament widths
The passage first summarizes how the width of the type's temperament includes the temperaments of its kinds, and so on down to the person. Find the chain: type → kind → person.
3
Understand the exclusion of organ temperament
The author states that organ temperament is not included because the widths are based on the totality of the body, assuming equilibrium of hot and cold, moist and dry organs. A single organ cannot represent the whole.
The key phrase is 'فَيَسْتَحِيلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مِزَاجُ مَجْمُوعِ الْبَدَنِ، مِزَاجَ عُضْوٍ وَاحِدٍ'.
4
Analyze the objection and answer
The objection argues that an organ is a type with its own kinds and individuals, so it should have its own specific balance levels. The answer concedes this is true in general but explains that medicine focuses on the body as a whole regarding equilibrium.
The answer begins with 'قُلْنَا : نَعَمْ إِلَّا أَنَّهُمْ نَظَرُوا...'.
5
Reflect on the medical perspective
Consider why physicians treat the body's temperament as a whole. This connects the theoretical classification to practical medical observation.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the key idea is that body temperament is about the balance of all organs, not individual ones.

فَظَهَرَ أَنَّ عَرْضَ مِزَاجِ النَّوْعِ يَشْتَمِلُ عَلَى أَمْزِجَةِ أَصْنَافِهِ، لِأَنَّ غَرَضَ الصِّنْفِ، بَعْضُ غَرَضِ النَّوْعِ، وَغَرَضُ مِزَاجِ الصِّنْفِ يَشْتَمِلُ عَلَى أَمْزِجَةِ أَشْخَاصِهِ، وَعَرَضُ مِزَاجِ الشَّخْصِ عَلَى أَمْزِجَتِهِ فِي حَالَاتِهِ، وَلَيْسَ مِزَاجُ الْعُضْوِ دَاخِلًا فِي الْعُرُوضِ الْمُتَقَدِّمَةِ، لِأَنَّهَا مَأْخُوذَةٌ بِاعْتِبَارِ مَجْمُوعِ الْبَدَنِ، مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ مَجْمُوعٌ إِذَا تَكَافَأَتِ الْأَعْضَاءُ الْحَارَّةُ بِالْبَارِدَةِ وَالرَّطْبَةُ بِالْيَابِسَةِ، فَيَسْتَحِيلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مِزَاجُ مَجْمُوعِ الْبَدَنِ، مِزَاجَ عُضْوٍ وَاحِدٍ. فَإِنْ قِيلَ : الْعُضْوُ نَوْعٌ مِنْ أَنْوَاعِ الْكَائِنَاتِ مُشْتَمِلٌ عَلَى أَصْنَافٍ مُشْتَمِلَةٍ عَلَى أَشْخَاصٍ، فَيَنْبَغِي أَنْ يُعْتَبَرَ لَهُ اعْتِدَالٌ نَوْعِيٌّ وَصِنْفِيٌّ وَشَخْصِيٌّ كُلٌّ مِنْهَا بِالْقِيَاسِ إِلَى الدَّاخِلِ وَالْخَارِجِ دُونَ أَنْ يُجْعَلَ قِسْمًا بِرَأْسِهِ مُقَابِلًا لَهَا . قُلْنَا : نَعَمْ إِلَّا أَنَّهُمْ نَظَرُوا إِلَى أَنَّ الطِّبَّ يَنْظُرُ فِي أَحْوَالِ بَدَنِ الْإِنْسَانِ وَأَعْضَائِهِ مِنْ حَيْثُ كَوْنِهَا عَلَى اعْتِدَالِهَا، أَوْ خَارِجَةً عَنْهُ، وَاعْتِبَارُ مِزَاجِ الْبَدَنِ، إِنَّمَا هُوَ بِاعْتِبَارِ تَكَافُؤِ أَعْضَائِهِ وَتَعَادُلِهَا فِي الْمِزَاجِ، بِأَنْ تَكُونَ حَرَارَةُ مَا هُوَ حَارٌّ مِنْهَا كَالْقَلْبِ تَعَادَلُ
So it became clear that the width of the temperament of the type encompasses the temperaments of its kinds, because the width of the kind is part of the width of the type, and the width of the temperament of the kind encompasses the temperaments of its individuals, and the width of the temperament of the individual encompasses its temperaments in its states; and the temperament of the organ is not included in the preceding widths, because they are taken with consideration of the totality of the body, insofar as it is a totality when the hot organs balance with the cold and the moist with the dry; so it is impossible for the temperament of the total body to be the temperament of a single organ. For if it is said: The organ is a type among types of beings, encompassing kinds that encompass individuals, so it should have a specific balance (i'tidal) at the type level, kind level, and individual level, each of them in relation to the internal and external, without making it a separate division opposing them. We say: Yes, except that they considered that medicine examines the conditions of the human body and its organs insofar as they are in balance or outside it; and the consideration of the temperament of the body is only with regard to the equivalence and equilibrium of its organs in temperament, such that the heat of what is hot among them like the heart is balanced
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage summarizes the relationships between the temperaments of type, kind, person, and organ, and then presents an objection and its answer. The author concludes that the width (range) of the type's temperament includes the ranges of its kinds, and so on down to the person's temperament encompassing his states. The organ's temperament is not included in these widths because the temperament of the body as a whole is considered from the equilibrium of all organs, not from a single organ. The objection raises that the organ itself is a type with its own kinds and individuals, so it should also be considered at its own levels. The answer concedes that this is true in general, but the physicians only consider the body's temperament as a whole because medicine deals with the balance of the entire body, not individual organs separately.

Correction: The author clarifies that the widths are based on the totality of the body, not on individual organs, because the body's temperament is determined by the balance of all organs together.

Refutation of Ibn Sina's Argument on Equatorial Moderation
Taftazani refutes Ibn Sina's equatorial moderation argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Taftazani rejects Ibn Sina's argument that equatorial inhabitants are closest to true moderation, and why uniformity of climate does not prove equality of opposing qualities.

Core Idea
Uniform conditions do not guarantee balanced qualities; adaptation can mask extremes.

Ibn Sina argued that equatorial inhabitants experience unchanging celestial positions, leading to no appreciable temperature variation, so they must be closest to true moderation. But is constant climate the same as balanced climate? Taftazani challenges this assumption by distinguishing between mere uniformity and genuine equality of heat and cold. In this passage, he refutes Ibn Sina's reasoning and clarifies the concept of true moderation.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Taftazani directly addresses Ibn Sina's argument.
Focus on the key phrase 'تشابه الأحوال' (similarity of conditions).
2
Identify Ibn Sina's claim and Taftazani's refutation
In your own words, state what Ibn Sina claimed and why Taftazani says it does not lead to the desired conclusion.
The refutation hinges on the difference between uniformity and equality.
3
Understand the logical flaw
Reflect on why a constant, unchanging climate does not automatically mean that the qualities (heat and cold) are balanced. Taftazani gives an alternative: one could be extreme but accustomed.
True moderation requires equal presence of opposites, not absence of variation.
4
Apply the principle
Think of a real-world example where a constant condition (like extreme heat) becomes 'normal' through habituation, yet is not moderate. How does that illustrate Taftazani's point?
This helps solidify the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن احتجاج ابن سينا على كون سكان خط الاستواء أقرب الأصناف إلى الاعتدال الحقيقي، بالنظر إلى أوضاع العلويات، فهو أن تشابه الأحوال بمعنى أنه لا يطرأ عليهم تغير يعتد به ولا تلحقهم كذلك نكاية من حر أو برد لا يفيد المطلوب. أعني قربهم من الاعتدال الحقيقي الذي تساوي فيه الكيفيات لجواز أن يكون البالغ في الحرارة أو البرودة المألوفة كذلك
As for the answer to Ibn Sina's argument that the inhabitants of the equator are the closest species to true moderation, considering the celestial positions, it is that the similarity of conditions – meaning that no appreciable change befalls them, nor does any harm from heat or cold affect them – does not yield the desired conclusion. I mean their closeness to true moderation in which qualities are equal, because it is possible that one who is extreme in familiar heat or cold is similarly [accustomed].
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage refutes Ibn Sina's argument that equatorial inhabitants are closest to true moderation. Ibn Sina based his claim on the fact that at the equator, the celestial positions cause minimal variation in temperature, so the inhabitants experience no significant change or harm from heat or cold. The refutation is that such uniformity of conditions does not necessarily imply that the qualities (heat and cold) are equal or balanced. It is possible that the inhabitants are simply accustomed to intense heat or cold, and their bodies adapt, so they feel no harm even though the actual temperature is extreme. Thus uniformity does not equal true moderation (where heat and cold are equally present).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that a constant, unchanging climate (uniformity) automatically means a balanced, moderate climate (equality of opposites).
Correction: Uniformity does not guarantee balance; it may simply reflect habituation to an extreme. True moderation requires equality of opposing qualities, not just absence of variation.

Key Takeaway
Uniform climate conditions do not prove balanced qualities; true moderation requires equality of opposing forces, not just absence of variation.

The lesson shows that feeling no harm does not mean you are in a moderate state. Where in your relationship with Allah have you perhaps mistaken 'no crisis' for 'health'? What step can you take today to seek the reality of balance, not just the comfort of familiarity?

The Fourth Clime and the Refutation of an Objection
Taftazani presents the view that the fourth clime is most temperate and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Taftazani argues that the fourth clime is most temperate and how he refutes an objection based on the distinction between apparent and true moderation.

Core Idea
Apparent moderation follows true moderation, so the fourth clime's prosperity proves its balanced nature.

This passage addresses a key debate in classical geography and theology: which region is most balanced for human development? Taftazani presents the view that the fourth clime is most temperate, then raises and refutes an objection based on the distinction between apparent moderation (al-iʿtidāl al-ʿaraḍī) and true moderation (al-iʿtidāl al-ḥaqīqī). The refutation ties the observable prosperity of the fourth clime to its proximity to true moderation and the divine principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and its basis
Recognize that Taftazani states that many scholars hold the fourth clime as most temperate, based on observable effects (prosperity, procreation).
2
Understand the objection
The objection argues that these effects are due to apparent moderation (suitable balance of qualities) rather than true moderation (equality of qualities).
The objector denies that visible prosperity proves true moderation.
3
Grasp the distinction
Distinguish between al-iʿtidāl al-ʿaraḍī (apparent moderation) and al-iʿtidāl al-ḥaqīqī (true moderation). The former is a favorable mix; the latter is exact balance.
The dispute is about which one the effects follow.
4
Follow the refutation
Taftazani refutes by stating that the closer a region is to true moderation, the more it is aligned with the One Principle, and thus more deserving of bestowing perfections. Hence, the fourth clime's prosperity shows its proximity to true moderation.
This links the observable results to the deeper reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب جمع من الأوائل وكثير من المتأخرين إلى أن أعدل الأصناف سكان الإقليم الرابع استدلالاً بالاثار كما هو مذكور في المتن، غني عن الشرح، وفيه إشارة إلى دفع اعتراضين : أحدهما : أن كثرة التوالد والتناسل وتوفر العمارات وغير ذلك من الكمالات إنما يتبع الاعتدال العرضي الذي هو توفر القسط الأليق من الكيفيات لا الحقيقي الذي هو تساويها ، وفيه النزاع. ودفعه أن المعتدل الفرضي كلما كان إلى المعتدل الحقيقي أقرب وبالواحد المبدأ أنسب كان بإفاضة الكمال أجدر ، فيتم
And a group of the ancients and many of the later scholars held that the most temperate species are the inhabitants of the fourth clime, inferring from observable effects as mentioned in the text, which needs no explanation. And in it there is an indication of repelling two objections: One of them: That abundant procreation, thriving constructions, and other perfections follow only the apparent moderation, which is the availability of the most suitable share of qualities, not the true moderation, which is their equality, and therein lies the dispute. Its refutation: That the hypothetical moderate [region] the closer it is to the true moderate and the more appropriate to the One Principle, the more deserving it is of bestowing perfection, so it is complete.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage discusses the view that the inhabitants of the fourth clime are the most temperate (balanced in temperament). This view is based on observable effects such as flourishing civilization and frequent procreation. However, there is an objection: these effects follow from what is called 'apparent moderation' (al-iʿtidāl al-ʿaraḍī) – that is, a favorable balance of qualities for life – rather than true moderation (al-iʿtidāl al-ḥaqīqī), which is the exact equality of opposing qualities. The objector argues that the abundance of life and culture does not prove true moderation. The refutation is that the closer a hypothetical moderate region is to true moderation, and the more it corresponds to the divine plan (the One Principle), the more it is worthy of generating perfections. Thus, the fourth clime's observable prosperity is indeed an indication of its proximity to true moderation.

الاعتدال العرضي
الاعتدال الحقيقي
الكمالات
الواحد المبدأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Visible prosperity, such as thriving constructions and frequent procreation, directly proves true climatic moderation (exact equality of qualities).
Correction: These effects follow from apparent moderation (a suitable balance), but the refutation shows that such prosperity indicates closeness to true moderation, not that it is identical to it.

Checkpoint
