A) True moderation (equality of qualities).
B) Apparent moderation (suitable balance of qualities).
C) The One Principle.
D) The fourth clime is the worst.

Key Takeaway
The fourth clime's prosperity is evidence of its nearness to true moderation, which aligns with the divine principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage links observable prosperity to closeness to the One Principle. Look at the 'prosperity' in your own life—your time, energy, achievements. Is the fruit you bear evidence of nearness to Allah? If not, what might that reveal about the true balance of your heart?
?
Taftazani refutes an objection that separates apparent from true moderation. When you make excuses for a lack of spiritual depth by saying 'at least I'm outwardly practicing', are you falling into a similar trap? What would it take for you to pursue true moderation—complete submission—rather than just a suitable appearance?
?
The fourth clime's perfection is tied to its alignment with the One Principle. If you were to measure your own 'temperateness' by proximity to Allah, what specific area of your life would need the most recalibration? Commit to one concrete change today to move closer to true balance.

Measuring Specific Gravity by Water Displacement
Al-Taftazani describes an experiment to determine specific gravity using water displacement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the experimental method described by al-Taftazani for determining the specific gravity of metals and stones using water displacement.

Core Idea
Water displacement reveals the hidden density of objects.

Al-Taftazani walks us through a careful experiment to find how dense different materials are. By measuring the water pushed out when an object is submerged, we can compare volumes and weights. This passage shows how medieval scientists like al-Biruni built tables of specific gravity that are still impressively accurate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the English translation. Notice the practical details: the spout, the purified water, the autumn season.
This is a hands-on procedure, not an abstract theory.
2
Focus on the gold example
The passage uses gold as an example. Identify the numbers: 100 mithqals of gold displace 5.25 mithqals of water. Understand how al-Taftazani calculates the weight in water by subtracting the displaced water weight.
This is a direct application of Archimedes' principle.
3
Understand the buoyancy rule
The passage states a clear rule: if displaced water weighs less than the object, it sinks; if more, it floats; if equal, it stays suspended. Connect this to the concept of density.
This rule is independent of the object's shape.
4
Reflect on the scientific method
Note the attention to variables: water source (Jihun), season (autumn), purification of metals and water. This shows awareness that conditions affect results.
Al-Taftazani is thinking like a modern experimentalist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذهب مثلاً، وجعل تحت رأس الميزاب كفة الميزان الذي يريد به معرفة مقدار الماء الذي يخرج من الإناء، وهكذا كل من الفلزات والأحجار، بعد ما بالغ في تنقية الفلزات من الغش، وفي تصفية الماء، وكان ذلك من ماء جيحون في خوارزم في فصل الخريف. ولا شك أن الحكم يختلف باختلاف المياه، واختلاف أحوالها بحسب البلدان والفصول فحصل معرفة مقدار الماء الذي يخرج من الإناء بمائة مثقال من كل من الفلزات والأحجار، وعرف بذلك مقدار تفاوتها في الحجم والثقل فإن ما يكون ماؤه أكثر يكون حجمه أكبر، وثقله أقل بنسبة تفاوت المادين، وإذا أسقط ماء كل من وزنه في الهواء، كان الباقي وزنه في الماء مثلاً. لما كان ماء مائة مثقال من الذهب خمسة مثاقيل وربع مثقال، كان وزنه في الماء أربعة وتسعين مثقالاً وثلاثة أرباع مثقال، والماء الذي يخرج من الإناء بالقاء الجسم فيه، إن كان أقل من وزن الجسم، فالجسم يرسب في الماء، وإن كان أكثر منه فيطفو، وإن كان مساوياً له، فالجسم ينزل في الماء بحيث يماس أعلاه سطح الماء. وقد وضع أبو ريحان ومن تبعه جدولاً جامعاً لمقدار الماء الذي يخرج من الإناء بمائة مثقال من الذهب والفضة، وغيرهما، ولمقدار أوزانها عند كون الفلزات السبعة في حجم مائة مثقال مائة مثقال من الذهب والجواهر في حجم مائة مثقال من الياقوت الاسمانجوني، ولمقدار أوزانها في الماء، بعد ما يكون مائة مثقال في الهواء. وهذا هو الجدول والله أعلم.
For example, gold, and he placed under the head of the spout the pan of the balance by which he intended to know the amount of water that comes out of the vessel, and likewise for each of the metals and stones, after he thoroughly purified the metals from adulteration and purified the water, and that was from the water of the Jihun in Khwarazm in the autumn season. There is no doubt that the judgment differs according to the difference of waters and their conditions according to regions and seasons. So he obtained knowledge of the amount of water that comes out of the vessel for one hundred mithqals of each of the metals and stones, and thereby he knew the measure of their difference in volume and weight, for that whose water is more has a larger volume and lighter weight in proportion to the difference of the two materials. And if one subtracts the water of each from its weight in air, the remainder is its weight in water, for example. Since the water of one hundred mithqals of gold was five and a quarter mithqals, its weight in water was ninety-four and three-quarters mithqal. And the water that comes out of the vessel when the body is thrown into it, if it is less than the weight of the body, then the body sinks in the water; if it is more, then it floats; and if it is equal, then the body descends in the water such that its top touches the surface of the water. And Abu Rayhan and those who followed him have placed a comprehensive table for the amount of water that comes out of the vessel for one hundred mithqals of gold, silver, and others, and for the measure of their weights when the seven metals are in the volume of one hundred mithqals of gold and gems in the volume of one hundred mithqals of the sapphire (al-yāqūt al-asmanjūnī), and for the measure of their weights in water, after making one hundred mithqals in air. And this is the table, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a descriptive explanation of a method for determining specific gravity (the ratio of a substance's weight to an equal volume of water) using water displacement. The author describes an experiment where a container with a spout is filled with water, and a body (e.g., gold) is placed in a pan under the spout. The water displaced by the body is collected and weighed. The author notes that the water used came from the Jihun river in Khwarazm in autumn, and acknowledges that results vary with water quality, region, and season. By measuring the displaced water for one hundred mithqals of each metal or stone, one can find the differences in volume and weight: a substance that displaces more water has a larger volume and lighter weight relative to its mass. He then gives a specific calculation: for gold, one hundred mithqals in air displace five and a quarter mithqals of water, so its weight in water becomes ninety-four and three-quarters mithqal (i.e., its buoyant force reduces its apparent weight). He explains the principle of buoyancy: if the displaced water weighs less than the body, the body sinks; if more, it floats; if equal, it is neutrally buoyant. Finally, he mentions that Abu Rayhan (al-Biruni) and his followers compiled a comprehensive table listing the displaced water for one hundred mithqals of various metals and gems, including seven metals and the sapphire, both in terms of volume equivalence and weight in water.

جدول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The experiment assumes all water is the same, so results are universal.
Correction: Al-Taftazani explicitly notes that the judgment differs with water source, region, and season, so experiments must be contextual.

Checkpoint

A) When the displaced water weighs more than the body
B) When the displaced water weighs less than the body
C) When the displaced water weighs the same as the body
D) When the body is made of gold

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani's experiment shows that specific gravity can be measured by water displacement, using a simple but rigorous procedure that accounts for variables.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Taftazani went to great lengths to purify metals and control the water source and season. When you seek knowledge—whether religious or worldly—do you take such care to remove biases and variables? Will you commit to one specific practice that will make your learning more rigorous starting this week?
?
The passage ends with 'والله أعلم' (And God knows best). In an age of information, it is easy to speak with false certainty. Think of a belief you hold strongly about your faith or life. Are you open to the possibility that your understanding is limited by your context (like the water from Jihun in autumn)? Write down one area where you will intentionally add 'God knows best' to your speech today.
?
Abu Rayhan and his followers compiled a table of data—they did not rely on guesswork. When you face a difficult question in your spiritual journey, do you seek systematic understanding through study, reflection, and reliable sources, or do you jump to comfortable conclusions? What is the next step you will take to build a more evidence-based faith?

The Difficulty of Classifying Psychic Powers
Discusses the difficulty of determining the genus and species of psychic powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classifying psychic powers is epistemologically challenging and why scholarly discussion remains undecided.

Core Idea
Classification of unseen powers is difficult because they are known only through effects and relations.

In this passage, the author highlights a fundamental challenge in the study of psychic powers: their classification into genera and species. Unlike physical objects that can be directly observed, these powers are known only indirectly through their effects. This creates uncertainty in determining essential versus accidental attributes. Let's read the passage to see how the author articulates this difficulty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'kalamuhum mutaraddid' – their discussion is undecided.
2
Identify the points of uncertainty
The author lists several unresolved questions: whether psychic powers are a genus, the divisions of sensory and motor powers, and all divisions in this topic. List them mentally.
These uncertainties are the core of the passage.
3
Understand the reason for difficulty
The author contrasts clear perception with indirect knowledge. Reflect on why something known only by its effects is harder to classify than something seen.
Consider examples from modern science, like subatomic particles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن كلامهم متردد في أن القوى النفسانية جنس للمدركة والمحركة أي بمنزلة الجنس، وكذا في انقسام كل منهما إلى ماله من الأقسام بل في جميع الانقسامات الواقعة في باب القوى، وذلك لأن معرفة الأجناس والفصول، وتمييز الذاتيات والغرضيات عسيرة جداً في الحقائق المدركة بالعيان، فكيف فيما لا يعرف إلا من جهة الآثار، ولا يعقل إلا بحسب الإضافات والاعتبارات ككون الشيء مبدأ التغير في آخر.
Then indeed, their discussion is undecided about whether the psychic powers are a genus for the sensory and motor powers—that is, in the manner of a genus—and likewise about the division of each of them into its divisions, indeed about all the divisions occurring in the chapter of powers. This is because knowledge of genera and differentiae, and distinguishing essentials from accidentals, is very difficult in realities perceived by sight, so how about that which is known only by way of effects and is understood only in terms of relations and considerations, such as something being the principle of change in another?
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage highlights the difficulty in classifying the psychological powers (sensory and motor). The author notes that scholarly discussion is undecided (mutaraddid) on several points: whether 'psychic powers' is a genus for the sensory and motor powers, and the subdivisions of each, and indeed all divisions in the topic of powers. The reason is that knowing genera (ajnas) and differentiae (fusul) and distinguishing essential (dhatiyyat) from accidental (gharadiyyat) is very difficult even for things perceived by sight. How much more difficult for things known only through their effects (athar) and understood only through relations (idafat) and considerations (i'tibarat), such as something being a principle of change in another. The author is emphasizing the epistemological challenge in this area.

Key Takeaway
The classification of psychic powers is inherently uncertain because they are known only through their effects, not direct observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Sense of Touch: Definition, Seat, and Necessity
al-Taftazani defines touch and explains its necessity and seat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition, necessity, and physical seat of the sense of touch according to al-Taftazani.

Core Idea
Touch is the essential sense spread throughout the body, without which animal life cannot endure.

Al-Taftazani begins the discussion of the external senses with the sense of touch, which he argues is the most fundamental and pervasive. He defines it as a power diffused in the body, distinguishes it from other senses by its necessity for survival, and describes its mechanism through the nerves. This passage lays the groundwork for understanding how physical perception operates in the human being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let al-Taftazani's own definitions come through before any interpretation.
The passage is incomplete at the end, but the main points are clear.
2
Identify three main elements
Look for (1) the definition of touch as a diffused power, (2) its necessity for animals compared to the nutritive faculty, and (3) its seat in the nerves and skin.
Notice that touch is said to be present except in certain internal organs.
3
Examine the list of perceptible qualities
The passage enumerates pairs of opposites: heat/cold, moisture/dryness, roughness/smoothness. Reflect on why these are fundamental to touch.
This list is based on induction (al-istiqra).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (أما الحواس الظاهرة فمنها اللمس : وهي قوة سارية في البدن تدرك بها الحرارة والبرودة، ونحوهما عند المماسة وهي للحيوان في محل الضرورة، كالغاذية للنبات، ولذا كانت لمعونة العصب الأعضاء، سوى ما يتضرر به كالكبد والطحال، والكلية والرئة سارية في جميع والعظم، وكان الحيوان يبقى عند بطلان سائر الحواس دونها . هي قوة تأتي في الأعصاب إلى جميع الجلد، وأكثر اللحم والغشاء، من شأنها إدراك الحرارة والبرودة، والرطوبة واليبوسة، والخشونة والملاسة، ونحو ذلك، بأن ينفعل عنها العضو اللامس عند المماسة بحكم الاستقراء، ولأنها لو أدركت
He said: 'As for the external senses, among them is touch: It is a power diffused in the body by which heat, cold, and similar things are perceived upon contact. It is for the animal in the place of necessity, like the nutritive faculty for plants. Therefore it was for the assistance of the nerves [in] the organs, except what is harmed by it, like the liver, spleen, kidney, and lung — diffused in all and the bone. And the animal remains when the other senses are annulled without it. It is a power traveling in the nerves to all the skin, most of the flesh and membrane, whose function is to perceive heat, cold, moisture, dryness, roughness, smoothness, and the like, such that the touching organ is affected by it upon contact, by the rule of induction, and because if it perceived...'
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage defines the sense of touch (al-lams). It is a power diffused throughout the body that perceives qualities like heat and cold upon contact. Touch is as necessary for animals as the nutritive faculty is for plants; it relies on nerves and is present throughout the body except in organs that would be harmed by it (such as liver, spleen, etc.). Even if all other senses are lost, the animal can survive with touch alone. The description continues: touch travels via nerves to the skin, flesh, and membranes, perceiving tangible qualities like heat, cold, moisture, dryness, roughness, smoothness, etc. The organ is affected by the object upon contact, established by induction. The passage breaks off at 'because if it perceived...' but the definition is clear.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Touch is only a sense of the skin.
Correction: Touch is diffused throughout the body, including internal organs, except those that would be harmed by it.

Checkpoint

B) Because animals cannot live without it.
C) Because it is like the nutritive faculty in plants.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
Touch is an essential sense diffused throughout the body that perceives physical qualities upon contact and is indispensable for animal life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The sense of touch perceives both pleasant and painful sensations. How do you respond to the 'painful touches' in your life—illness, loss, discomfort—as signals from Allah calling you back to Him? Write down one physical discomfort you are currently facing and ask yourself: Is this a reminder of my dependence on Him?
?
Like touch being diffused throughout your body, Allah's mercy encompasses all of creation. But you only feel it when you are in contact with His signs. Are you in constant contact with the Qur'an and remembrance, or have you become numb? Commit to a specific dhikr habit today that will keep your heart 'feeling' His presence.

Optic Nerve Anatomy and the Proof of Perception
Taftazani describes the optic nerve pathway and offers a proof from defect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani uses anatomical knowledge of the optic nerves (chiasm, ventricles) to argue that the perceptive faculties reside in the brain and its connections.

Core Idea
A defect in a specific location proves that the corresponding faculty resides there.

Taftazani begins by citing established anatomical knowledge about the optic nerves. He describes the origin, crossing, and path of the first pair of cranial nerves, then derives a powerful proof: because damage to these parts impairs vision, the perceptive faculty of sight must be located in those parts. This passage exemplifies the kalam methodology of using empirical observation (through anatomy) to support rational arguments about the soul's faculties.

Guided Path
1
Read the anatomical description
Read the passage carefully, noting the specific details about the origin, crossing, and destination of the optic nerves. Try to visualize the path described.
The description matches the optic chiasm, which Taftazani calls 'تقاطع صليبين' (a cross intersection).
2
Identify the proof
Locate the sentence starting with 'والدليل' which presents the proof. How does the author transition from anatomy to proof?
3
Reflect on the methodology
Consider why this proof is considered valid in kalam. How does the occurrence of a defect (آفة) in a specific part show that the faculty is there?
This is a form of argument from correlation: if A damages B, then B depends on A.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تقرر في علم التشريح، أنه ينبت من الدماغ أزواج سبعة من العصب. فالزوج الأول، مبدؤه من غور البطنين المقدمين من الدماغ، عند جوار الزائدتين الشبيهتين بحلمتي الثدي وهو صغير مجوف يتيامن النابت منهما يساراً، ويتياسر النابت منهما يميناً، ثم يلتقيان على تقاطع صليبين، ثم ينفذ النابت يميناً إلى الحدقة اليمنى، والنابت يساراً إلى الحدقة اليسرى. والدليل على كون القوى المدركة في المحال المذكورة، هو توجب الافة في تلك القوى .
It has been established in the science of anatomy that seven pairs of nerves grow from the brain. The first pair: its origin is from the depths of the two anterior ventricles of the brain, near the vicinity of the two appendages resembling the nipples of the breast, and it is small and hollow; the one growing from them inclines to the right towards the left, and the one growing from them inclines to the left towards the right, then they meet at a crossed intersection, then the one growing to the right penetrates to the right pupil, and the one growing to the left to the left pupil. And the proof that the perceptive faculties are in the mentioned locations is the occurrence of defect in those faculties.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage provides an anatomical description of the optic nerves and then offers a proof for the localization of perceptive faculties. The author states that anatomy teaches that seven pairs of nerves originate from the brain. He details the first pair: its origin from the anterior ventricles, its crossing (chiasm), and its path to the pupils. Then he gives a proof (dalīl) that the perceptive faculties (al-quwā al-mudrika) reside in the specific bodily parts mentioned (anterior ventricles, nerves, etc.): the fact that defects (āfa) in those parts cause defects in those faculties. This is a standard kalam argument: the effect is attributed to the locus where damage directly impairs it.

Key Terms
تشريح
عصب
دماغ
حدقة
آفة
قوى مدركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof relies solely on empirical dissection.
Correction: The proof is a rational argument from the correlation between defect and impairment, not merely observational.

Checkpoint

B) The occurrence of defect in those faculties when those parts are affected.
C) Philosophical deduction from the nature of the soul.
D) Revelation from scripture.

Key Takeaway
The occurrence of defects (āfa) in specific brain structures demonstrates that the perceptive faculties reside there.

Refuting the Necessity of Vision Conditions
Refutes the claim that vision is necessitated by its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that vision must occur when its conditions are met is refuted as a matter of customary knowledge, not logical necessity.

Core Idea
Vision's conditions are customary, not necessitating.

In this passage, the author examines the standard conditions for vision and the claim that they necessitate sight. He argues that while we observe vision absent when conditions are absent, this does not prove impossibility, and the inference that conditions necessitate vision is based on customary experience, not logical certainty. He also presents an alternative view that these conditions apply only to earthly vision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for vision
Read the passage and list the conditions mentioned: sound sense, intention of the seer, presence of the seen object, density, illumination, facing or its ruling, no veil, no excess in distance, size, or opacity.
These are the standard conditions discussed in kalam.
2
Understand the claim of necessary occurrence
The philosophers and Mu'tazila claim that when all conditions are met, vision must occur; otherwise, we might fail to see large objects like mountains. This is the argument they present.
3
Examine the refutation of the first proof
Their first proof: we observe that when a condition is absent, vision is absent. The author refutes: absence does not prove impossibility.
Customary knowledge is not logical certainty.
4
Examine the refutation of the second proof
Their second proof: if vision could fail with conditions, we might not see mountains, which is absurd. The author refutes: if by absurd they mean logical impossibility, we deny it; if they mean merely improbable, then the conclusion is not necessary—it's only customary.
5
Consider the alternative view
Some scholars say these conditions apply only when the soul is attached to the body; in the afterlife, vision may occur without them, like seeing a face in a mirror (which is not truly facing).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وقد يشترط في الإبصار) : بعد سلامة الحاسة، وقصد المبصر، وحضور المبصر، كونه كثيفاً مضيئاً مقابلاً، أو في حكمه من غير حجاب، ولا إفراط قرب أو بعد أو صغر أو بسبب غلظ ويدعى لزوم حصوله عند حصول الشرائط، وإلا لجاز أن يكون بحضرتنا جبال شاهقة . وردّ بأن نفي ذلك من العلوم العادية . زعمت الفلاسفة وتبعهم المعتزلة أن الإبصار يتوقف على شرائط يمتنع حصوله بدونها، ويجب حصوله معها . أما الأول : فلأنا نجد بالضرورة انتفاء الرؤية عند انتفاء شيء من تلك الشرائط. وردّ . بأن العدم لا يدل على الامتناع . وأما الثاني: فلأنه لو جاز عدم الإبصار معها لجاز أن يكون بحضرتنا جبال شاهقة، ورياض رائقة ، ونحن لانراها واللازم باطل قطعاً. ورد بأنه إن أريد باللازم إمكان ذلك في نفسه فلا نسلم بطلانه، وإن أريد الاحتمال، والتجويز العقلي بحيث لا يكون انتفاؤه معلوماً عند العقل على سبيل القطع ، فلا نسلم لزومه . فإن ذلك من العلوم العادية على ما سبق تحقيقه. ومنهم من قال : إن اشتراط هذه الشروط، إنما هو عند تعلق النفس بالبدن ، هذا التعلق المخصوص ، أو كون الباصرة على هذا القدر من القوة، لا على حد آخر فوقه كما في الاخرة ،قال أو في حكم المقابل، يعني كما في رؤية الوجه في المرآة.
He said: 'And it is conditioned in vision (al-ibṣār):' after soundness of the sense, intention of the seer, presence of the seen object, that it be dense, illuminated, facing, or in the ruling thereof, without a veil, and without excess of proximity or distance or smallness or due to opacity. And it is claimed that its occurrence is necessary when the conditions are met, otherwise it would be possible that there be towering mountains in our presence [and we not see them]. It was responded that negating that is among customary knowledge (al-ʿulūm al-ʿādiyya). The philosophers and the Mu'tazila following them claimed that vision depends on conditions without which it cannot occur, and with which it must occur. As for the first [proof]: we necessarily find that vision is absent when any of those conditions is absent. It was responded: absence does not indicate impossibility. As for the second: because if it were possible for vision to be absent with them, it would be possible that there be towering mountains, and lovely gardens, in our presence, and we do not see them, and the necessary conclusion is absolutely false. It was responded: if by 'necessary conclusion' is meant the possibility of that in itself, we do not accept its falsity; and if is meant the probability and rational permissibility such that its non-existence is not known to reason with certainty, then we do not accept its necessity. For that is among customary knowledge, as previously verified. And some of them said: the conditioning of these conditions is only when the soul is attached to the body, this particular attachment, or when the visual faculty is at this level of strength, not at another level above it as in the afterlife. He said: 'or in the ruling of the facing', meaning as in seeing the face in the mirror.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative refutation. It begins by quoting a statement listing the conditions for vision: sound sense, intention, presence of the object, density, illumination, facing or its ruling, no veil, no excess in distance, size, or opacity. It then presents the claim that these conditions necessitate sight; otherwise, we might not see mountains before us. The first response is that the absence of sight when conditions are absent is a matter of customary knowledge, not logical necessity. The philosophers and Mu'tazila argue that vision depends on conditions necessarily: first, we observe that when a condition is absent, sight is absent—they take this as proof of impossibility without them. The author retorts that absence does not prove impossibility. Second, they argue that if sight could be absent despite conditions, it would be possible for us to miss large things like mountains, which is absurd. The author responds that if by absurdity they mean logical impossibility, we reject that; if they mean it is merely not known with certainty, then the conclusion does not follow, because such knowledge is customary, not certain. Finally, an alternative view is mentioned: these conditions apply only to earthly vision with the soul attached to the body; in the afterlife, vision may occur without them, as illustrated by seeing the face in a mirror (which is not truly facing).

Key Terms
إبصار
شرائط
العلوم العادية
باصرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If all conditions for vision are present, sight must occur.
Correction: The author argues that this conclusion is based on customary knowledge, not logical necessity; it is possible that conditions are present without sight, as in the case of the afterlife.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the second proof of the philosophers that absence of sight with conditions would imply missing mountains?
A) By denying the possibility of mountains being present
B) By arguing that such knowledge is customary, not certain
C) By asserting that conditions are unnecessary
D) By claiming that mountains are always seen

Key Takeaway
The occurrence of vision when its conditions are met is a matter of customary experience, not logical necessity, and may be suspended in the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fulfill all the conditions for something you desire—like studying for success or praying for guidance—do you feel entitled to the outcome? How does the lesson's distinction between customary and necessary change your expectation of Allah's response?
?
Are there areas of your life where you have mistaken 'this is how it always happens' for 'this is how it must happen'? Will you trust that Allah can act beyond your experience, even withholding what seems guaranteed or granting what seems impossible?
?
The author says that absence of a condition does not prove impossibility. In your spiritual life, what 'absence' have you used to conclude that something is impossible with Allah? Will you repent of limiting His power based on your own custom?

Rational Hints for the Soul's Existence
Sanusi presents a rebuttal and three rational hints for the soul's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun used rational hints alongside scripture to prove the soul's existence, and how they countered objections based on lack of evidence for immateriality.

Core Idea
Rational hints reveal the soul even when the body hides it.

After critiquing earlier arguments, Sanusi presents the distinctive method of the mutakallimun. They did not rely solely on scripture but also used rational observations—tanbihāt ʿaqliyya—that point to an immaterial self independent of the changing body. This passage shows how everyday experiences like self-awareness and inner volition become theological evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the structure: an objection, a rebuttal, then positive proofs.
The passage uses footnote markers (1) and (2) for commentary but are not in the text itself.
2
Identify the objection and counter
Find where Sanusi states the objection: 'no evidence for immateriality, so it must be denied.' Then see his counter: 'also no evidence for spatiality, so that must be denied too.'
This shows the principle of parity in argumentation.
3
List the three rational hints
The passage gives three tanbihāt ʿaqliyya. Identify each one and note how they point to a soul distinct from the body.
The hints are: (1) body changes, soul remains; (2) self-awareness independent of body; (3) willing what body resists.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider why the mutakallimun used both scripture and rational hints. How does this approach differ from relying only on scripture or only on philosophy?
This connects to the broader kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاستدلال، بأنه لا دليل على تجردها، فيجب نفيه فمع ضعفه ،معارض بأنه لا دليل على تحيزها فيجب نفيه . يعني لما لم يثبت عند المتكلمين اختلاف أنواع الأجسام، واستناد الاثار إليها، ليحتاج إلى فصول منوعة ، ومبادىء مختلفة، بنوا إثبات النفس على الأدلة السمعية، والتنبيهات العقلية، مثل إن البدن وأعضاءه الظاهرة والباطنة دائماً في التبدل والتحلل (۱) ، والنفس بحالها، وأن الإنسان الصحيح العقل، قد يغفل عن البدن وأجزائه، ولا يغفل بحال عن وجود وجدانه (۲) ذاته، وأنه قد يريد ما يمانعه البدن مثل الحركة إلى العلو وبالجملة. قد اختلفت كلمة الفريقين في حقيقة النفس
As for the inference that there is no evidence for its immateriality, so its negation is necessary, it is weak, and countered by the fact that there is no evidence for its spatial occupation, so its negation is necessary. Meaning, since the mutakallimun did not establish the diversity of bodies and the attribution of effects to them, so as to need varied divisions and different principles, they built the proof of the soul on scriptural evidences and rational hints, such as that the body and its outer and inner limbs are constantly in change and dissolution (1), while the soul remains as it is, and that a person of sound intellect may be heedless of the body and its parts, but is never heedless of the existence and finding (2) of his own self, and that he may will what the body resists such as upward movement. In summary, the two groups have disagreed concerning the reality of the soul.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage first addresses a possible objection: someone might argue that there is no evidence for the soul's immateriality, so it must be denied. The author responds that this is weak because it is countered by the fact that there is also no evidence for its spatial occupation (tahayyuz), so that would also require denial. He then explains the mutakallimun's positive method: since they did not establish a diversity of bodies sufficient to explain different effects, they based proof of the soul on scriptural evidence (adilla samʿiyya) and rational hints (tanbihāt ʿaqliyya). Three examples of rational hints are given: first, the body constantly changes while the soul remains the same; second, a person can be unaware of his body but never unaware of his own self; third, a person may will things the body resists (like upward movement). The passage concludes that the two groups (theologians and philosophers) have disagreed on the reality of the soul.

Key Terms
أدلة سمعية (adilla samʿiyya)
تنبيهات عقلية (tanbīhāt ʿaqliyya)
تحيز (tahayyuz)
تجرد (tajarud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun only used scripture to prove the soul's existence.
Correction: The passage shows they also used rational hints (tanbihāt ʿaqliyya) from everyday experience.

Checkpoint
What is one of the rational hints (tanbihāt ʿaqliyya) mentioned for the existence of the soul?
A) The body stays the same while the soul changes.
B) A person can be heedless of his own self.
C) A person may will what the body resists.
D) The soul is made of fire.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun proved the soul's existence using both scripture and rational hints from self-awareness, bodily change, and volition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are completely absorbed in thought, have you ever momentarily lost awareness of your body? Did that make you doubt your own existence? How does this experience, as mentioned by Sanusi, strengthen your certainty in the soul's reality?
?
The third hint says you can will what your body resists, like upward movement. Think of a time you resisted a physical urge for a higher purpose. What does that struggle reveal about the soul's authority over the body? Are you using that authority to pursue what pleases Allah?
?
The passage treats self-awareness as a rational proof. If you truly believed that your soul is constant and will return to its Creator, how would your daily priorities change? What one attachment to this world are you ready to release because your soul is not of it?

Definitions of Theoretical and Practical Wisdom
Sanusi defines theoretical and practical wisdom and gives a common definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of theoretical and practical wisdom and how wisdom unites knowledge and action.

Core Idea
Wisdom is both knowledge and action, actualizing the soul's potential.

The author begins by establishing what wisdom means, distinguishing between theoretical and practical aspects. This passage lays the conceptual foundation for all subsequent discussions on the classification of sciences and virtues.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Identify the definitions of theoretical wisdom (knowledge of things as they are) and practical wisdom (doing things as they ought). Notice the parallel phrasing.
2
Understand the combined definition
Read the sentence that defines wisdom as the soul's transition from potentiality to actuality. Reflect on how this combines theory and practice.
This is a key philosophical definition.
3
Reflect on the scope
Consider why wisdom must include both knowledge and action. The author says wisdom is a name for both 'ilm and 'amal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ويتفرع على النظري) : الحكمة النظرية المفسرة بمعرفة الأشياء كما هي بقدر الطاقة البشرية، وعلى العملي. الحكمة العملية المفسرة بالقيام بالأمور على ما ينبغي بقدرها . فمن ههنا يقال : إن الحكمة هي خروج النفس من القوة الى الفعل في كمالها الممكن، وأن اسم للعلم والعمل جميعاً وقد يقال : الحكمة العملية لمعرفة الأمور المتعلقة باختيارنا وتخص النظرية بما ليس كذلك فإن تعلقت بما يستغنى عن المادة ذهنياً، وخارجاً فما بعد الطبيعة. أو ذهناً فقط. فالرياضي، أو يحتاج فيهما، فالطبيعي والعملية إن تعلقت بإصلاح الشخص فتهذيب الأخلاق أو المشاركين في المنزل، فتدبير المنزل، أو المدنية، فسياسة المدن الفاضلة، اعتدال القوة الشهوية وهي العفة والغضبية وهي الشجاعة والنطقية، وهي الحكمة ومجموعها العدالة ولكل طرف إفراط وتفريط هما رذيلة، فللعفة الخمود والفجور، وللشجاعة التهور والجبن وللحكمة الجريزة والغباوة .
He said: And branches off from the theoretical: Theoretical wisdom explained as knowledge of things as they are to the extent of human capacity, and from the practical: Practical wisdom explained as carrying out affairs as they ought to the extent of [human] capacity. Hence it is said: Wisdom is the soul's transition from potentiality to actuality in its possible perfection, and that it is a name for knowledge and action together. And it may be said: Practical wisdom is for knowledge of matters related to our choice, and theoretical is specified for what is not like that. For if it relates to what is independent of matter in intellect and externally, then it is metaphysics; or only in intellect, then mathematics; or needs matter in both, then natural science. And practical wisdom, if it relates to the reform of the individual, then ethics; or to participants in the household, then household management; or to the city-state, then politics of ideal cities. The moderation of the appetitive power is chastity, of the irascible power is courage, of the rational power is wisdom, and their aggregate is justice. And for each side there is excess and deficiency which are vices: for chastity, [excess] is frigidity and [deficiency] is licentiousness; for courage, [excess] is recklessness and [deficiency] is cowardice; for wisdom, [excess] is cunning and [deficiency] is dullness.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies wisdom. The author states that theoretical wisdom is knowledge of things as they are, while practical wisdom is performing affairs as they ought. He then gives a definition that spans both: wisdom is the soul's actualization of its possible perfection in knowledge and action. The passage then subdivides theoretical wisdom based on its relation to matter: metaphysics is independent of matter both in thought and externally, mathematics depends on matter only in thought, and natural science depends on matter in both. Practical wisdom is divided into three: ethics (reform of the individual), household management (reform of the household), and politics (reform of the city). The author then explains the four cardinal virtues: chastity (moderation of the appetitive faculty), courage (moderation of the irascible), wisdom (moderation of the rational), and justice (the aggregate of these). Each virtue has two extremes that are vices: chastity's extremes are frigidity (excess) and licentiousness (deficiency); courage's extremes are recklessness (excess) and cowardice (deficiency); wisdom's extremes are cunning (excess) and dullness (deficiency).

Key Terms
الحكمة النظرية
الحكمة العملية
القوة
الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is practical wisdom defined?
A) Knowledge of things as they are
B) Carrying out affairs as they ought
C) Transition from potentiality to actuality
D) Moderation of the rational power

Key Takeaway
Wisdom unites knowledge and action, as the soul moves from potential to actual perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that wisdom is both knowledge and action. In which specific area of your life are you content with just knowing without doing? What would it cost you to delay acting any longer?
?
The lesson says wisdom is the soul's transition from potential to actual perfection. Are you actively moving toward that perfection, or are you stagnant? Identify one concrete step you will take today to actualize a potential you have been neglecting.
?
Think of a situation where you know what is right but fail to do it. How does that contradict the definition of wisdom in this lesson? What will you change starting now?

Subdivisions of Sciences: Theoretical and Practical
Sanusi subdivides theoretical and practical wisdom into three disciplines each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how theoretical and practical sciences are subdivided based on their relation to matter and context.

Core Idea
Sciences branch according to their dependence on matter and their scope of application.

Having defined wisdom as both theoretical and practical, the author now classifies the sciences that fall under each branch. He divides theoretical sciences into three based on their relation to matter, and practical sciences into three based on the object of reform.

Guided Path
1
Examine the theoretical sciences
Look for the three types: metaphysics (independent of matter), mathematics (dependent intellectually but not externally), and natural science (dependent in both). Notice the criteria: independence from matter.
Metaphysics is 'what comes after nature'.
2
Examine the practical sciences
Find the three types: ethics (reform of self), household management (reform of household), and politics (reform of the city).
3
Understand the basis of division
Reflect on why theoretical sciences are divided by their relation to matter and practical sciences by the object of reform. This logical division is foundational for Islamic classification of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Cardinal Virtues and Their Extremes
Sanusi enumerates the four cardinal virtues and their corresponding vices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four cardinal virtues (chastity, courage, wisdom, justice) and their corresponding vices of excess and deficiency.

Core Idea
Each virtue is a mean between two extremes that are vices.

The passage concludes by discussing the four cardinal virtues that arise from moderating the soul's faculties. For each faculty (appetitive, irascible, rational), the mean is a virtue and the extremes are vices. Justice is the aggregate of all virtues.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three faculties and their virtues
Locate the three powers: appetitive (chastity), irascible (courage), rational (wisdom). Understand that each virtue is the mean.
2
Identify the extremes for each virtue
For each virtue, find the two vices: excess (e.g., frigidity for chastity) and deficiency (e.g., licentiousness).
Memorize the pairs.
3
Understand justice as aggregate
Notice that justice is the sum of all three virtues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Primary Divisions of Theoretical and Practical Wisdom
Sanusi divides wisdom into theoretical and practical, then subdivides each into three sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how wisdom is divided into theoretical and practical based on its subject matter and goal, and what the primary subdivisions of each are.

Core Idea
Wisdom is either about truth or about action, each with three branches.

This passage establishes the foundational classification of the sciences according to the goal of knowledge. It distinguishes between theoretical wisdom, which seeks truth, and practical wisdom, which seeks good action. The author then lists the primary subdivisions of each, starting with ethics for practical wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Notice the clear logical structure: definition, criterion, division, subdivisions.
The passage is concise; each sentence carries a key part of the classification.
2
Identify the criterion for division
Find the condition that makes wisdom either theoretical or practical. The text states that if matters are within our power and choice, the wisdom is practical; otherwise it is theoretical.
This criterion is central to Islamic classification of sciences.
3
Note the three theoretical branches
Theoretical wisdom is divided into divine (metaphysics), mathematical, and natural sciences. These cover the main domains of theoretical inquiry.
These correspond to what are often called the three theoretical philosophies.
4
Examine practical wisdom's subdivisions
Practical wisdom is primarily divided into the science of ethics (as explicitly mentioned), along with two others implied by the classification but not detailed here.
The full set includes household management and politics, but only ethics is given in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قسم المفسرة بمعرفة الأشياء كما هي إلى النظرية والعملية، لأنها إن كانت علماً بالأمور المتعلقة لقدرتنا واختيارنا فعملية، وغايتها العمل وتحصيل الخير، وإلا فنظرية وغايتها إدراك الحق، وكل منهما ينقسم بالقسمة الأولية إلى ثلاثة أقسام. فالنظرية إلى الإلهي والرياضي والطبيعي والعملية إلى علم الأخلاق
And the [wisdom] defined as knowledge of things as they are has been divided into theoretical and practical, because if it is knowledge of matters related to our power and choice, then it is practical, and its goal is action and attaining good; otherwise it is theoretical, and its goal is perception of truth. And each of them is divided by a primary division into three parts: theoretical [wisdom divides] into divine, mathematical, and natural; and practical [wisdom divides] into the science of ethics...
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage provides a clear classification: wisdom is either theoretical or practical. The criterion is whether the matters are within our power and choice (practical, aiming at good action) or not (theoretical, aiming at truth). Each division then has three primary sub-divisions: theoretical wisdom into divine science (metaphysics), mathematics, and natural science; practical wisdom into ethics (science of character), presumably household management and politics, though here only ethics is mentioned explicitly. The passage is a concise summary of the division of sciences.

Key Terms
النظرية
العملية
الإلهي
الرياضي
الطبيعي
علم الأخلاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the divisions listed (especially practical wisdom) are exhaustive as stated.
Correction: The passage explicitly says 'primary division' and only mentions ethics for practical wisdom; the classical complete set includes ethics, household management, and politics.

Checkpoint
What is the criterion for dividing wisdom into theoretical and practical?
A) Whether it deals with matter
B) Whether it depends on sense perception
C) Whether it concerns matters within our power and choice
D) Whether it is certain

Key Takeaway
The first division of wisdom is into theoretical (seeking truth) and practical (seeking good), each further divided into three core sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you acquire knowledge, do you prioritize theoretical truth or practical good? Which one truly drives your studies? This lesson reveals both are essential. How can you restore balance if you lean too heavily on one?
?
The passage says practical wisdom aims at 'attaining good'. Examine your daily actions: are they genuinely directed toward good, or are they tainted by self-interest? What concrete changes can you make to align your practical knowledge with pure goodness?
?
Theoretical wisdom seeks truth for its own sake. Is there a truth you have been avoiding because it is uncomfortable? This lesson calls you to pursue truth even when it demands change. What step will you take today to face that truth?

intermediate

The Threefold Division of Theoretical Sciences
Classification of theoretical sciences by relation to matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of theoretical sciences according to their relation to matter, and how each science is defined by whether it conceives and/or subsists with matter.

Core Idea
Theoretical sciences are distinguished by how their objects depend on matter for conception and subsistence.

This lesson unpacks the first major classification in the passage: how theoretical science is divided into natural, mathematical, and divine based on the relationship of their objects to matter. The author provides clear criteria that will help you see why these categories are fundamental in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: a conditional 'if...then' pattern for each science.
The passage begins by completing the list of practical sciences before defining theoretical.
2
Identify the three criteria
The division hinges on two features: conception (tasawwur) and subsistence (qiwam). Understand what each means in this context.
Conception refers to mental representation; subsistence refers to existence in the external world.
3
Match each science to its criterion
Natural science: both conception and subsistence require matter. Mathematical: subsistence requires matter, but conception does not. Divine: neither requires matter.
4
Review examples for each
The author gives examples: lines and surfaces for mathematical; the Necessary and immaterial beings for divine. Reflect on why these fit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلم تدبير المنزل، وعلم سياسة المدينة. لأن النظرية إن كانت علماً بأحوال الموجودات من حيث يتعلق بالمادة تصوراً وقواماً، فهي العلم الطبيعي، وإن كانت من حيث يتعلق بها قواماً لا تصوراً. فالرياضي . كالبحث عن الخطوط، والسطوح، وغيرهما، مما يفتقر إلى المادة في الوجود، لا في التصور، وإن كانت من حيث لا يتعلق بها لا قواماً ولا تصوراً فالإلهي، ويسمى العلم الأعلى، وعلم ما بعد الطبيعة، كالبحث عن الواجب والمجردات وما يتعلق بذلك . واعترض صاحب المطارحات بأن في الإلهي ما يتعلق بالمادة في الجملة، كالوحدة والكثرة ،والعلية والمعلولية، وكثير من الأمور العامة، وفي الرياضي ما قد يستغنى عنها ، كالعدد وهو مدفوع بقيد الحيثية . فإن العدد إذا اعتبر من حيث كان مستغنياً عن المادة، ويبحث عنه في الإلهي . وإذا اعتبر من حيث هو في الأوهام، أو في الموجودات المادية متفرقة و مجتمعة، فيبحث عن الجمع والتفريق والضرب والقسمة، فهو علم العدد المعدود من أقسام الرياضي، وإلى هذا أشار في الشفاء. إلا أنه قد يناقس في اختصاص حيثية الجمع والتفريق، والضرب والقسمة . وبالجملة المباحث الحسابية لغير المجردات، والحكمة العملية، إن تعلقت بآراء ينتظم بها حال الشخص، وزكاء نفسه. فالحكمة الخلقية، وإلا فإن تعلقت بانتظام المشاركة الإنسانية الخاصة، فالحكمة المنزلية والعامة، فالحكمة المدنية والسياسية .
And the science of household management, and the science of city politics. Because theoretical [science] if it is knowledge of the states of existents insofar as they are related to matter in conception and subsistence, then it is natural science; and if [it is] insofar as they are related to it in subsistence not conception, then mathematical, like investigation of lines, surfaces, and other things that require matter in existence, not in conception; and if it is insofar as not related to it neither in subsistence nor in conception, then divine, and it is called the highest science and metaphysics, like investigation of the Necessary and the immaterial and what pertains to that. And the author of al-Mutarahat objected that in the divine there is what is related to matter in general, like unity and multiplicity, causality and effect, and many universal matters, and in the mathematical there is what may dispense with it, like number. And it is repelled by the restriction of aspect (haysiyya). For if number is considered insofar as it is independent of matter, it is investigated in the divine; and if it is considered insofar as it is in the imaginations or in material existents, separated and combined, then investigation of addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division is carried out, so it is the science of number counted among the divisions of mathematics. And to this al-Shifa referred. Except that there may be discussion about the specification of the aspect of addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division. In summary, the mathematical investigations are for non-immaterial things. And practical wisdom: if it is concerned with opinions by which the state of the person and the purity of his soul are regulated, then it is ethical wisdom; otherwise, if it is concerned with the regulation of special human partnership, then it is household wisdom; and [if with] general [partnership], then it is civil and political wisdom.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage classifies the theoretical and practical sciences. It begins by completing the list of practical sciences: household management and city politics. Then it explains the division of theoretical science based on its relation to matter: if the science considers existents in terms of both conception and subsistence requiring matter, it is natural science; if it requires matter only for subsistence but not for conception (like mathematical objects which can be conceived without matter but exist in matter), it is mathematical; if it requires matter neither for conception nor subsistence, it is divine science, also called metaphysics, studying the Necessary Being and immaterial beings. An objection from the author of al-Mutarahat is then presented: that divine science also deals with matters related to matter, such as unity, multiplicity, causality, etc., and on the other hand, mathematics has objects like number that can be independent of matter. The objection is refuted using the restriction of aspect (haysiyya): number considered as independent of matter belongs to divine science; considered as applied to material things (addition, subtraction, etc.) belongs to mathematics. The passage notes that al-Shifa (Avicenna's work) alludes to this, though there may be discussion about the specification of these aspects. Finally, practical wisdom is subdivided into ethics (regulating the individual's soul), household management (regulating the family), and politics (regulating the city or state).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which science studies objects that exist in matter but can be conceived without it?
A) Natural science
B) Mathematical science
C) Divine science
D) Practical science

Key Takeaway
Theoretical sciences are distinguished by whether their objects depend on matter for conception and subsistence, yielding natural, mathematical, and divine sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that theoretical sciences are ranked by their independence from matter. If you spend most of your study time on natural or mathematical sciences, what does that reveal about your priorities? Are you neglecting the divine science that deals with what is eternal and immaterial? How will you adjust your intellectual life to give metaphysics its due place?
?
The passage defines metaphysics as the study of the Necessary Being and immaterial entities. When was the last time you genuinely contemplated the existence of the immaterial – your own soul, or the Creator? If the answer is 'rarely,' what practical step will you take this week to turn your mind toward the highest science?
?
This classification implies that some knowledge is more noble than others. Does your daily schedule reflect a pursuit of higher knowledge, or are you consumed with what is material and passing? What specific change can you make tomorrow to prioritize the study of divine truths?
